THE RED PAGE.
In his recently-issued ‘ Children’s Ways ’ Prof.
Sully reproduces from 1895’s ‘Studies of Child-
hood ’ many authentic scrawls by juvenile artists.
Unillumined parents must regard these with
startled ouriosity. To see elevated to scientific
rank the random caricatures traced deviously on
the slate of ante-bedtime five-year-olds is !
And one cannot say that Prof. Sully exactly justi-
fies his ponderous discussion of the small peoples’
mimicries. The in-aeternum inferences from
four-year-Tommy’s ‘ A HORS ’ or seven-year-
Cissy’s ‘ A MAN ’ remain, after much argument,
invisible. Even the temporary interest is strictly
placid and parental. And none of Prof. Sully’s
examples of child-art is as attractive as those
shown on this page (taken from ‘The Pall Mall
Budget ’of aforetime). Mark the fine sentiment
preserved in the figure of the murderer ! the noble
recklessness of that ‘ pier on the Thames ’! One
feels sure the pier-artist would grow up to
denounce criticism and point out that nobody but
a nice little pig with a curly tail can possibly be
qualified to pass judgment upon nice little pigs
with curly tails.
* * * *
From an impersonal standpoint, children’s art
1b less interesting than savage or aboriginal art,
because it lacks that aesthetic quality of which
adult-age generally shows glimmerings. The
child’s stimulus is not artistic, but imitative—
probably in essence creative ; based on that desire
to make something, that pleasure in the Ego’s
handiwork, which branches in so many directions
from the root of race-preservation. Prof. Sully
takes his mimes a trifle too seriously. Over-
seriousness, indeed, is the fault of his book, as
himself dimly realises. His heavy style clogs the
nimble heels of his subject. With twice the
energy he could have told four times as much in
half the space. Books should be like rivers, with
a free movement to an ultimate sea. Or, if they
he landlocked lakes, replacing with the charm of
rest the strength of motion, the eye should range
infinitely through clear depths. And Prof.
book is like his mind —muddy—with synthesis
swallowed in the cloud of details.
» * * *
Nonetheless, he adds together many attractive
items and illustrations —as, for example, indeal-
ing with the childish imagination. The childish
imagination is a development of the creative
instinct previously referred to : the same force
impels to the building of myths and brick houses :
in each case the essence of the pleasure is a con-
structive emission from the Ego. And as this
creative power lessens, so lessens the individual
or racial hold on life.
* • • •
A child’s capacity for vitalising inanimate ob-
jects springs from sis own superabundant vitality.
Se can not only live simself, but can dower trees
and stones with life by a projection of the Ego—
just as the childlike races did in olden time, as we
know by their myths ; just as childlike savages do
to-day. Jean Ingelow relates how, when about
three years old—
I had the habit of attributing intelligence not only to
all living creatures, the same amount and kind of intelli-
gence that I had myself, but even to stones and manu-
factured articles. I used to feel how dull it must be
for the pebbles in the causeway to be obliged to lie still
and only see what was round about. When I walked out
with a little basket for putting flowers in I used some-
by s[?] Edwen Landseer
aetat 19
times to pick up a pebble or two and carry them on to
have a change: then, at the farthest point of the walk,
turn them out, not doubting they would be pleased to
have a new view.
In other words, Jean Ingelow read her Ego into
the Universe; she re-ereated the Universe in
terms of herself; and that is what every individual
and every race does in greater or less degree, in
proportion as physical organisation supplies the
need and the power.
* * * *
So the reality of a doll to a little girl, or
of a toy-horse to a little boy depending
partly upon the individual brain and its stage
of development—depends partly, perhaps chiefly,
upon the brain’s vital capacity to raise
‘the imagination’ to the required pitch. And
A pier on the Thames.— Sketched by an im-
pressionist artist, aged 2.
a child can see men and women where an
adult sees chips and shells, partly because the
child’s vitality is stronger than the adult’s. A
ohild of very intense vitality does not even need
the symbol. The little girl in William Canton’s
book cuddled in her arms a space of empty air
where she saw the ‘invisible playmate’ lie.
• * * *
Immaturity of the brain may to some extent
account for such feats of the childish imagination.
Before the higher cells have attained full develop-
ment and control a child’s brain is apt to confuse
internal with external perceptions. The child
who, frightened by the story of a bogey-man, fears
to go to bed in the dark, does actually see the
bogey-man in the dark. He is not there to adult
eyes; but he is there to the child’s brain, and
quite conceivably to the child’s eyes—since a
child’s visual centres, normally ill-regulated, may
paint an apparition on the retina just as the ab-
normally ill-regulated centres of an adult can.
When William Canton’s little girl died, her
father, bending over her, saw for a moment her
‘invisible playmate’ in the flesh, just as she saw
it always. He thought the sight ‘ incredible ’—it
was cerebrally quite simple.
• * » *
It follows from what has been said that the need
which many people experience for ‘ perfect
scenery ’ in dramatic representation is partly »
sign of lowered vitality, or possibly of a brain so
busy that it has no surplus vitality to
spare for visualising unseen objects. Yet it is
not so long since, for example, the change of
scene from a forest to a streeb was depicted on
the stage by a mere change of placard from A
Forest to A Street. Shakespeare’s plays were
originally represented on a floor strewn with
rushes, the side walls hung with arras; a board
with ‘Westminster,’ ‘Corinth,’ ‘ Messina,’ fixed
the scene of the play ; the audience’s imagination
did the rest. When a battle was to be fought,
‘ two armies fly in represented by swords and
bucklers, and then ’—asks Sidney in his ‘ Defence
of Poesie’— ‘ what hard heart will not receive it
for a pitched field ? ’ We are more exacting nowa-
days—partly because brain-life has become so com-
plex that we cannot imagine a forest unless we see
scenery simulating a forest, or an army unless we
see at least several dozen supers ; and partly, also,
because- the modern playgoer is not as.strong a
man, not as vital a man, as his forefathers,
ignorant of tea and coffee and tobacco, and of all
the multifarious strains and stresses of an age
which the other day impelled one of its victims to
the pathetic complaint, ‘ It’s so horrid to be
hurried ! ’
• • • *
And note that the scenery-exacting person is
the complex-brained person who goes to see
Irving, not the simpler-brained man who prefers
Chevalier. The audience which enjoys Shake-
speare may be said, generally speaking, to be a
more ‘eduoated’ audience than that which
attends the music-halls ; and it demands more in
the way of scenery to make the drama real to its
brain. ‘The Merchant of Venice’ must be
mounted with historic exactitude. But music-hall
performers do turn after different turn before the
same * interior ; ’ and the spectators never dream of
asking that a coster song shall be sung in a coster
environment, or that an imitation draper’s-shop
shall be fitted up before Dan Leno sings ‘The
Shopwalker.’ The child dispenses with costume
and scenery ; the music-hall audience is content
with costume; the theatrical audience demands
both costume and scenery. And there is a corre-
sponding retrogression in vitality of body and pro-
gression in complexity of brain.
» « » »
[lt would be interesting to argue from this the
place of the Chinese in the vital scale of races. In
a Chinese theatre there is no scenery, no necessity
for scenery, since the vivid Chinese imagination
takes as much as you please for granted. An
actor comes before the audience and says he is a
king, walking in the gardens of his palace. That
is enough for the audience : there is the king,
there are’the gardens, there is com-
plete in every detail. The European eye sees a
curiously-bedizened personage fall flop on tile
boards, get up again, and walk off o.p. ; the
Chinese eye sees a beautiful damsel, crossed in
love, despairing of life, drown herself in the canal
which runs through her father’s estate, and pic-
tures the finding of the body, the solemn funeral,
the grief of the parents, the remorse of the res-
ponsible male, and fifty consequences more or less
remote. —Deductions are left to readers : The
Bulletin doesn’t purpose telling all it knows for
sixpence—or even all it doesn t know.]
* * * *
It is a good observation of Prof. Sully’s that a
child so easily sees quaint likenesses between
objects because sis mind is untroubled by all the
complexity of the objects. A child’s fresh brains
base simple analogies on leading traits the only
traits they recognise; while adults, hoarding a
multitude of impressions, lose vivacity, and lose
also power of detaching single impressions. Thus
the idea of parental relationship, dominant in W.
Canton’s ‘W.V.,’ sent her mind leaping to the
pretty phrase, ‘Aren’t the buds the trees little
girls?’—a phrase poetical, almost recondite, _ to
adult-age, but almost matter-of-fact to a child.
The explanation of nearly all the quaint sayings
of children lies in this reference from their limited
known to the unknown : we smile to see Infinity s
pyramid toppling from such an impossible apex-bal-
ance. Thus one of the first notions of a child, as of
a savage, is that motion implies life.. Ihe little
girl who offered a biscuit to a tram is paralleled
by the Red Indians who brought corn to the first
prairie locomotive. And doubtless the awe with
which Fulton’s steamboat was regarded is kin
to a dog’s fear of a bone moved by a string. The
immature or incompetent intelligence is always
generalising on insufficient data : from one
analogy it argues others, often proceeding
to complete resemblance. Just why the mind
should be so eager to associate unrelated or
I correlated ideas is inexplicable : there is
no physical or psychical explanation of the
reason of the pleasure we take in discover-
ing likenesses or differences in concepts or per-
cepts ; but the pleasure itself is one of the most
notable facts of mental life, especially in children.
Probably this joy in classification accounts for
much of the charm in poetical allusions to stars as
‘ flowers of the sky,’ or to flowers as ‘ stars of the
earth,’or to butterflies as ‘pansies flying’—the
last a childish analogy.
» * * •
A child’s ‘ pitiless logic ’ arises partly from sis
power of subjective concentration. Se not only
Steamer. —Sketched by an impressionist artist,
aged 3½.
sees one aspect of a thing to the possible exclusion
of all other aspects; but temporarily devotes the
whole mental force to the act of seeing. It is
this singleness of view which accounts for much
of the * unfeelingness ’ of childhood. The child’s
mind often cannot hold , more than one
emotion at a time, and the propositions
crowd out the corollaries. Thus the boy who
shouted down the shaft where his brother had
fallen, ‘Say, Tommy, if you don’t ever come up
alive oan I have your pup ? ’ was not necessarily
callous or unfraternal. He was simply dominated
by the pup-idea to the exclusion of the brother-
idea. Quite conceivably, in another mood, the
same boy would have given all his belongings or
his life to get his brother out. There are oases
where the egoistic sentiment is obviously charac-
teristic, yet it does not follow that blame can be
even hypothetically attached. Utter unselfish-
ness can no more be expected from the childish
brain than 100yds. in lOsecs. can be expected
from childish legs. Prof. Sully quotes a boy of
three who was told by his mother to stay and
mind a baby-sister while she went downstairs.
On going up again some time after she met him
on the stairs. ‘ Being asked why he had left the
baby, he said there was a bee in the room and he
was afraid he would get stung if he stayed, there.
His mother asked him if he wasn’t afraid his little
sister would get stung. He said “ Yes,’’but added
that if he stayed in the room the bee might sting
them both, and then she would have two to take
care of.’ No charge of want of sympathy lies
against this plausible little self-seeker. He may
fairly plead mental minority. The child is to be
tested by sis own standards, not by adults’,
• » * *
Many such arguments are suggested by Prof.
Sully’s little book, which most people will find
more valuable, because more lucid and less,
baggage-encumbered, than his big book. Possibly,
however, the attempt at a scientific explanation
of children’s ways is itself essentially unscientific.
It sums up with the statement, ‘ The child is
young’—and understanding of that all-compre-
hending youth comes better from examples than
from precepts, from illustrations than from argu-
ments, Prof. Sully’s long chapter on ‘ The Age of
Imagination ’ adds little to Kenneth Grahame’s
picture of Harold the muffin-man, ‘ who day and
night went through passages and up and down
staircases, ringing a noiseless bell and offering
phantom muffins to invisible wayfarers. It sounds
A Murderer.—Copied from an illustrated
paper by a child artist, aged 7½ .
a poor sort of sport; and yet—to pass along busy
streets of your own building, for ever ringing an
imaginary bell and offering airy muffins of your
own make to a bustling, thronging crowd of your
own creation ! ’ Or how many books of commen-
tary on boys’ ways is Hack Finn worth ? It’s the
old controversy—should one see flowers as a
botanist or as a poet ? But are botanist and poet
mutually exclusive ? Can one not be a little of
both?
THE REASON WHY.
“ When I was at the party,"
Said Betty (aged just four),
A little girl fell off her chair,
Eight down upon the floor;
And ail the other little girls
Began to laugh, but me—
l didn’t laugh a single bit,"
Said Betty seriously.
“ Why not? ” her mother asked her.
Full of delight to find
That Betty—bless her little heart 1—
Had been so sweetly kind.
“ Why didn’t you laugh, darling?
Or don’t you like to tell ? ”
“I didn’t laugh,” said Betty,
“ ’Cause it was me that fell 1”
Mary E. Bradley.
BEDTIME.
’Tis bedtime ; say your hymn, and bid “Good-night"}
God bless mamma, papa, and dear ones all;
Your half-shut eyes beneath your eyelids fall,
Another minute you will shut them quite.
Yes, I will carry you, put out the light.
And tuck you up, although you are so talk
What will you give me, sleepy one, and cal’
My wages, if I settle you all right?
I laid her golden curls upon my arm,
I drew her little feet within my hand ;
Her rosy palms were join’d in trustful bliss,
Her heart, next min\ beat gently, soft and warm;
She nestl’d to ine, and by love’s command
Paid me my precious wages—" haby’s ki9S.”
Lord Rosslyn.

SURGEON LANGSTON, M.R.C.S., Eng.,
the greatest and most successful practi-
tioner IN THE WORLD.
But few of noted specialists have sprung into pre-emin-
ence and an immense practice as Surgeon Langston, a
resident and practitioner at 121 Collins-street, Melbourne.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
PAPUA DIABOLI.
’Twas late at night, in a dismal camp ; and now
and then in the breeze
A trembling shower of raindrops fell like tears
from the giant trees ;
Above me towered the scrub-clad range, below
was the river-bed;
And I sat and shivered with fever, chills, and
thought of the lonely dead.
A bird flew past like an evil dream, but what
could it bode to me ?
I saw the ghosts of the men who tramped to the
hills, away from the sea.
The distant sound of a native drum rang warn-
ingly through the air,
I heeded not, for I only heard the groans of new
despair.
For one was killed by a poisoned spear, ond one
by a falling tree,
And one was caught in an ebbing tide and drifted
out to the sea;
And one was bushed in the mangrove swamp and
choked in pestilent breath,
And one, poor fellow, was trapped, betrayed, and
cruelly done to death.
Ah, whence we came and whither we went, fierce,
sudden danger was there ;
But most I shudder to think of those who drooped
and died of despair;
Who stumbled onward from day to day, with
ever-lightening load,
But hearts too heavy to carry on, too sick to live
on the road.
A road ? My God ! through an endless scrub, en-
closed in silence and gloom,
We climbed and panted and cursed and prayed
and blundered —into a tomb.
And, cold and hungry and fever-racked, we
camped at night in the rain,
And rose and clambered through moss and mist,
again and again and again.
And so we wandered through days of dread, and
never found that we sought
Till one and the other lay down to die, and we
who buried them thought
With helpless wrath of the day we first set eyes
on that beautiful land,
And loved the light of the morning sun as it shone
on the palm-fringed sand.
The legend tells of a woman fiend in the charm of
her youth so fair
That men would laugh at the warning cry, and
rush and die in her lair.
It seems to me I have seen the face and felt the
kiss of the ghoul
Whom God has clothed in an angel shape and
given a vampire soul.
N.Q.
VON KOTZE.
THE SPLITTER’S SONG.
The long day’s nearly over, and the cool, calm
night’s at hand,
It’s time for givin’ over work, I know ;
Peace is creepin’ down the valley, silence settles
on the land,
The shadows they are length’nin’ down below.
Par away the sun is shinin’ on the eastern head-
land still,
On rugged rocks and sheets of glitterin’ foam ;
Here the wekas ’gin to answer one another from
the hill,
And the wood-splitters are making tracks for
home.
For day is done, and night’s begun,
And dreamless sleep’s a cornin’ !
As the last man stumbles wharewards the more-
porks harshly hoot,
And sleepy mokis rustle in the bush ;
From the tangled mass of lawyer, and supple-jack
and tute,
He hears below the creek’s half-muffled rush;
Now stars begin to twinkle in the clear sky one
by one;
All’s silent save for bleat of mother-sheep
And cry of young lambs answerin’ —with settin’
of the sun
The valley seems to sink in quiet sleep.
And day is done, and night’s begun,
And dreamless sleep’s a-comin’!
There streams a flood of firelight from the open
whare doors ;
The cook’s had supper ready long ago.
Fling the logs upon the hearth till the wooden
chimney roars—
Black pine, e gai-sticks, and matipo !
Supper over, smoke begins; then a yarn, a song
maybe,
The splitter tumbles early into bunk—
Up before the break of day, little time for dreams
has he;
Soon in deepest depths of slumber he is sunk.
For song is sung, and day is done,
And dreamless sleep’s a-comin’!
O my brothers, O my sisters, by the bitter bond of
graft,
By sweat of brow, by salt of scalding tears,
Who seem doomed to travel forward while the
lucky journey aft —
To toil and moil and struggle all the years;
Quiet night comes down at length on the longest
working-day,
And sweet and deep is sleep when grind is past;
There’s a rest at least before us, and the landlord
asks no pay,
For the bed that welcomes weary bones at last!
When day is done, and night’s begun
There’s dreamless sleep a-comin !
M.L.
DORA WILOOX.

expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the

ich ether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 8000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 27, 1897.
To Whomsoever It May Concern.
The announcement that the New South
Wales Government intends fighting the next
election on the question of Upper House re-
form—the same question on which it fought
the last election—is another hint that the
dissolution of Parliament is not far off. It
can’t be more than seven months off, in any
case, and it may come much earlier. Under
these circumstances it is about time some
visitant of ability troubled the green scum
that has gathered on the surface of the poli-
tical pool of Siloam, The Ministry’s state-
ment as to the issue on which it intends
contesting the election may or may not be
true—in an exceptional sort of way it pro-
bably is true, as the issue is a nice easy one,
and the cause of the Upper House, which it
is proposed to fight against, is dead already,
which makes it still easier. All that is
needed now is to go out and bang the corpse.
The next question is—on what issue do the
other two parties propose to fight, and what
kind of chance have they? The fact that
Premier Reid proposes to use this old dead
subject over again as the crucial issue shows
his whole-souled contempt for the direct
Opposition ; he simply proposes to promise
his old broken promise once more, with a
blessed conviction that his opponents haven’t
sense enough to offer anything better. And
the Opposition, pegging away dismally at its
one subject-—Protection—has so far fully
justified Reid’s contempt.
* * * *
This steady “pegging away,” as Opposi-
tion leader Lyne himself describes it, is one
of the most pitiable aspects of the case. It
is a slow, heavy, cumbrous, utterly unpro-
gressive siege of the Government’s position
which never gets any “ forrarder.” Yet the
Reid Government is a thing of straw—a
house built of cards—a pyramid upside-
down—an egg balanced on its end, or any-
thing else that symbolises the unstable and
the shoddy. A clever democratic leader
could wipe it oft’ the face of the earth at
next election without turning a hair. The
present Opposition leader—barring his steady
pegging away in a dead fashion at the old
fiscal issue—has announced no programme.
The reason he ascribes is that his pro-
gramme, if prematurely exposed, is liable to
be stolen by the Government. But the ex-
cuse is of the thinnest, for the present
Ministry has never shown itself clever
enough to successfully steal anything. The.
only time it ever stole anything with an
appearance of success was when it assumed
the role of prophet of Federation, and it
hasn't even been able to keep up a decent
pretence of sincerity in the Federal cause.
The very last thing the Reid Ministry would
steal is a vigorous and comprehensive demo-
cratic policy. It has dropped almost every
one of its own democratic views by the road-
side as it went along, and now in its old age
and its Toryism it is only waiting to be
thrown out when the right man comes.
* * * *
There is a wealth of material in New South
Wales out of which to make a vigorous
Liberal policy. The Local Government
idea, which has been dropped every year for
the last quarter-century, and which has just
been dropped again with an even more
destructive thud than usual, is waiting for
some live leader to take it up. So is the
reform of the Upper House, by making it a
paid elective body, chosen by manhood, or
manhood and womanhood suffrage, the
whole province voting as one constituency.
Likewise the province badly wants an effi-
cient scheme for the prevention of deadlocks;
and if both Houses are made equally elective
and equally responsible to the nation the
policy of letting them decide disputed ques-
tions by sitting and voting as one would be a
simple and efficient solution. The abolition of
fees in State schools, thus making education
absolutely free, and the imposition of news-
paper postage —which would about pay for
free-education —are two financial reforms
which the Reid Government assuredly would
not borrow. Another one, which Lvne
could have all to himself if he chose
to take it up, is the passing of an
Act to absolutely prohibit borrowing ex-
cept for directly reproductive works;
the whole system of Premier Reid’s finance
depends on charging huge sums spent on
unreproductive works to loans, and he would
either have, to oppose such a . measure to the
utmost and pose as a spendthrift, or accept
it and have an annual deficiency of a million,
or accept it and go back on Freetrade for
the sake of revenue. Also, there is abundant
room for another Act providing that new
loans, even for reproductive works, shall
bear such a proportion to the increase of
population that the debt per head shall be a
diminishing quantity every year. And that
also is an item which Premier Reid wouldn’t
borrow from anybody’s programme.
* • * *
New South Wales is pre-eminently the
land of huge properties ; much more than
half the freehold area is held in estates of
10,000 acres and upwards, and nearly one-
third in areas of 40,000 acres and upwards.
There is a magnificent opening for a law,
similar to that which has proved so success-
ful in Maoriland, for the compulsory resump-
tion of big estates at an independent valua-
tion and their division into small holdings,
and ever since the Reid Government
dropped this idea so unobtrusively over two
years ago it has been waiting for some
vigorous democrat to take possession of it.
Further, the country wants a graduated land-
tax after the Maoriland plan -which will
further burst up the big estates and the
absentees, in place of the present purely
revenue arrangement which taxes the
agriculturist and the speculator, the
large and small holder, the citizen and
the absentee, all in the same ratio. It wants
a graduated income-tax which will differenti-
ate between the man whose income is derived
from property and the one whose sole means
of support are his. personal earnings. It
wants a probate-duty, quite unlike Premier
Reid’s iniquitous proposal, which will exempt
small estates and give special exemptions to
widows and orphans in poor or very moderate
circumstances. Also it is waiting for some
reliable person to offer it a State Land Bank
after the Victorian and S.A. pattern, since
the Reid Government has defaulted on this
idea along with many others ; likewise a
decent banking law ; a State note-issue ; an
old-age pension scheme ; a State Life Insur-
ance Department ; a plan for the election of
Ministers by Parliament, in place of the
foolish and iniquitous Party-system ; a well-
devised and thorough-going Protective tariff;
and a few other things.
*.* * *
The Bulletin puts this matter to Opposi-
tion-leader Lyne. He has never yet been
Premier, so he is not definitely committed to
any policy. When he was a Minister, he
belonged to a Cabinet which, by abolishing the
plural vote, performed the straightest demo-
cratic action ever done in recent New South
Wales politics, so he can yet claim, if he has
sufficient assurance, to be a Democrat,
hitherto suffering under shameful misrepre-
sentation. He belongs to a party which used
to be the advocate of every Liberal measure
and the opponent of everything Tory, so he
can again claim to be a Democrat, if he has
the awful amount of gall which such a claim
would require. He has so far refused to
advance any policy apart from Protection, so
he can say, if he likes, that his Democratic
instincts have been boiling in concealment
under his shirt-front all this l ime. He has
nothing to gain by his half-hearted alliance
with the Upper House ; the Upper House
has done nothing for him, and never can do
anything for him; besides, the present
Legislative Council will die just as soon as
the Reid majority makes up its mind to
carry out its long-promised reform, and it is
no good being in alliance with a corpse.
Lyive is not young and not known to be
affluent, and he has no earthly prospects of
the Premiership as things are going. But if
he likes to take up in real earnest, from
either good or bad motives, a thoroughly
Democratic policy, and to hire a really
clever “ghost” to prevent him making any
worse blunders than are absolutely neces-
sary, he has still a future before him. He
may hold the Premiership for six consecu-
tive years, and grow rich, and be in
the running for the billet of head of
the Federal State, and have good jobs to
give away to his friends, and go to England
at next Jubilee, and be made a deeply-exe-
crated baronet, and when he is dead he may
have a huge funeral and a statue, and ten
columns of indignant denunciation in the
daily papers, and an obituary in the London
Times stating that he was one of the most
remarkable products of his age.
• • * *
If Lyne doesn’t jump at the vacant situa-
tion, the same offer is open to whomsoever
the matter may concern. The most easily-
won Premiership in the Seven Provinces is
waiting for the clever and aggressive Demo-
crat who likes to rise to the occasion and
grab at the office. Premier Reid can’t go
straight in financial matters ; the immense
sacrifices he has pledged himself to make in
the interests of the Sydney importers have
rendered it impossible through sheer want of
money. And he can’t carry out any big re-
forms through absolute lack of the necessary
constructive ability. The Labor-party, as at
present constituted, has apparently no possible
show of getting into office because it lias no
fiscal policy and no definite financial policy,
and because it is a Labor-party instead of the
all-round Democratic party which the coun-
try requires. So New South Wales, with
almost the most liberal franchise in Australia,
is in the curious situation of being run by a
Government which is three-parts Tory, and
by an Opposition which, according to its re-
cent acts, is all Tory save on the one issue of
Protection. And, with so much waiting to
be done, the next election is to be fought on
the same old lonesome issue which was
threshed to death at the election of 1895,
and nearly to death in 1894. On both these
occasions the matter was practically settled
just the way Reid asked that it should be
settled, and the proposal to pound the
ancient, defunct subject all over again in
1898 is very dispiriting.
A Bank Where the Wild-Cat Grows.
The report of the Parliamentary Investigation
Committee upon the Queensland National Bank
is about the wildest and weirdest financial docu-
ment which ever got into print. It is difficult
to imagine that any other such bank was ever
run outside a comic-opera. Yet the story told
by the committee is put into the mildest pos-
sible language ; and most of the horrors are ex-
pressed in such gentle phrase that it is quite
obvious the investigators desired to make things
as smooth as possible for the influential persons
concerned. Also, it is an unfinished tale at
the best. The committee acknowledges that it
draws a veil of oblivion over many of the bank’s
huge bad debts on the ground that they were
merely the result of imprudence and bad bank-
ing and general foolishness. This is the only
place in which the report seems to infer that
the losses it does particularise were the result
of something worse than imprudence or foolish-
ness or bad banking. On the face of things,
many of them range from crime to idiocy, and
the mild-mannered committee evidently felt
that they did, though it didn’t like to say so.
* * * *
The Q.N. Bank was apparently an institu-
tion which ran itself. The doors were left
open, and the winds blew overdrafts out and
bad debts in very much as they listed. And
the manager did what he darned well pleased,
and the directors did what the manager told
them to do, and the auditors did more or less
likewise, and the subordinate officials never
kicked, and the business was mostly run by
the office-cat. When the manager died he had
an overdraft of about .£68,000, of which £46,000
is lost and irrecoverable. Two directors didn’t
know of the existence of this huge bad debt,
and the third one knew of it but thinks that
he thought the other two directors had autho-
rised it when he was out—or something of the
sort. The committee, therefore, concludes
that the Man Who Is Dead “made large
advances to himself without the sanction of the
board ” —his employers. In ordinary commer-
cial life, when a man makes advances to himself
out of the cash without the sanction of his
employers, it is generally called plain embezzle-
ment, but banking has a code of ethics of its
own. Thomas MTlwraith, the Gentleman
Who Is Away, owed the bank, in June, 1896,
£251,000, of which £190,000 was covered by no
security. Also he seems to have been respon-
sible for £77,000 of losses apart from the above,
and to have had eight overdrafts altogether,
and to have received a present of £lO,OOO
under certain very incomprehensible circum-
stances. The board claims that it had no idea at
all that MTlwraith owed anything like this
sum, so between Drury and MTlwraith— the
Man That’s Dead and the Gentleman Who is
Away—one-half the capital seems to have
stepped out and forgotten to come back with-
out the board noticing its absence. And yet
the board claims to be a sane institution of
good business capacity, and neither blind, deaf
nor senile-
* * * *
The unspeakable dunder-headedness of the
board sticks out everywhere. From November,
1893, to June, 1894, it didn’t even keep an
official record of its own acts, so that it can
hardly tell now for certain what it did or did
not do, or whom it lent money to or how much
it lent, or why it lent the money. The records
from June, 1894, to February, 1895, were
written up from notes, or from memory, or
from somebody’s pencil memoranda, or hiero-
glyphics, or something, well on in 1896, so
whether they are a true record of what the
board did or forgot to do the board doesn’t
really know. And the record seems to be a
false one anyhow, for each meeting’s report
begins by stating that the previous meeting’s
minutes were read and confirmed, whereas six
months’ minutes were apparently confirmed all
in a heap about a year afterwards. The board
apparently didn’t mind this discrepancy in the
least. Among other things, the Q.N. Bank did
a gigantic gamble in Mount Morgan shares, and
by buying at a high price and selling at a low
one it lost <£350,000 —nearly half its capital.
What directors, or directors’ friends, or friends
of the management it might be who thus
passed their loss on to the bank doesn’t appear.
Some person or persons—the report doesn’t say
who—lied so horribly about the solvency of the
bank that this loss was never mentioned in the
balance-sheet, and some other persons—not
specified—were so utterly unfit to be at large
that they never discovered that these other
people were lying. In the case of almost every
big bad debt the board says blankly that it
“ didn’t know.” Some of the things which the
board apparently knew, however, were almost
as shady as a lot of the things it wasn’t aware
of. For instance, when the bank made its last
new issue of shares the public was always per-
mitted to believe that they were all taken up
by outside investors, whereas the Q.N. Bank
took up a lot of them itself; and it bought more
afterwards at a high figure to keep up the
market price, and it is a large holder of its own
utterly useless shares to this day. The
directors admittedly knew of this, and appar-
ently failed to see anything remarkable or ob-
jectionable about it. As one result, the alleged
callable capital was, to all intents and purposes,
a lie, and the people who depended on it as
security were grossly deceived, for the bank
couldn’t strengthen itself in time of need by
calling on itself to pay itself the money which
it owed itself on shares held by itself.
* * * •
In 1892, it is admitted by one of the directors,
the Queensland Government floated a big loan
“in the interests- of the bank.” In other
words, it borrowed a vast sum on the public
credit, in order to keep the bank afloat.
MTlwraith was the Treasurer who supplied
this money—or Griffith on MTlwkaith’s
guarantee that all was right—and MTl-
wraith seems to have got a lot of it in
personal overdraft, and the directors say
they didn’t know of MTlwraith’s over-
draft—or most of it. Anyhow, the direc-
tors plainly knew, five years ago, that
their bank w-as in great trouble. Yet they
claim that they went on taking the manager’s
assurance for everything, and knowing nothing
about many of the big, disastrous debts,
and apparently they were aware that their
institution was on the rocks, and yet not aware
how it got there, and too tired to find out. It
is claimed that they often asked the manager
for information which he never supplied, so
apparently the board was too utterly weak and
incapable and too great a fool to make its ser-
vant answer its questions. During 1894 and
1895, it is stated, the profit and loss account
was cooked or falsified to the extent of .£71,000
—without the directors’ knowledge and without
the auditors noticing anything very unusual.
The bank was in such a dreadfully tight place
for the last eight years that, in the committee’s
opinion, the payment of dividends should have
been stopped in 1889, but the big, rich, pro-
sperous, acute business men who formed the
directorate never heard about it. One of them
even swears that in 1892—when the insolvent
concern had been paying dividends out of losses
for nearly three years, and when it had to’
bolster up the market price of its shares by
buying them itself on a large scale at a heavy
loss, and the same year when it had to be saved
from collapse by a big State loan—he “had
full confidence in the solvency of the bank.’
As a self-presented certificate of incapacity and
shameful neglect of duty, this statement is
unique.
* * • •
Among the things these astonishing directors
say they didn’t know, the general system of
management w r as one. There was never any
proper method of inspection. One branch, it is
alleged, w'as not inspected for 12 years, though
in that time £BOO,OOO blew out of its open door
in advances and overdrafts. Books containing
a record of the advances stood habitually on the
board-room table, but years rolled on, and the
board apparently never looked at them, and all
it can say about bad debts of £40,000 and
£50,000 and £lOO,OOO and a quarter-million, and
other huge sums, is that it “doesn’t know.”
Even when manager Drury’s policy ended in
the 1893 smash they only went on trusting him
more blindly than ever. Nobody ever appears
to have kicked against The Man That’s Dead.
He suppressed the most awful reports presented
to him by the inspectors, and they let the
matter pass. The local manager at Brisbane
had strong opinions about certain ugly transac-
tions, but “ did not think it his duty to remon-
strate.” The assistant-secretary dul likewise.
The auditors, whose duty it was to supervise
the manager, say that they also saw some weird
doings, but accepted his assurance that all was
right. So the shareholders relied on the direc-
tors, and the directors and the staff and the
auditors relied on the manager, and the manager
pegged out, leaving a personal bad debt of
£46,000 and some £3,000,000 of other losses,
and some of the directors died also, and the
most sensational overdrafter lies low in Ger-
many, and nobody is responsible.
* * * •
As the voice of the late manager can t be
heard from his peaceful residence among the
worms all this tale is, of course, to a great
extent, the ex parte statement of the other
fellow—the great aggregate other fellow. If
Drury had been alive the tale might, or might
not, have read differently. As matters stand,
the old board has either to pose as being, in
whole or in part, a liar and swindler and falsi-


fier of accounts, or as being only a shamefully
foolish and neglectful ass, which was taken in
every day for years by its own servant —and it
accepts the latter role. Probably the role is
the true one, and the inference to be drawn
from it is that the said board, for complete and
unparalleled stupidity, is beyond description.
Also, that it got its fees under false pretences,
inasmuch as it received them for watching over
the shareholders’ interests, and never made a
decent show of watching over anything. As
for the auditors, the only thing to be said about
them is that they were a shade more stupid and
neglectful, and utterly incapable, than the
management; apparently they never saw any
of the large swindles, and, when they came
upon the small ones, they accepted the assur-
ances of the man they were paid to watch and
supervise that all was perfectly right. In
which respect they rank with the detective
who always lets his prisoner go on his personal
assurance that he is a perfectly respectable
member of society. And as for Premier
Nelson, who inspected the utterly bankrupt
and broken concern in 1893, and couldn’t find
anything wrong at all, and gave his personal
assurance that the hopeless derelict was quite
sound, he takes a high place as the prize incap-
able of the crowd.
• * * •
The Bulletin’s plea for a regular State
audit of all banks comes in just here. Of all
the banks which have burst under disgraceful
conditions in Australia during the last few
years—and they run to two dozen or so—there
isn’t one in which the directors didn’t plead
their own blank idiocy, or incapacity, or their
shameful neglect of business, as an excuse. And
there isn’t one, however rotten, which the
auditors didn’t certify as quite sound to the
very last; nor is there, so far as The Bulletin
remembers, one case in which the auditors
didn’t explain afterwards that they relied on
the assurances of the men they were appointed
to supervise, and took tilings on trust, and fell
in. And The Bulletin doesn’t believe there
was one case in which the auditors wouldn’t
have been promptly sacked if they had
attempted to expose anything, for, as a rule,
they are appointed by the votes of the manage-
ment and the directors and their friends and
proxies, and these people don’t appoint them to
make trouble, but rather to keep quiet. The
case of the Q.N.B. is an instance of how the
private auditor can go on for years putting his
respectable autograph to the accounts of an
institution which is insolvent to the extent of
nearly £3,000,000, and never see anything all
that time. And the necessity for strict audit
by an entirely different kind of official,
who isn’t appointed for the express purpose
of never seeing anything, is too conspicuous
to require explanation. Also it seems about
time that other provinces followed the lead of
Victoria by making directors responsible for
something. At present they are not responsible
for anything. And if it is decent justice that
the man who wrecks £5O worth of old ketch by
a minute’s negligence shall be heavily punish-
able there should be some punishment for the
men who wreck about £3,000,000 worth of
property by 10 years’ consistent slumbering
while on duty. According to the most chari-
table view’ of their conduct, the Q.N. Bank
directors never woke up, except to draw their
fees and dividends.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Taxation of Racing Frizes.
Dear Bulletin, —The unfairness of the Vic.
Income-tax Commissioners’ action in levying
upon the stakes won by “ foreign ” horse-
owners at the V.R.C. and other race-meetings
is so obvious that it amounts to a grave scandal.
If the Vic. law sanctions the proceedings, it
practically places Mr. Prout Webb in the
position of a licensed brigand. By no rule of
reasoning can the stakes won at a particular
meeting be said to represent income, a point
which is admitted by even Mr. Webb when he
consents to recognise a claim for expenses as a
set-off against the nominal winnings of a visit-
ing stable. Income, in the ordinary way, re-
presents the annual profit derived from all the
investments, taking one with another, of the
taxed individual; therefore it stands to common
sense that the turf - income of a man
who keeps a stud of horses for racing purposes
is merely the surplus (if any) of business re-
ceipts over expenditure. Every year he pays
away an amount of money on account of the
purchase and, perhaps, the breeding of blood-
stock. Also, he has to charge the business
with training bills, jockeys’ fees, &c., and,
above all, entrance fees. All these items figure
on the debit side of his ledger, whilst he credits
himself with the gross amount of stakes won
plus any receipts from the sale of his blood-
stock. It is not an extraordinary circumstance
for a race-owner in England to be .£lO,OOO out
at the end of a year’s racing which may have
yielded him a few thousands in stakes receipts.
It is usual, rather than otherwise, for an
owner to lose a little by his racing specu-
lations, quite apart from the betting
question. Gains or losses by betting have really
nothing to do with the case of Mr. Prout
Webb’s conscienceless loot. He levied upon
stakes, and for so doing he had no more honest
justification than he could plead for the taxing
of a merchant’s gross profit from one particular
deal in sugar, pig-iron, blankets, or chewing-
gum. Considering that the income-tax is an
old English institution, I am amazed at the
meek submission of N.S.W. “sports” to this
most illogical claim. When a French horse is
taken across the Channel to capture a stake in
the income-tax’s native country, the French
owner is not required to leave a percentage of
his stake-winnings with the British Govern-
ment. To be quite safe in my assertion, let
me say that I’ve never heard of the im-
position being practised, or even suggested.
If there be English precedent for the Prout
Webb policy, then, of course, the vigilant com-
missioner is absolved from all suspicion of
“bluff,” and the Act that he interprets is not
without excuse. But, whether Mr. Webb’s
claim be new and original, or merely an old
English trick, matters not to the bleeding
victims. The tax is shamefully unfair to the
persons chiefly interested. Moreover, it is
a pretty bad thing for the general public,
already suffering enough from the roguery of
horse-owners who would no doubt be much less
dishonest if they had better opportunities to
make money by going straight. The Aus-
tralian Turf is cursed with the poverty —com-
parative poverty—of its “ patrons.” And here
we have Prout Webb making them poorer
than ever.— Touter.
A Pill for Ikey Mo.
Somewhat late in the day the Vic. Racing
Club committee has discovered that something
must be done towards renovating the character
of the Australian Turf, which is now a shock-
ingly fraudulent institution, even by com-
parison with the Turf of ten years ago. The
beautiful Fiemington course—the best-managed
and most attractive show-place of its kind in
the world—was becoming notorious as a happy
hunting, or rather, racing ground for rogues.
No sudden reversal of form seemed too extra-
ordinary for acceptance by the V.R.C. rulers.
Meanwhile, the easy-going Australian people,
instead of asserting their right to decent treat-
ment from the horse-owners who depend upon
their patronage for “added money” in racing
stakes, were content to scramble for crumbs
of disgusting “ information ” re animals
“ on the job ” or “ off it.” But of
late the people have shown a growing indiffer-
ence to “ sport ” conducted on these crooked
lines. Punters of the James White-Donald
Wallace era were converted into mocking
cynics as time went on. To take an interest in
modern racing for the sake of the sport was to
invite ridicule, and nothing can survive a long
continuance of ridicule when its shafts are
pointed with reasonable argument. Something
had to be done by the Fiemington authorities.
They tackled the recent Parthenopreus scandal
feebly in the first instance, but with surpris-
ing determination subsequently, and the re-
sult is most satisfactory. In disqualifying
the horse and its owners for life the
V.R.C. committee has loosened Ikey Mo’s
grip of racing management. Samuel Allen
and Thomas Arnfield were bookmakers—
“distinguished” bookmakers, in the language
of sporting rags. They are now branded with a
conviction for fishy practice as horse-owners, and
the careless racing public will perhaps give a
little thought to the matter of Ikey Mo’s bear-
ing upon turf swindles in general. Australia has
no racing aristocracy to speak of—meaning by
“aristocracy ” a class of wealthy horse enthu-
siasts who, being personally honorable in all
gambling transactions, can afford to make
honesty the best policy for their employes.
Possessing few, very few, unimpeachable
owners, Australia is naturally lacking in
trainers and jockeys of reputation. Where are
there any rich enviable positions open to
men who might become artists in their
respective lines if a big future lay before
them ? The late Mr. James White, and, in
a smaller way, Mr. Donald Wallace had
positions to offer to their employes, conse-
quently their horses were sent “fit” to the
post, and were ridden by the two best jockeys
Australia has known— Tom Hales and Mick
O’Brien. The White and Wallace stables
showed this country how horse-racing could be
conducted—there is little enough of such racing
nowadays. Victoria, in particular, is cursed
by a crowd of ignorant, dissolute stablemen,
not well-versed in any business save roguery.
They are duffers working for duffers, and their
low cunning has brought them mostly into the
meshes of Ikey Mo. Said Ikey was, in fact,
beginning to do pretty well as he pleased with
Victorian racing (not to mention that of other
provinces) when the V.R.C. gave him this Par-
thenopseus pill to swallow.
The Live Relative and the Dead One.
Seven or eight years ago the Gillies Ministry
tried to shelve a great difficulty in the usual
fashion—by appointing a commission to inquire
into the charitable institutions of Victoria. In
those days, it was the fashion to have every-
thing inquired into by a commission, and to
leave it at that. This particular commission
reported that the Victorian charities were in-
sufficient, badly managed, badly supervised,
partially insolvent, and a disgrace generally,
and Gillies then sat down to rest and let
things slide. They had slid for many years
before Duncan got this far, and, until the pre-
sent year, they continued sliding under the
care of Duncan’s successors. Now Turner has
tackled the question with his usual vigor, and
his beastly tendency for doing things threatens
to burst up a useful old war-cry—one that is
almost as old as the Local-Government idea in
N.S. W. Turner’s scheme is short and to the
point. He will give the Vic. charitable insti-
tutions a fresh start by paying off all their over-
drafts, and they are warned to walk a chalk-line
in future and keep clear of further embarras-
ments. The State is to continue its endowment
of .£lOO,OOO a year. A certain proportion of
public house licensing-revenue—about £BOOO a
year will also be given to the cause of
charity/ A tax of 5 per cent, on the
gate - money at out - door sports probably
another £BOOO a year—is to be levied for
the same good purpose, if anybody can find out
how to gather in the money. The charities are
to collect what they can from private persons,
as heretofore, and if these funds are not suffi-
cient a municipal tax of not more than l|d. in
the £—equal to £70,000 a year—may be levied
to fill the gap. In addition to all this, large
powers are conferred on the Board wdiich is to
administer the fund to compel the relatives of
the sick, senile, and disabled to contribute
towards their support—and it is just here that
the one illogical feature of the case comes in.
The powers granted in this direction are very
extensive —so much so, as one Melbourne paper
points out, that part of the earnings of the
humble servant-girl may be annexed to help
maintain her brother, whose only occupation is
that of belonging to a “ push,” while he is re-
covering from a broken arm received while
fighting with a fellow-drunk or kicking at a
policeman. Also the man with 30s. a week and
five young children to maintain may be called
upon to contribute towards the repair of the
small family’s disreputable uncle who got his
head broken while insulting someone else’s wife.
Yet in Victoria, as in the other Australias, the
law still recognises the alleged “right” of
everybody to leave his property where he
pleases, and to disinherit his wife, son, daughter,
father, mother, brother, sister, and all other
relatives. And the Turner Govt., so far, doesn’t
propose to interfere with this arrangement.
So the old person who has a humble income of
30s. a week from property may be compelled,
while he is alive and really needs the money,
to share it with his disabled son or daughter
or wife—which is logical enough, as these
persons have at least more claim on him
than on strangers. But when he dies and
doesn’t want the money any longer, he may
leave it to any stranger he pleases, and compel
the public to support his disabled relative afore-
said—which isn’t at all logical. When it is
proposed to give such large powers to compel
the live relative to pay up the complete
irresponsibility of the dead relative is an obvious
absurdity.
Everybody’s Girl.
“ Soda-AND-B.” on an enthralling subject:—
Ed. Bulletin, — A Melb. suburban branch of the A.N.A.
invited a discussion on barmaids, the other evening-, and
John Vale, of the Victorian Alliance, came along to pro-
test the awful immorality of Hebe as a class. Vale being
teetotally ignorant of his subject, repeated the ancient
temperance story that “ fresh young girls came from the
country and entered first-class hotels as barmaids. They
soon went on the down-grade, drifting into lower-class
public-houses, then to the streets and the haunts of
Chinese, and finally to the hospital and the grave.”
Vale’s narrative opens with an untruth, as every bar-
loafer will perceive at a glance. The girls at first-class
hotels are, as a rule, matured and full of wisdom
(of a kind) rather than young and fresh. Serving
behind a bar is like other lines of business in
that experience, steadiness and ability go far to-
wards achieving the better class of billets. The
proportion of girls “ ruined ” through constant association
with drinkers and drink is not so vast as Vale likes to
suppose, and many of those who do go utterly to the bad
were very likely to get there even before they faced the
temptations of their new life. The one really great
objection to smiling Hebe’s presence in the social system
is not perceptible to blue-ribbonist Vale. Neither is it
apparent to the habitual frequenters of pubs. Society
suffers less from the demoralisation of barmaids by their
customers than from the harm done to customers through
their friendships, more or less affectionate, with the girl
who hands them the bottle. At the little meeting where Va le
spoke nonsense a certain municipal councillor hit Che mark
when he said “ Some men take a pride in offering disgust-
ing insults to defenceless women behind a bar. A girl in
that position becomes unwomanly owing to her continual
contact with masculine depravity and vice.” All bar-
maids are in that position sometimes—seldom or often as
the case may be —and the most respectable of them are
bound to become unwomanly to some extent. If “good”
girls, they are quite antithetic to Trilby, of whom her
author said that she possessed every female virtue save
the one that played the name part. A “modest” Hebe
conserves the one virtue whilst losing her original respect
for nearly everything else that is admirable in woman.
She practises deception mechanically, she lures
men to drink in a calm, ordinary' way of busi-
ness. She judges all human nature by the nature of
the man she sees oftenest (inevitably a pretty worthless
lot), and by the girls who, working beside her in the bar
business, see life only as she sees it. Because she is a
good woman gone wrong in all her ideas of honor, save
the “ honor’’that Bland Holt’s melodrama heroines are
so fond of referring to, she has a false attraction for cus-
tomers in general. Men who have ever been proof against
the wiles of a mere flimsy, yellow-haired Flossie are apt
to get drawn by this good girl into bar-loafing habits, and
“ unfortunate attachments,” and other phases of foolish-
ness incidental to an honest faith in Everybody’s Girl.
She is unwomanly. The male beast who offers disgusting
insults is to blame, but the other, and more decent,
fellows must pay a penalty’ for his crime.

On Behalf of the Widow.
Here is an interesting item for the considera-
tion of humane people—if the race hasn’t died
out. Last year, N.S. YV. Postmaster-General
Cook estimated that his department was run at
a loss of ,£133,000. It is this deficit which
makes Premier Reid’s new probate duties neces-
sary. A great part of the deficit arises through
carrying N.S.W. newspapers free, and thereby
protecting the newspaper-owning industry at
the expense of the people, and contrary to the
declared policy of the Government, and subsi-
dising the proprietors of wealthy journals at
the public cost, and thus coddling and pauper-
ising these people. Even if the imposition of a
Jd.-postage rate reduced the N.S. W. newspaper
postal circulation to the level of thatfof Victoria,
where postage is charged, it would still yield
£50,000 per annum! And £50,000 would enable
the Government to exempt from probate tax the
widow who is left with £lOOO (equal to from
12s. to 15s. per week), or less, as the sole sup-
port of herself and her family. Especially in
the case of the widow who loses her bread-
winner and her sole source of income, and is
left with about £2OO worth of property, con-
sisting of the cottage and furniture, as the only
resource between her little family and utter
want, it would save the necessity of sel-
ling the parlor sticks to raise the special
tax which Premier Reid proposes to
lay upon her by way of celebrating her
bereavement. And The Bulletin asks Premier
Reid —seeing that he will really be the ghoul
who seizes the widow’s parlor-table for probale-
duty on the cottage and furniture, even if the
actual doing of the dirty work is entrusted to a
deputy-,ghoul—whether he thinks it a fair thing
to tax this kind of widow in order that the
wares of the rich Telegraph, and Herald,
and Evening News, and Star, and Bulletin
proprietaries should be carried free? Unless
George Reid has the soul of a bum-bailiff of
the worst description that question can admit
of only one answer.

“Oh, yes; her milk was mixed with that of
others, and distributed around.” Thus a Sydney
suburban dairyman, with reference to a cow
which, when slaughtered, was found to be in-
ternally “ riddled with tuberculous abscesses,”
and which, according to the veterinary-inspec-
tor, must, “ for months before, have presented
a glaring case of disease.” The whole premises,
in fact, had been thus converted into such a
hot-bed of tuberculosis that six other cows had
also to be destroyed, after which, in order that
no chance of carefully disseminating phthisis
might be thrown away, the corrupted carcases
were disposed of to a “ poultry-farmer,” whose
fowls, in company with dogs and cats, were actu-
ally found by the inspector feeding upon the dis-
eased flesh. The danger to human beings through
fowls thus nourished is obvious and direct,
and the dogs and cats, a 3 the health authorities
tranquilly observe, would also probably infect
humanity “through being eaten by pigs”—
which is in itself a truly pleasing consideration.
And—while all that can apparently happen to
a wholesale death-dispenser of this sorb is the
cancellation of his license—we have the aston-
ishing assurance to call ourselves a civilised
community. We hang the man who straight-
forwardly stabs his enemy; we “cancel the
license ” of the creature who, for the sake of gain,
cold-bloodedly decimates a city suburb.
The Westralian Draco who sentenced a couple
of men to 36 lashes and 12 months in irons, for
escaping from Fremantle gaol, was defended in
W.A. Assembly by a member who alleged that
the weapon in use for whipping is “ a mere
plaything.” Slight modifications in the struc-
ture of the lash have been lately made in most
of the gaols of Australia, but the instrument is
far from being “ a plaything.” The cord-tails
are still nine, and the knots on each tail are three,
and the flagellator is as heartless a brute as ever.
By the way, in consequence of certain alterations
having been privately made in a number of
gaols in the weight and texture of this instru-
ment of torture Australian Parliaments should
ask for a full official description of those at
present in use. Originally the British Govern-
ment prescribed the length, weight, and num-
ber of knots which the Australian lash was to
disport, and the early Governors used regularly
to handle the instrument to make sure that the
regulations were complied with. That custom
has fallen into disuse, and the public are
thus entitled to a little light on the current
humors of the Criminal Law departments.
There is some analogy betw'een the absence of
machinery to enable a private member of As-
sembly to have his Bill introduced into the
Council, as noted in these columns lately, and a
condition of things brought to light in England
in 1893 with reference to the subject’s imme-
morial right of petitioning Parliament. It
seems that an individual wished to petition
Parliament to inquire into the alleged unjust
conduct of a Judge, but that members, either
through fear of offending the Judge, or for
social, friendly, or other reasons, refused to
present his petition. He brought an action
against one of them, but lost it, the Court of
Appeal declaring, with bombastic wisdom, that
while undoubtedly every subject had a right to
petition Parliament, yet, although he could only
do so by getting a member to present such peti-
tion, he could not compel any member to do so.
This would equally apply in Australia, and re-
form is needed in this case, as in the private
member’s Bill case alluded to, whenever any
Constitutional Reform Bill is before Australian
Parliaments.
That artful old man, Premier Reid, definitely
announces that he will fight the next N.S.W.
general-election on the question of reform of
the Upper House. The announcement was
made on the same day that he backed down
utterly to the Council over the Land Tax Vali-
dating Bill. Why he wants to fight over
Council reform nobody knows. He fought the
last election upon it, and came back with a big
majority—in fact, nobody really objected to him
reforming the Upper House. Since then, for
years, nobody has tried to prevent him
doing it at any moment, and nobody prevents
him doing it now' —nobody except himself, that
is. There is no person of any account to fight
wdth over the question, unless Premier Reid is
going to have a fierce and blubbery conflict
with himself. Considering that there is really
no objection and no opposition, G.R.’s economy
in saving up this question for a war-cry is very
economical indeed. Meanwdiile, the Opposition
seems to have nothing at all to go upon—except
Protection. Yet a really lovely battle-whoop
is waiting to be taken up by somebody ; the
necessity for a law prohibiting the expenditure
of loan moneys on any save directly reproduc-
tive works. That is the most urgently-needed
reform of the day—and there is no danger of
Premier Reid stealing it, anyhow. That
gaudy spendthrift and bequeather of obligations
to posterity would sooner steal a red-hot stove
and a live death-adder.

Victoria must be beginning to gaze suspiciously
at the “mixed” system of teaching in Slate-
schools and Bible-classes. Not long ago there
w'as the case of a pious warehouseman who
“resisted”—successfully, he said—the giddy
advances of a co-worker in short skirts and
golden hair. More recently the Melbourne
public have feasted on the details of a perjury
charge brought by an ex-State-school master
against a young female colleague. Having been
“sacked” for improper dissipations w'ith the
damsel, he desired to retrieve his precious
character by proving that she swore falsely in
denying that the seduction was on her side.
Magistrate Panton ultimately stopped the
accuser’s revelations by saying: “I have
watched the evidence given by this creature
from beginning to end, and though it is
partly supported by filthy documents he
has given it with such shuffling cun-
ning,” &c. Well, Mr. Panton discharged
the girl on the reasonable supposition that no
jury would convict her to gratify the spite of
the “ creature ”in question. Common sense
always takes a liberal discount from the
“scathing remarks” of any learned Judge or
magistrate in these morality cases, but, all the
same, it seems clear enough that simple division
of the sexes should be introduced into State-
schools. The mixing of ’em is responsible for
some very sad allegations concerning the
wide range of woman’s influence. As, for
instance (in a recent case): — (a) “Witness
said he had found the girl intriguing
with every man she met. He had found
her in the school with the head-teacher once,
after marking the time - book as if they
had left. The teacher was a man of about 60.
At another time the teacher’s son, a lad of 17,
was standing at a table close to Miss . He
could see the boy’s passions were aroused by
the expression of his eye. They went behind 'a
screen to continue the flirtation, out of witness’s
view.”

His attitude towards British politics and methods
is that of petulant critic.

The “Royal Divorce,” which unrolled its
military splendor at Her Majesty’s on Saturday
night, is.a drama which must be judged mainly by
its pictorial aspect. The two tableaux of Waterloo
can compare, for solid magnificence, with any-
thing else of the kind which Sydney has seen up
to date; and if they do represent Napoleon
raging in the forefront of the battle, when he was
in reality mostly employed holding his anguished
stomach in the background, that sacrifice was
necessary for the sake of effect. The public bad
to see its Napoleon howling in the front rank, else
it would have demanded its money back. If the
picture had been drawn from the other side of the
story, the same public would probably have in-
sisted on seeing George 111. and George IV., and
Wellington, and Pitt (who was dead), and the
Britannia off the back of the bronze penny, all
charging together. Another picture which will
do a great deal to keep the drama alive, and which
even lays over the flaring Waterloo, is the view of
Napoleon doing a lonesome dream, with only the
. sea and the carotty sunset for company, at St.
Helena. By comparison, the retreat from Moscow
falls flat; and the flaming city in the background
—considering that the city had been put out and
became a damp ember some weeks earTh*r, and
that the army was far out of sight of Moscow
before the snow set in—is a gratuitous outrage on
history. Apart from the pictures, the “ Divorce ”
is a series of episodes held together by a very thin
hair of plot. Also the hair straggles off the track
a good deal. There is no record that the old
■ Empress and the new one ever met, or that
Josephineever encountered the baby Kingof Rome.
There is also no record that the divorced Josephine
hung tearfully round the scene of her bygone
. glories, and wouldn’t keep off the grass. No in-
furiated Parisian mob got so far as to attempt to
brain Marie Louise with a knob-kerry when the
news of the great retreat arrived ; and even the
giddy, fluffy, shallow-pated daughter of the Caesars
'was hardly the woman to lie down flat on the
ground, to the serious damage of her pretty frock,
■ and wait peacefully to have her head stepped upon
. by her subjects.
* » * *
The “ Divorce ” is a weep/ drama all
through. The audience gets five distinct views of
Josephine: first, as the broken-hearted woman
who is about to be divorced ; second, when the
new empress calls on her and jeers at her dis-
tresses third, when she drifts, ghost fashion,
round her lost palace and grieves over the King of
Rome who should have been her son but isn’t ;
fourth, when she hovers in suspense around
Jehappes, the day before Waterloo ; and, finally,
when she pleads vainly for permission to accom-
pany her Bonaparte to St. Helena. And it has
five distinct glimpses of Napoleon : when he con-
sents miserably to the divorce ; when he plods
with an icicle hanging from his nose among the
snow, and the dead, and the wolves, getting home
from Moscow; when he is a lost emperor at
Waterloo; then as a prisoner on a British ship;
and, finally, as a dismal spectre on a rock looking
at the sunset. There is not a gleam of happiness
about any of these appearances, and Talley-
rand, dragging an ancient, rheumatic leg
through the story, hovers round like a
cheerless bird of prey and intensifies the gloom.
Still there is a certain tearful vigor about the
drama, and it is a blaze of magnificence all through,
and the three tableaux abovementioned are suf-
ficient in themselves to keep it afloat. Misses Ada
Eerrar and Elliott Page are so convincing that it is
difficult to decide whether the empress who stands
up or the empress who lies down and shrieks is the
better character. As for Napoleon, the author
makes him a strained, high-strung theatrical indi-
vidual, always visibly humping along the vast globe
of his “destiny,” and Julius Knight probably
does hinj as well as circumstances allow. Julius
looks Napoleonic, anyhow. Some other indi-
viduals—especially Atholwood as an innkeeper
with de'irmm tremens —will receive due mention
next week.
** * *
Sydney Palace re-opened on Saturday with
“Binks, the Photographer a poor, little, wilted
brown rag of literature which hasn’t even the
merit of being actively and aggressively bad. It
is flat with the deadly flatness of the great African
desert, and hardly one green vegetable of mirth
breaks the sky-line anywhere. An utter want of
purpose pervades it. In the first act old grand-
father Binks is just thinking of taking his bald
and slippered way to bed on a winter night, when
some of his sons and daughters-in-law call in.
Therefore, they sit up and have supper, and drink
bach other’s health, and make dreary speeches of
congratulation. The proceedings have all the
riotous mirth of a tea-meeting, and when the com-
pany separates grandmother Binks goes to bed,
and grandfather Binks sits down to smoke by the
fire and has a dream. In the dream, which fills
up act 11., grandfather Binks goes back 40 years,
and is again a young and dreadfully tattered and
insolvent photographer. And in his vision a
youth and four girls come in to have their tin-type
taken, and the rest of the proceedings are ordinary
song and dance and mash. Finally, just as Binks
proposes to the prettiest girl, his dream vanishes —
or rather, the way things went on Monday night,
a number of men came in behind, and carried
it away in pieces. There was no illusion
about the change. The gas didn’t even turn
down. There was simply an awful pause, and then
the studio of young Binks began to break up and
go off by instalments, some of the instalments
tottering sideways as they started, while voices
could be heard shouting directions among the
ruins ; and the old room, with old Binks sleeping
m the chair beside the dead fire, was visibly and
laboriously dragged on. It wasn’t quite the same
room, for there wasn't time to replace the furni-
ture ; it was a cold, empty apartment with no-
thing in it but a chair, and a piano, and old
slumbering Binks. Then old Binks gave a
shriek and woke up in the early dawn. And at
this opportune moment all the relatives of the
previous night rushed in and informed him that
his grand-daughter had twins, and the curtain
fell.
» * * *
What The Bulletin has worried about
till its head feels like bursting is the
dream of old Binks represented a real episode in
his early life, and grandmother Binks was actually
the girl who came to be photo-ed in tights 40
years before; or whether Binks was merely
dreaming the undigested, irrelevant lobster which
walketh in darkness. It matters very little either
way ; still this paper feels curious. If it was
rfeal it was a very weak episode in the life
of a very stumpy old man. And if it was
mere lobster the present writer reckons he
had a wilder dream himself last week. “ Binks,”
whose life looks as if it would be shorb
and troubled, is supported principally by George
Lauri, Misses Addie Conyers, Marietta Nash, and
Amy Gourlay, also Willie Freear, Watson, and
William Gourlay. The latter is the thinnest man
in the profession, and if ever he takes to journal-
ism, the contributions will blow right through him
when the door is left open. Willie Freear is really
funny once, but the other people, as a rule, take
* Binks ” in a fitting spirit of solemnity.
** * *
Rather ancient looks old Grattan Riggs
when you meet him in the street nowadays.
Yet, on the stage the veteran goes through his
part with all the vim of fifteen or twenty years
ago, and on the opening night of the “Irish
Detective ” at Sydney Lyceum he seemed to drop
his years off him like a garment. The “Detec-
tive” is practically all Grattan Riggs. He plays
six characters and himself as well, and all the six
characters are exceedingly well disguised. Some
of them are full of fight, and some are full of beer,
and some are Chinese and full of cat-pie, and most
of them are as full of agility as a bull-ant. As a de-
tective Arthur Tracey is a howling stage-joke, inas-
much as he always tears off his disguise just when it
is most successful, and states who he is, and then
covers his retreat with a gun in one hand and his
false back hair in the other without waiting to
really detect anything. This pleases the gallery a
great deal, though it makes the process of detec-
tion very slow. But as an essay on the versatility
of Grattan Riggs, and his capacity for being seven
different persons, all utterly unlike the other six,
the ancient drama has a great deal to recommend
it. Apart from Riggs, the principal feature
is a shriekingly impossible villain who is a fashion-
able doctor by day, and at night murders
casual strangers for the sake of their loose change,
and casts their remains in the cellar, where the
smell would naturally give him away inside a
month. But the gruesome doctor, and the heroine,
and the lost will, and the bones in the cellar, are
all mere side issues. The real beginning, middle
and end of the drama are Riggs out detecting, dis-
guised as a nigger selling clam soup, and a Chow
dealing in pies, and an old woman trading off
bruised fruit at a great reduction ; and that sur-
prising ancient tows the horror through, and
brings it into dock again about 11.15 quite un-
injured.

The new show at Melb. Bijou made a pro-
mising start on Saturday night. The house was
crowded, and the principal items in a long list
of turns ” went off with a bang of superior ap-
probation. Also, the lesser items maintained the
credit of the programme, and put a gilt edge on
the tout ensemble. Comparing the present
popular-priced show at the Bijou with the
variety - entertainment staged by Garner and
Goatcher at the Princess’s this time last
year—*the entertainment that had Knowles for
its central attraction The Bulletin rises to
acknowledge the splendid audacity of Garner’s
management in charging five shillings for a dress-
circle seat. The only business fault to be found
with this Biograph Vaudeville eo.. as a whole,
is the lack of ginger in the mixing, and the
noticeable absence of sheer inanity. It is too good
to be fully appreciated by small boys, or book-
makers on the burst. Miss Lilian Tree, Phil
Newbury, the boy Sherwin, and Sydney Deane
would seem a satisfactory co. if they gave their
turns in a town-hall. Miss Fanny Wentworth
may be bracketted with the conjuror and
the ventriloquist as a clever drawing-room
performance. Alma Grey is a dainty little
make-weight, and the Biograph well, when
Harry Rickards states that he paid £3OOO for the
Australian rights of the Biograph for six months,
he probably doesn’t exaggerate the ease beyond
the limits of theatrical license. The Biograph was
duly honored in this journal a few weeks ago,
likewise all the people who support that worthy
apparatus. Their united efforts appear to have
set a large portion of Melbourne afire, and they
will give eight performances a week, including
matinees on Wednesday and Saturdays, during a
short season.

( At Melb. Royal the latest melodrama is
“Meg the Castaway,” which begins with the un-
doing of a bull pup who chewed a box of matches,
and ends with a happy union between the un-
kempt Castaway and a wealthy amateur trader in
baked potatoes. Miss Maggie Moore imperson-
ates the ragged heroine, whilst Harry Roberts
retires from private life to push a ’tater emporium,
and these two reliable artists are thrown to-
gether by their mutual affection for some-
body s long-lost child - said infant having been
seized as compensation for the damaged dawg.
“ Meg ” is a cheerful, preposterous drama,
full of broad comedy, and sudden lapses into
sentiment and song. The bloom of comparative
novelty has departed from it, and the public
should, as a rule, like “ Meg ” all the better on
that account. A very steep plot is apt to seem
less precipitous at a second time of seeing. To-
night (Thursday) is a great bicycle occasion at the
Royal, under the patronage of the L.V.W. and
other whirling bodies. On Saturday there will be
another revival—of the “Trilby” burlesque, per-
haps. “ Trilby ” wasn’t quite certain at time of
writing, but the advts. will speak for themselves.

Dear Bulletin, —Tn a recent note on a big London social
event, whereat were present “Mrs. Ronalds and several
dukes,” “ Cleo ” asked—“ Who is Mrs. Ronalds, any-
how?” Well, she is of much more importance than
several dukes, for she is the best-known and most influen-
tial person in musical and artistic London. Her Sundays
during the season are rushed by leaders-of thought and
fashion ; the Prince of Wales being a regular caller. She
is the great friend of Sir Arthur Sullivan, and is, by somg,
regarded as the real manager of Covent Garden, and
every other great musical haunt. She entertains all the
big managers, and to be allowed to sing, play, or recite
at her house is sure to bring the favored performer a good
engagement She is American-born, cosmopolitan in
training, and “fetching" as Cleopatra. She is 60 in
years, but Under 40 in looks, and has the daring of the
devil—as, for instance, when she went to the Duchess of
Devonshire’s great historic ball last season with bars of
Sir Arthur Sullivan’s music printed on her frock. No one
but a “down-under” exile would ask “Who is Mrs.
Ronalds? ” for she is the world-known manager of London
brains and beaut}'. — Pat.
A bilious Australian writes from London:—“The new
play Dot Boucicault stage-managed at the Court Theatre
called ‘The Children of the King,’ is a failure, or bids
fair to be. It is neither opera nor drama, but a mixture
of both, and has a symphonic orchestral accompaniment
by ‘ Hansel und Gretel’ Humperdinck. One can’t hear
the words for the music, nor the music for the words.
They get at logger-heads with each other, and after a
strong tussle through several acts, are both overthrown.
Hilda Spong is in the caste, but has nothing to do.
Neither has anyone else except the drums and the double
bass and the fiddles. The awful Cissy Loftus (who ‘ imi-
tates ' everyone without anyone knowing precisely
whether it’s Dan Leno or Madame Favier she’s doing, or
someone else) plays the principal girl part.”
