Writes “ Ignoramus” : The “Two Little Vagabonds” is
automatical melodrama, and the only time the plav is
lifted out of Punch-and-Judyism is inthe jealousy scene
in the first act, wherein Titheradge forgets the type of
play, and goes off on his own, and becomes masterful and
convincing. The play is not a play of human interest;
it is a play which must be watched out of the green eyes
of tender years, or else gloated over by eyes w hich are
affected by a stomach full of tripe and onions and arf and
’arf, and pockets full of pea-nuts. Melodrama, to be suc-
cessful, must be realistic, or gory, or loud, or swearful.
And where these things are wanting, and where the tender
sentiment hovers round an attenuated, consumptive boy,
there is little scope for success. One relieving feature in
the “Two Vagabonds,” however, is that there are four
acts, so there is ample opportunity for munching pea-nuts
and taking in a cargo of onjapullenlemnade.

Dear Bulletin,— Re a recent “ Poverty Point” par. The
first theatre in Adelaide was a wooden structure on North
Terrace. Solomon’s theatre (“ the new theatre”) was
called the Queen’s, and was in Gille’s Arcade, Currie-
street. Opened 11th January, 1841 (under management
of Mr. J. Lazar, Mayor of Adelaide for three successive

S.S.: “Have just heard Maggie Moore singing ‘The
Wearing of the Green ’—The Bulletin long ago said the
song, as sung by M.M., was the most dramatic thing to be
heard on the Australian stage—and am convinced that
the popular W'arbler is an arch Fenian and a rebel from
hair-string to shoe-laces. The sorrows ov ould Oireland
must be very real to the singer, who fires off that emo-
tional wail with such soul-searching, heart-lifting senti-
ment, and the iron heel of the base, bloody, and brutal
Sasenaoh is on her patriotic corns all the time. As Maggie
Moore sings it,‘The Wearing of the Green’ is the Irish
‘Marseillaise,’ but with the moaning of a heart-broken
nation taking the place of the spirited audacity of the
song of God in France. When I hear the ‘Marseillaise,’
I see an army marching in a desert, with shining eyes.
When I hear Maggie Moore sing ‘The Wearing of the
Green ’ I see Erin sitting on a grave, bemoaning the slain
and cursing the slayer.”

There «6ems to have been a flavor of maudlin romance
about the Langtry business, after all. The latest yarn is
that Mr. “ Lily ” used to haunt railway-stations where his
wife was expected, dodge away just in time to avoid her,
dodge back to inquire of the officials how she looked, and
then depart in a painful state of weep. In return for
which he gets from her fair hands a large hunk of white
garden-stuff-on his coffin. Moral : There are two sorts
of women with steel-grey eyes. One sort is loaded.
IL ■ Miss Sutherland, the rough-cut, worthy philan-
thropist, hack in Melb., addressed Pastor Edgar’s last
Wesley Church Pleasant Sunday Afternoon. She related
that, when in London a few weeks ago, she received a
free ticket of admission to one of the West End theatres
for a very fashionable matinee in aid of charity. It
puzzled her who could have sent it, and she went out of
curiosity. The check-taker looked up and down her
plain Quaker;,’ garb. “ Dear, dear, you can’t come in
here.” “Why?” “ Why ?—Royalty is to be here.” Just
then the manager came up, and kindly introduced her
through the stage-door to an excellent seat in front. A
young lady who performed sent the ticket. Miss Suther-
land had rescued her, as a ragged little Waif, in the streets
of Melbourne.

The early decease of the Earl of Egmont, a young man
who used to hang around the London 'alls and “shout"
for the “ stars,” seems to have linked Maoriland to the
British haristocracy. The new Egmont was born at Can-
terbury (M.L.) in 1856. He put in some timeat sea, in the
merchant service, and joined the London Fire Brigade,
from which he rose to the less worrying occupation of
beadle at a town hall. Also, the sassiety historian states
that his lordship married one of Spiers and Pond's bar-
maids.

“ Water used far too lavishly ” is Colonel
Home’s comment on what little irrigation there is
in N.S.W. But he adds that it is the common
failing of new-chums at the game. Also, the sites
for many artesian bores have been badly chosen-
in the lovv-lying parts, instead of at points where
distribution by gravitation would be possible.
One humorous incident of the big Melb.
fire was the way in which barrels of beer and mis-
cellaneous cages and bottles of other liquor were
run out of the threatened pubs, and coolly planked
down on the steps of St, Paul’s Cathedral for a
giant policeman to watch. Another one was the
lady mid nodings on, who got so scared that she
didn’t wait to dress, but fled up-street in the same
condition. This latter incident is humorous or
pathetic, whichever way you look at it.
Mr. A. B. Paterson writes : “In your last
issue, there was a paragraph to the effect that the
Antipodean magazine was nearly falling through
the other day for want of advertisements to the
publishing guarantors’ minimum. This is a sur-
prising statement to make. It makes it appear as
if the Antipodean had not sufficient financial
backbone to come out unless it got a certain num-
ber of advertisements. This is wholly untrue.
The Antipodean is published by George Robert-
son and Sons, and I am editing it; and tHere is
no publishing guarantors’ minimum; and the
magazine would have come out just the same if it
had no advertisements.”

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
According to Part XII, of the N.S.W. Statis-
tical Register, just issued, the N.S.W. deficit at 30th
June, 1897, was £2,363,139—the highest figure it has yet
reached. During the past year it increased £63,000. In
other words, the law compelled Premier Reid to pay off
£160,000 of it, which he did with one hand, while he
added on his new deficit of £213,000 with the other.
Seeing that Mr. Reid claims to have had a surplus last
year, this increase in the deficit is an awkward thing to
account for. And if the present Govt, hadn’t converted
about £340,000 of it into permanent debt by taking that
sum out of loan moneys and putting it into revenue the
deficit would now be well on to £3,000,000. Anyhow',
why does the deficit increase when there is a surplus?
* * * *
For the financial year ended 31st March, 1897,
Maoriland added a trifle of £1,280,435 to its public
debt, which is now £6O 13s. 9d. per head. This
wipes out the progress made in the last eight years.
In 1889 the liability was £6O 12s. 2d. for each
white inhabitant. Then, by a process of hard grinding,
the increase of debt was kept so much below the in-
crease of population that in 1894 the liability was only
£57 Bs. lOd. per head. It is now back' at the old
figure ; and though the borrowings nowadays are mostly
for reproductive works, whereas in the old days they were
mainly for works which didn’t pay a cent of interest
and the business was mostly boom swindle pure and
simple, still there is a vast deal too much loan in M.L.
even now. Somehow, no Australasian province can keep
sober more than five years at a time, ahd very few keep
quite sober for 12 months.
• * • •
Maoriland revenue for the quarter ended 30th
September was £1,169,032—£109,000 more than that for
the corresponding.quarter of 1896, and £178,000 more than
the September quarter of ’95. What wouldn’t some im-
pecunious Australian Treasurers, as they sit on the Trea-
sury doorstep on a hot summer afternoon looking to see
if any revenue is coming up the road, and seeing nothing
but a cloud of dust and a dog, give for such a show as
that ?
N * * * *
Old John Boom Forrest, of Westralia, thinks he
sees his way this year to have £3,008,000 revenue and
£3,319,454 expenditure. If this comes, off, the deficit will
be £311,000, which will swallow up the grand accumulated
surplus, all but £4OOO. John further adds that he doesn’t
propose asking avithority for anymore loans at present,
which, seeing that he has already got authority to borrow
five millions, is a striking instance of moderation.
* • * 1 *
The .£120,000 of Victorian 3 per cent, stock
issued in Melbourne for conversion purposes realised
£lO3 Bs. Id., which is by far the highest price obtained
for any Australian loan up to date. The next best trans-
action of the kind was the N.S.W. 3 per cent, loan floated
in Sydney at £lOO. After that comes the Westralian 3
per cent, stock which sold in London at £99 ss. sd. net.
The fact that the two best records were put up in the
local market is another hard smite to the old superstition
that Australia can’t possibly struggle through without
the aid of English capital.

In the amazing matter of the report on the Q.N.
Bank, part of M’llwraith’s defence is that he and manager
Drury colleagued to do —so-and-so. The Man that’s Dead
and the Gentleman Who Is Away are bad enough singly;
but the Q.N. funds found the combination irresistible.
Queensland National Bank director Arthur
Palmer refused to give evidence on the ground of ill-

tive Council- though he is notoriously unfit to fill the

Ex-director A. B. Webster declared flatly that
Q. Govt, borrowed £600,000 in 1892 on the public security
“in the interest of the bank.” It was “not borrowed to

£251,461 in overdrafts (saying nothing of other advances),
to meet which there is now security valued at £60,700 1
The credit of the country mortgaged by the Treasurer to

Bank’s total loss, .£3,497,803, on a capital of

One of the strangest samples of opinion in the
great Queensland National Bank revelation is the ex-
planation publicly offered by Chief Justice Psalm Griffith.
Griffith was Premier in 1892. At that time the Q.N.B.
held a large amount of public money payable on demand,
and £600,000 of this was to be drawn out to pay interest
on the public debt. The bank informed the Griffilwraith
Government that it couldn’t produce the money—in
other words, that it was insolvent. Whereupon the
Griffith Ministry borrowed the money from the Bank of
England, and averted the smash. Griffith says that he
takes all the credit of this transaction. The credit (to be
divided among a number of people) pans out thus : The
Government knew the QN.B. to be unable to meet its
engagements in 1892. It kept dark about the matter,
and let the public go on putting its money in the rotten
concern. It left the public’s own money in it. It put a
lot more of the public’s money into it. Two Ministers, in
1893, declared the bank to be perfectly sound, and said
nothing about the 1892 act of insolvency. Quite a lot of
Ministers stood by and said nothing, while the bank con-
tinued to issue balance-sheets showing large profits, and
to declare dividends. Griffith also stood by and said
nothing. The board of directors and the management
knew that the Q.N.B. couldn’t pay its debts as far back as
1892, and they concealed the fact from the shareholders,
and kept on issuing prosperous-looking balance-sheets,
and paying dividends —and they also said nothing. And
Griffith says that he takes all the credit of initiating or
helping on this state of things. He is welcome to the
credit. The Bulletin wouldn’t deprive him of it for the
world. It wouldn’t have such a thing about the house.
The Q.N. Bank committee tries desperately hard
to let that awful business down easily. In 1888 the bank
launched out oh a huge scale; it lent out 2J millions in
- one year, and branch managers were “ advised that ap-
plications for advances would be favorably received m
other words, they were urged to hunt round for bor-
rowers, and not be too particular re the security. The
committee says, in excuse, that “ the money had to be
lent, for interest had to be paid on deposits.’ 15ut it
doesn’t once mention that the Q.N.B. made this necessity
by touting desperately for deposits. The report also
states that there is nothing against the personal character
of the directors. The only thing against them seems to
be that they were utterly neglectful of their business;
that they made the bank take up part of its own new
share issue, and tacitly deceived the public into the belief
that the new issue had all been taken up by outside in-
vestors, thereby presumably giving the shares a fictitious
value ; that they bought the bank’s own shares in the
market to keep up the price, and thereby gave it a bogus
aspect of solidity, evidently intended to deceive some-
body ; that some of them borrowed the bank’s money for
their own uses ; and a lot of other things.

Hunk of great joy from the report of the
Queensland National Bank committee: “The account
(of the Mount Perry Copper Co.) began with a transfer
of £5OO. ... On 19th February, 1883, the overdraft
stood at £4357. . . . Another transfer was made of
£BOB9 from the debit of Thomas MTlwraith to the debit
of the co. . . . The company’s account grew from
year to year until it was transferred to doubtful debt
account in December, 1893. But the Mount Perry Copper
Co. was amalgamated with the Reid’s Creek Gold Mining
Co. . . . The flotation was effected in London, but
the new co. did not take over the liabilities of the Mount
Perry Copper Co.” (it only took over the property and
left the bad debts to the Q.N. Bank) “ and the overdraft
remained. Moreover, the bank never received any scrip
in the new co., and it apparently allowed the Mount
Perry property to pass out of its hands without receiving-
anything whatever in exchange. Further, some consider-
able time afterwards, two cheques were drawn on the old
Mount Perry account by Thomas MTlwraith. One was
for £4OOO and the other £6OOO. ... It would thus
appear that not only did the bank gratuitously surrender
its property, but that it absolutely made a free gift of
£lO,OOO to those who took it.” In the face of these and
many other transactions equally mysterious it is hardly
wonderful that some people are suggesting—well, what is
it natural to suggest under the circumstances ?
• * » *
On every Queensland National Bank balance-
sheet which The Bulletin has in its possessi on _ an( j they
number a good many—the auditors solemnly declare that
they believe the document to be a true' statement of
affairs “as shown by the books.” Taken literally, these
words mean-as this paper has been pointing out at in-
tervals for years past—that if the books were right the
balance-sheet was right, and if the books were a lie and a
swindle the balance-sheet was exactly the same lie and
swindle. Consider the value of that kind of certificate !
Also of that kind of auditor !
* » * •
The Q.N. Bank report has roused many one-
time bitter enemies to acknowledge the wisdom of the
Victorian banking law which Turner and Isaacs shoved
through amid the accumulated curses of Pro putty. The
main points of the Victorian law are that it requires
a vast amount of detailed information to be given in the
balance-sheet, and that it fixes a cast-iron responsibility
on directors and auditors—whereas, in other provinces,
directors and auditors can get out of practically all res-
ponsibility simply by pleading that they grossly and
habitually neglected their business. Victoria and Maori-
land were the only provinces which learned anything
from the great,bank-smasb. In N.S.W. an attempt was
made to do something per medium of Dibits’* temporary
Banking Act, but even that has been allowed to lapse,
and the permanent Act which was promised shows no
sign of coming. N.S.W. is now back in the old groove,
and anybody without a penny can call himself a bank,
and issue bank-notes, and run riot in the old way. This
doesn’t matter so very much just now, for in this time of
depression there is no room for bogus banks. But, when
the boom comes again, there is nothing to prevent the
old fraud starting afresh as gaily as ever.
» * * *
Shortly after his return from Europe, Q.N.B.
Manager Drury—the Man that’s Dead—determined to
utilise a wrinkle he had picked up from Scotland Yard.
So he had a special electric-bell connection made between
the Roma-street police-barracks and the bank’s strong-
room, where all the cash was kept. Whenever that bell
rang the police-officer in charge had special instructions
to immediately communicate with his superior officer;
the S.O. with the Inspector; and the Inspector with
Police-Commissioner Seymour, who would at once advise
Manager Drury—so that, the bank premises having been
surrounded by armed police, the thief or thieves could be
without fail captured and locked up. And that bell
never rang ?
* * * *
The English, Scottish, and Australian Bank
reports by cable that there is a profit for the 12 months
ended June, 1897, of £10,870. Last year there was a loss
of £27,950, and for 1895 the loss was £16,432, so the pre-
sent year’s results are £38,820 better than those of 1896.
Unless another dry rot sets in, it would seem, therefore,
that the large saving effected by the reduction of deposit
interest at the second (1896) reconstruction has brought
the E.S. and A. Bank back to a state of safety. Apart
from this its business, which isn’t a very large one at the
best, shows signs of improvement. New deposits and
current accounts (£1,396,000) are £268,000 more,than
they were a year ago ; cash on hand (£851,000) is £136,000
better, and £460,000 of reconstructed deposits have been
paid off, or taken in satisfaction of debts, or otherwise
got rid of.
Where the very bad shock comes in is in the
news that another big writing-off of lost capital is essen-
tial. In 1893 the E.S. and A. Bank wrote off £450,000
(half its then paid-up capital), and all its reserves (about
£315,000) went the same road. The bank now sets aside
another £431,550 of capital to cover further depreciation
in the assets, so that this very moderate-sized institution
has now made provision for losses to the extent of
£1,200,000. All the capital and reserves existing before
the 1893 smash have gone overboard, and the E.S. and
A. stands on the same footing in this respect as the Com-
mercial of Australia. Considering that it was supposed
to have been more closely scrutinised in 1893 than any
other bank, and that its soundness was then alleged to be
something simply wonderful, this is hardly the sort of re-
sult which was anticipated. And the profits, after all
the saving effected by a large reduction of deposit in-
terest, are only equal to about 2 per cent, per annum on
the remaining capital—the capital which is alleged to be
perfectly safe and beyond all dispute. Under these cir-
cumstances the prospects of the shareholders—especially
as there is a definite obligation to establish a reserve-
fund before these long-suffering individuals get anything
—seem unspeakably poor. And if, by any chance, the
1897 writing-off proves no more final than the 1893 one
did, and some other painful accident happens about
1901
* * * «
The Bank of New South Wales has a profit of
£89,462 to show for the half-year ended 30th September,
as compared with £87,898 last half-year and £87,661 at
30th September, 1896. The usual 9 per cent, dividend
absorbs £87,750, leaving £1712 to carry forward. Out of
the profit-and-loss balance, £7650 is added to the reserve-
fund, bringing it up to the magnificent figure of £1,200,000.
The B.N.S.W. does the biggest and soundest business in
Australia, but eVen on it the aggravated depression of the
last 12 months is telling seriously. Cash on hand
(£5,307,000) is £1,400,000 less than it was a year ago. In
the same period, note circulation has decreased £15,000 ;
deposits have declined £515,000; bills payable have
dropped £455,000; and. discounts have diminished
£173,000.
* * « •
The Bank of Australasia has done a trifle better
than usual this half-year. The net profits are £45,375,
while for the last two half-years they were £40,399 and
£40,568 respectively. The ordinary 5 per cent, divi-
dend accounts for £40,000, leaving a balance of £6376
an event which hasn’t happened to the Australasia for
quite a long time. The business looks prosperous in
every respect, still for the past year the Australasia only
earned 3 >4 per cent, on the shareholders’ funds, which is
nothing specially magnificent.
In N.S.W., Victoria, and S.A. the crops, which
looked well up to a lew weeks ago, have gone down
badly. Drought, heat, and high winds struck them
simultaneously. In a great part of Victoria it now
appears that the farmers will get less out, of the ground
than they put into it. In N.S.W., though things are
better, some 2,000,000 bushels have been taken off the
estimated crop in two weeks ; and S.A. is almost as bad
as Victoria. And this is the third bad failure in three
years. Young man, go on the land !

Faith in experts still lives. A certain Austra-
lian meat co. employed one to reduce wages and make
divs. By-and-bv something about midnight burials
•leaked out. The expert left, and the directors of
the co. set men digging in a sandy spot. In half-a-
day they unearthed 40,000 tins of preserved beef, 20,000
of which were 61b, tins. At last advices they were still
digging and getting good prospects. The co, wonders
with a cold perspiration on its skin whether the meat
that reached the European market is preserved like that
found in the sand and, if so— —? ? ?

With reference to a paragraph which appeared
in The Bulletin of 13th inst. re the new Speaker of the
Vic. Assembly, this paper was in error in stating that Mr.
Mason had been a “ director” in the Provincial and Sub-
urban Bank in the 70’s. He never occupied any position
in connection with that institution, but the trustees of his
wife’s estate invested a sum of £lO,OOO in the then mush-
room venture, nearly the whole of which was lost, to the
misfortune of Mr. and Mrs. Mason and family, for whom
great sympathy was expressed at the time.

The record opal-find for White Cliffs (N.S.W.)
was made the other day—a 270 z. piece. But it was of
poor quality, though saleable at £245. The next biggest
piece was found four years ago. It weighed 130 z., but
was all tip-top stuff. The opal market has lately been
very flat.

The Tuesday after the Lucknow strike was
declared “ off ” Peter Phillips, secretary of Bendigo
miners, wired to the Lucknow secretary reporting that
manager Lock’s agent was still engaging men there at
7s. 6di per shift. And Jaeger, secretary of the strike
committee, replied : “ Strike declared off. Splendid win.
Hundreds practical miners unemployed here. Advise
men not to come.”

The owners of the Paddy Lackey mine, N.S.W.
—a mine which no one condemns and which no one seems
game to buy—are now going down with their new shaft.
The Paddy Lackey Deep Sinking co. has also begun a
shaft on the boundary to pick up the lode on the under-
lay. Possibly they might strike it at 2000 ft. Far more
likely they will never get down half as far.

Once more the Rockvale mine, New England
district (N.S.W.), has been sold—this time to an English
co. represented by Dr. Schnedel. The writer wishes the
buyers luck with it. No doubt the price paid for mine
and plant is small as compared with the money lost on it
during the last six years.

The brottiers Baker, sons of Ezekiel Baker,
better known as “ Betsy,” who while Minister for Mines,
N.S.W., got into a row over the Milburn Creek copper
mine, have, it is said, sold their silver mine in the New
England district, after taking some £lO,OOO w’orth of rich
ore out of it, at a big profit. It is an antimonial sulphide,
principally in quartz ; the spot was known to more than
one man for years, but kept dark pending the passing of
a Mining on P.P. Act. The Bakers made terms with the
owners of the freehold and have now, by all accounts,
made big terms with some Bull buyers. In the same
locality, some five years ago, Blue-Ribbon-Gospel-Tem-
perance Booth and some very smart Yankee canvassers;
created quite a boom by comparing these small, erratic
and patchy silver veins to the Comstock lode, and a few
Melb. men, believing the “yabber,” fell in heavily.

What’s wolfram worth ? Well (writes a Bulle-
tin correspondent), I’ve just had a quotation from Black-
well, Sons, and Co., Liverpool, about the biggest mer-
chants in this line, mentioning £25 per ton. But as much
as £35 has been quoted—this for, say, 70 per cent, stuff.
As you say, the trouble is to find a buyer who isn’t run-
ning his own mines. , That is at the bottom of the troubU
with most foreign metal merchants arid brokers.

Klondyke fever has already broken out down
South amongst a number of adventurous miners who
have a bit to lose, and, in anticipation of the epidemia
spreading, a steamer has already been advertised to leave
Melbourne for the alleged Eldorado in time for the Spring
rush.
* * » •
The Maldon mining-lease scandal, referred to
by Tub Bulletin some weeks ago, only found Parlia-
mentary publicity, in Vic. Leg. Assembly, on 16th inst.
The restrictions placed by the Vic. Premier upon the
scope to be allowed the Select Committee of Enquiry
are most unfavorably commented upon in the best
informed mining circles. Considering that the scandal
has been public property for close on two months, and
that it is known that very powerful influence was
exercised to squash the matter, even before then, there
must be something in the Vic. Premier’s insistence upon
the enquiry not only being limited to the question as to
how Mr. R. B. Stamp got his very valuable and very, early
information, but in his (the Premier’s) restriction as to
the enquiry not being made as a “ hostile move against
the Govt.” Whatever the result of the Select Commit-
tee’s enquiry may be, those “in the know” down South
can already—so they say—safely lay odds on the man who
will take “ the Scapegoat Stakes.”

* * * *
A pretty frank statement re the Mt. Deddiek
(Gipps.) show has been published in Melb. Argus.
None of the prospecting shafts have gone.deeper than
50ft., and, “as the country is somewhat unsettled, it is
probable that another 50ft. will have to be sunk before
the permanence of the field will be .thoroughly demon-
strated.” Average galena assays reveal lead 7o per cent.,
silver 250 z. per ton ; gold 2dwt. per ton ; copper 1-6 per
cent., but the report is silent as to the quantity of stuff
put to assay thusfar. The Government is cutting a track
from Gippsland Lake Entrance to the Deddiek field, a dis-
tance of 70 miles, and, when the track is completed,
“ about the middle of December,” it should be possible to
raise, cart and treat the ore at a cost of “ not more than
£5 per ton.” This friendly estimate of working exes,
will make Deddiek shareholders very anxious for the per-
manence of the field to be demonstrated. Also, it will
need to be demonstrated beyond any possible doubt what-
ever before the Government will speculate in a Deddiek
railway or tramline at a roughly-estimated cost of
£TOO,UOO.

• * * *
The Towers has given Bull some good things,
some indifferent, and likewise some dreadful failures.
The New Charters Towers Gold Mines co., which em-
braces nearly the whole of Towers Hill in a tunnelling
area, is not the least of the latter. Bull is evidently get-
ting sick of paying the piper to no purpose, as the co. lately
got three months total exemption. The English investor
is never a good prospector ; he only holds on to his scrip
when divs. are rolling in. When the mine is call-paying
he passes. A strike in this co.’s mine, owing to a certain
little trouble, was heard very little of locally.

WATSON’S WHISKY.
COXIETY
S.M. Herald takes pains to say, with re-
gard to “extraordinary rumours about a divorce-
suit said to be pending, that no such suit has, so
far, come under the cognisance of the court
officials.” Which is all very well in its way, but the
mere fact of such rumours having arisen goes to
show that so long as the law is a respecter of per-
sons—so long as it allows privileged people to
obtain divorces privately—even the highest per-
sonages in the community will be liable to the
suspicion of being concerned in scandals which
do not come in the ordinary way under public
observation. The less smoke people see, the
hotter they’ll suspect the fire is. Verily the pri-
vileged.-private-divorce-before-8 a.rn. business has
come home to roost.
Re a recent Bulletin par. The Governor
of a certain Australian prison once lost the key of
his private safe, and sent for an expert burglar
then under his charge to open it. The prisoner,
learning what was required of him, stoutly refused,
on the ground that he was serving a sentence for
picking a safe-lock, and that what he couldn’t do
legally out of gaol he was not going to do in it!
The Governor, being told this, went himself to
interview the recalcitrant confinee, and was horri-
fied to recognise in the ex-burglar a nephew, whom
he had not seen for 12 years !
* * * *
Extraordinary efforts are being made down
South by Wesleyan parsons to get comely single
ladies for the Chinese mission-field. So far as
The Bulletin can gather, the principal induce-
ments offered are a free passage out to China and
a martyr’s crown in Heaven. No salary is given
for the first 12 months, but the ladies who are
lucky enough to escape the coronation ceremony
during the first year will receive a small yearly
allowance towards the expenses of saving their
vekins. A really “ fat ” job, truly. S
* * * *
In a recently-published “ Peerage ” occur
these two lines, in succession :
Title. Created. Heir.
Hardwicke ... 1754 ... Viscount Royston
Hare wood ... 1812 ... Viscount Lascelles.
In the early ’4o’s, an individual known as “Vis-
count Lascelles,” who claimed to have been de-
puted by the British Government to enquire into
the condition of the convict-population, swindled a
numberjof persons out West (Molong and Orange).
He was sentenced to seven years. And now the
soi-disant Earl of Hardwicke, who had been stay-
ing in the same district, is before N.S. W. Supreme
Court for alleged false pretences.

Despairing of getting people to attend the
old-fashioned church service, W. Maitland
(N.S.W.) Wesleyans got up a sacred orchestra of
20 performers and trained 200 children to sing in
chorus. Now the church isn’t big enough to hold
the crowds that attend the Sunday matinees and
evening concerts. “ Where does the religion
come in ? ” ask troubled ancient hardshells. And
the committee points triumphantly to two new
collection-plates.

* * • »
N. : “I have exceptional opportunities
for studying Chinese half-castes, especially on the
girl side. No case has come under my observa-
tion in which a legally-married Chinese proved an
unkind husband. But the girly offspring always
grows up with at least a tinge of melancholia.
This is through the cruelty of circumstances. The
Ailivey or orangey young women are educated well
as Australiennes, and will not think of Chinese
for spouses, but their grief is that the young
whites fight so shy. The yellow aroma is not
dirt. The poor girls almost live in the hot bath,
but scrub, break and shatter the vase as you will,
the scent of the rose is over it still.
Dear B., — “The Bulletin wanted a cor.
to explain the other day, if he could, the alleged
‘natural antipathy ’ of the Australian white woman
to the Chow. It isn’t a local failing that there is
no marked antipathy at all. In Safrica (thanks
for that: may we use it this side?) it might,
perhaps, be thought that the Kaffir is treated with
as much contempt by white women as he is by
white men. Nothing of the kind, though ; and
woman’s indiscretion in the matter is responsible
for a good many of the frequent outrages reported
from that quarter, for the Kaffir’s blood is warmei
than the Chow’s, and his methods dissimilar in
other respects.”—P.

Rev. E. Walton, a Tas. delegate to Yic.
Baptist Union, thinks the unions in all the pro-
vinces should have a uniform method of admitting
candidates for orders, so as to keep out of the
ministry mental incapables, wily adventurers,
habitual drunkards, and immoral men of other
descriptions. He proposes that candidates should
be required to pass an educational examination
and supply references as to character. If, as is to
be naturally inferred, this hasn’t hitherto been the
case, much of the milk in the clerical cocoa-nut is
easily accounted for.

* • * *
Ed. Bulletin, —Sydney police vigilance
continues vigorously to wink the other eye. A
small suburban tobacconist was fined the other
day for keeping open upon the very same Sunday
on which I twice bought cigars at night in a city
main-street. Also, in a city oyster-shop I had
stout with my oysters, and about 10 other people
publicly drank beer in the same establishment.
No concealment whatsoever about the matter.
Further, there are in two Sydney main streets at
least 40 gambling-shops in full public blast, into
and out of which flows all day long a perfect tide
of men, women and boys. But the Law can’t see
them. It is looking out too hard for the little
suburban shopkeeper who strikes a desperate
blow at public morality by passing sixpenn’orth of
smoke over an ungodly Sawbath counter. —J,
Calvin Wesley Knox-Brunker.

A sweetly-romantic marriage story was
told in Melb. Divorce-court last week by a deserted
wife. She had been acquainted with her husband
for some years before they got joined in wedlock
by the obliging Kinsman. She paid Revd.
Nathaniel K.’s fee at her husband’s request, and
then the happy pair walked off to the bride’s lodg-
ings. They were now alone together for the first
time as man and wife, and the husband promptly
confessed his anxiety to make a nest for his birdie.
He had seen a suitable villa, and was prepared t<
HE WAS QUITE SATISFIED.
JIM: “ How are yer doing, Bill!”
BILL (with baby) : “ Oh, just holding me own!'
buy it right away—with the bride’s savings. But
she said “No, I will give no man my money.”
Whereupon the bridegroom gathered up his hat
went, and never to return. There was no
cohabitation between the parties, no apparent
love, no “ scene,” no anything beyond a quiet
Kinsman marriage, and a little misunderstanding
as regards Cash. He thought that a woman eager
to get married to a homeless suitor would be wil-
ling to purchase some connubial endearments on ,
his own terms. He was a working tailor, and
snips are good judges of human nature, as a rule.
They sit, and stitch, and think a lot, yet even the
deepest thinker may err in his calculations.
Moreover, the tailor in this case was dealing with
a self-supporting charmer, who, when she found
herself forsaken, sought a billet as barmaid m
Westralia.
SARCASM.
BIOGBILL THE BARMAN: “ Wot! cadgin’ for drinks agen, are yer? If you
don’t take yer hook quick I’ll crack yer jaw with this bloomin’ jug.
Clear!”
THE BEERY: “ Well, blime, Bill, you are sarcastic.”
The Sydney public appears to be slowly
awaking to the fact that it is quite as likely to get
its daily tuberculosis from the poultry-farm as
from the shambles or the dairy. The evil-colored
flesh (and still-more-evil odor after cooking) of the
ordinary shop-fowl is owing to the fact that its
diet has been mostly butcher’s offal and similar
abominations. And “the succulence” of roast
duck brings with it horrible suggestions of the
recently-related Bulletin instance of the man
who, while skinning a defunct Newfoundland
dog, cheerfully informed an inquirer, “Oh, we
just feed such things to the ducks ! ” The trickery
born of throat-cutting competition, in fact, is fast
relieving us of all our beautiful illusions about
“pure country milk ” and “dairy-fed pork” and
“maize-meal ducklings ” and sweet-fleshed pullets
reared in Arcadian innocence of garbage. Even
the blind bliss of ignorance is being taken from us
day by day.
* • » *
Members of the Permanent Artillery at
Auckland are retiring in indignation at being
compelled to wear their uniform whilst off duty.
The fact is, these modern defenders of un-
threatened shores begin to feel ashamed of their
clothes.
AKNICKERBOCKER BCALLAD
She mounts her
American wheel,
With a hop full of
dexterous grace;
There’s a glimpse of
an ankle and heel,
And a vision of
petticoat lace.
Then she spins down
the road at a
pace,
Quite a modest and
beautiful sight,
Of boldness not even a
trace—
But she pedals in knickers at night l
This secret she’ll sometimes reveal
With the daintiest blush on her face.
“ What responsible functions," you feel
“Devolve upon button and brace!"
In the daytime the skirt is in place,
But, she says, in the uncertain light
It alters the state of the case—
So she pedals in knickers at night.
At a period more mystic
than real,
In a country that borders
on Thrace,
To settle a lover’s appeal
Atalanta once ran in a
race.
Milarion pi evaded in
the chase:
Her form was not up to
his flight—
He might never have
won her embrace
Had she pedalled in
knickers at night.
ENVOY.
Dear lady! Time cannot efface
The vision that haunteth this wight—
Of her, sprinting forth into space,
When she pedalled in knickers at night.
Melb.
D. BOUVERIE.
CUPID’S NOSE.
“Especially in regard to—let us call it Love the
powerful influence of the olfactory nerves has never been
dulv appreciated. A vast percentage of human beings
fall in love through the nose.” —V. Kotze in Bulletin,
Nov. 13.
By tilt' south sea silvered in starry sheen
She declared she’d ever be true—
He had recently chewed up a coffee-bean
And devoured a pink cachou ;
And he whispered, “ Dear heart, by the stars above,
“ We’ll taste all the sweets of an Austral love 1 ”
And he sniffed with joy in her warm embrace,
For she gave him back scent for scent—
Her blouse and her bang and her very face
With perfume were redolent;
And she whispered, “Dear hoy, by the stars above,
“ We’ll taste all the sweets of an Austral love ! ”
And the calm, omniscient moon looked on
At the spooning of man and maid,
And she thought with a sigh when the pair had gone
To hide in the ti-tree shade—
“ It’s the old, old story ! By ‘ stars above,’
They’re smelling the sweets of a nostril love ! ”

BRISBANE, QUEENSLAND.
THE CHIVALROUS MALE.
LEGEND (in both oases): “ 'Urry up, Liza —'ow you women do drag!”
Gov. Brassey, following Excellency Hope-
town’s example, has been “ doing ” the wilds of
Gippsland with a large camp-following consisting
mainly of devoted press representatives and atten-
tive persons with cameras, the latter of whom
hung on to Uncle Tom with dog-like pertinacity,
and “ took him ” whenever opportunity offered.
The heavy weeklies of Melbourne—the sustained
dulness and sameness of whose illustrations make
them as inspiring, week after week, as the passing
of 50-million weary sheep in a narrow way—come
out religiously with pictui-es of Brassey leaning
against a tree, Brassey leaning on a fenoe, Brassey
on a horse in the bush, Brassey on a horse in a
township, Brassey here, there, and everywhere,
doing this, that and the other, but always, always
the same stout, solid, depressing Brassey. Still,
The Bulletin is sorry for his Ex. This compara-
tively harmless old gentleman is hunted through
the country by an army of rapacious cameras so
that he dare not scratch his ear for fear of imperil-
ing the stability of the empire by being made to
appear ridiculous in a public print, whereas a
Butler is simply hanged for his sins.
• • * *
Governor Brassey, or the young men re-
sponsible for supplying pressmen with the names
of guests at “State” functions, might take a hint
from the procedure at Sydney Govt. House, where
the cards are always made up in alphabetical
■order, whilst typewritten lists are in some cases
got ready for the reporters. The principal worry
is the “sassiety” hen-and-chickens who hand in
three or four of their cards so as to ensure news-
paper mention —and these are just the ladies who
get left out “ by accident,” and who sail round in
a rage next day to demand the instant dismissal of
the scribe.
* * * *
0. H. Nicolson, P.M., before the Vic. Law
Reform Commission: “I am against whipping
altogether. My experience leads me to the con-
clusion that flogging entirely fails as a corrective
or a deterrent.” But the P.M. recommends in
place of the cat “special dietary treatment.”
Convicts, he said, would rather be flogged, than
put upon low diet. Mr. Nicolson’s reform is not
very drastic after all—he would substitute rats in
the stomach for cat on the back. Certainly his
form of corporal punishment is the most philo-
sophical. Sinfulness goes with a high stomach.
But against this there is the experience of an
intelligent leading Salvationist: “We rarely keep
the converts who come to us with empty stomachs. ”

Apropos of the recent sensational escape
of a long-sentence prisoner from Lyttelton (M.L.)
gaol. Every day a gang of perhaps 100 convicts
employed on Govt, works traverses the streets of
that peaceful port in charge of a handful of war-
ders. If ever these men see fit to “ run amok ”
there will be a memorable scatter in the town of
Lyttelton. Also, in most M.L. towns supplies
THIS HOT WEATHER.
“ Waiter, pass the sugar and flies.”
are wheeled to the gaols in handcarts drawn by
two or more prisoners under the supervision of an
armed warder—rather a cruel way of “ rubbing it
in ” to men who may possibly be known to half
the inhabitants.

Dear B., — He clergymen acting as adver-
tising mediums for bootmakers, there’s a clock in
Melb. St. Patrick’s Cathedral which bears in bold
characters on its dial the donor’s name and ad-
dress, which suggests a regular bonanza for
poverty-stricken clergymen. To make a church
self-supporting, why not let out the interior to
advertising-agents, and then, during the sermon,
the congregation could inform its mind about
the virtues of Jones’s corn - plaster, Brown’s
Bavarian bolus, and so on. In no place is the
mind in greater need of a little wholesome distrac-
tion than in church, and, as for profanity, we’d
never hear a word about that once the parsons
caught on to the solid oof there is in the business.
—J.E.D.
• * * •
The Lucknow police have emerged triumph-
ant from the official investigation into the criminal
charges levelled against them by the peripatetic
Rev. Isitt. Public record of the result of the
investigation is due to the maligned bobbies. But
something more is due. It is understood that one
of them has applied for leave to prosecute Isitt for
defamation, and that his application has been
refused. Why should a public officer who feels he
has been defamed and desires an ordinary citizen’s
form of redress be officially choked into silence ?
* * * *
The body of a sleeper-cutter named Haw-
thorne, who went amissing some weeks ago, has
just been dug up in the Hutt Valley, Maoriland.
There was the usual bullet-hole in the back of the
head, and the usual mate has been arrested. This
makes the third mate arrested within a year,
Butler and Josh. Beard being the other two. The
property of the deceased, which was only worth a
few pounds in all, is said to have been found in
the mate’s possession. The grave, according b-
the telegraphed report, was too small, and tin,
body showed signs of having been doubled up and
jumped upon to make it fit in. Which is
reminiscent of Butler, who also dug his graves
too small, and had to double up his departed
friend to make him fit in. Joshua Beard was
apparently the only mate who took sufficient time,
and dug his graves large enough, and planted the
deceased in a comfortable attitude. The Bulle-
tin begins to wonder how widespread this mate
business may be. As you go along the roads of
Australia you generally meet the swagman either
singly or in twos —hardly ever in threes. Is the
lone swagman always the mate coming back from
the funeral ?

is on the side of the sensible
clergy and laymen, who want
to knock off this anti-Malthu-

In Melb. divorce-court :
“ Why did your husband leave
you?” “Only because he was
tired of me. He said five years
was long enough to live with
any woman.” Yes, sir; six
months to find out her virtues,
fifty-four to discover her faults.

At the Cue, Murchison (W.A.), races, a
willy-willy (whirlwind) came along in a line with
the grand-stand, the roof of which was composed
of hessian. Some bookies called out “Two to
one the roof goes ! ” Nobody took them up; bub
the willy took the roof clean off, and left the ladies
scorching in the sun.
The Bulletin often receives this kind ol
thing :
The science of surgery doesn’t seem to make much
headway in Melb. Hospital. This case I know to be a fact.
A lad with a diseased shinbone lay there for over a year.
Every month the bone was bored, burnt, scraped. At
last it was decided to take the limb off. The parents
objected, and took him home and cured him with onion-
poultices, and he can now “ whip behind ” a ’bus with the
best of ’em.—K.
Now, what is the doctors’ version ?

THE HATTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The stuff is poor, the creek is dry,
My luck is up a tree ;
But —twioe a day she passes by
And smiles, and nods to me.
I know the cursed claim won’t pay—
It never paid before,
And never will; and yet I stay
Just working for the store.
’Tis nothing but a tucker show,
Like all the rest up here ;
I should have slung it long ago,
But—somehow —l can’t clear.
Each washing-up, right from the start,
I swore should be the last;
But ere I saddled up to part
Another month had passed.
I wonder when I’ll say good-bye—
If ever that will be—
For twice a day she passes by
And smiles, and nods to me.
V ON KOTZE.
A Bush Lyrist—Will H. Ogilvie.
This is a photograph of Will. H. Ogilvie—
otherwise “ Glenrowan ” —whose many verses pub-
lished in The Bulletin (and presently to be col-
lected in book - form) stamp him equal to the
best of our bush bards. In one of his poems Mr.
Ogilvie charmingly toasts “ fair girls and gray
horses,” and girls and horses are his staple themes.
In verse often finely phrased, and always touched
with tender sentiment, he has embalmed many of
the romantic moods of a bush horseman ; and his
book has a wide popularity assured. Mr. Ogilvie
was born near Kelso, Scotland, in 18C9 ; and came
in ’B9—with all the dreams of tingling twenty hot
in him—to Belalie station, on the Warrego, to gain
“'‘olonial experience.” Thence he travelled to
Maaoupe station (Penola, S.A.) as overseer ; and
then came overland to Bourke. Latterly he has
circulated more or less freely in a district bounded
by the Coorong, Cunnamulla, Coonamble, and
Condobolin—playing the parts of drover, horse-
breaker, rouseabout, and gentleman -at-large. He
has learned to passionately love the Bush—he
always loved a good horse ; and since adult-age he
has had a discriminating regard for the best brands
of Scotch whisky. And here, on the heels of this
notice, comes Mr. Ogilvie’s last poem—
WHEN HORSES ARE SADDLED FOR LOVE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
■ The saddle-slaves of Love are vve
Who mount by sun and moon,
No matter what the se son be
So long as it be soon.
The golden and the gray light
Have seen the girth-straps drawn
For love that rules the daylight,
The dark and dusk and dawn.
What hoof-beat on the gravel!
What haste with Love to be 1
What snatching at the snaffle !
What reefing-, head to knee !
Now faster still and faster —
The white moon laughs above —
She knows we have no master
Except the Lord of Love..
The low road keeps the river.
The high road skirts the hill—
No road so short but ever
We find a shorter still ;
And if the floods run blindly
Where love, not life, ’s the loss,
Dame Fortune treats us kindly
And holds our hands across.
The bush-wind blows to meet us
As though she understands,
The hop-bush holds to greet us
A hundred clasping hands ;
There’s not a bird but sings us
A welcome in the grove,
They know ’tis Lov’e that brings us,
And all the world loves Love.
Be skies a-light or leaden
Long miles bring no regret,
And if the white spurs redden
Our horses soon forget;
So toss the bars, my beauty,
And cream the reins with foam ;
It’s ten moon-miles to duty,
And ten more dawn-miles home !
Gleam lights in the verandah,
Flash lamps across the lawn ;
But soft the shadows yonder
Where reins are tightly drawn.
Gut there the dews are glistening,
The leaves are scarcely stirred,
So close the Night-Wind’s listening
To every whispered word.
The moon she dips to morning-,
The lamps are burning low,
Gur love belated scorning—
“ One kiss before I go !”
Now slowly- through the starlight,
Slow, slow, in dreams away,
Till eastward gleams the far light
That leads the breaking day.'
WILL. H. OCILYIE.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, November 23, 1897.
Mr Dear Myee—
The Dante Society made its second bid for fame
on Thursday evening, in Y.M.C.A. Hall, when a
feminine and floppy gathering listened to a dis-
course from the fluent Prof. Butler on the ancient
Italian poet whose name patronises the society.
A lengthy excursus on the poems showed that
Dante’s world-renowned imagination broke down
badly in the department of horrors. An ice-cold
Inferno would be a pleasant change of climate
after last week’s scorchers. The President made
a few remarks in Italian, and after the piano had
been undressed, which took some time, there was
a little music. Miss Florence Mack warbled
sweetly, in a white frock.
Last Friday was a night of alarms for the un-
fortunates dwelling within earshot of the theatres.
The railway collision in “Man for Man,” at the
Royal, gave the neighborhood its shock all right
and regular at about 9.30. A while later, volleys
of musketry sounding through the stilly night
announced that the Indians in the wild and woolly
West at the Lyceum were having their little
funeral for the last time during the run of the
“Prairie King.” These crashes were ordinary
alarms, but about midnight loud and unexpected
explosions aroused sleepers, with the idea that the
comet had struck the earth at last. But it was
only a belated rehearsal of the “ Royal Divorce,”
and they were testing the cannon of Waterloo.
Smart drapery windows are very gay with
colored hosiery. After a long reign of black, in
which everything fanciful was tabooed except a
tiny pin’s-head dab of color, or a kind of meat-
safe ventilation about the ankles, stockings now
look like the flags of all nations, and the correctly-
dressed woman can’t have them too gay. Tartan
hose are worn by daring young persons who like
to revel in the newest styles; but the most popular
stocking of the moment has a black foundation
and any amount of gaudy pattern on it. Colored
shoes demand the same hue in hosiery. Men’s
shoe-shop windows exhibit green leather boots,
but up to now I haven’t seen the man courageous
enough to sport a pair. To match this freak of
fashion, a bilious hue is thrown over men’s mercery
generally ; verdant socks and shirts and under-
garments now ornament nearly all the toney win-
dows. It is the bicycle that has resuscitated
gaily-colored hose. They're so much more seen
now.
The d.c. on the first night of the “Royal
Divorce” was crowded and fussy and frilly and
behind-time, and subsided into its seats with much
pomp and flutter and cackle; so it was a tho-
roughly high-toned audience. There was a fringe
hanging by its elbows to the back-ledge of the
circle, and stalls were reserved to the remotest
corners. The “ Divorce ”is a seasonable topic to
stage just now. The stage-folk, like tactless
callers who will talk about ropes to the family of
a hanged man, would keep harping on the subject.
Many bejewelled hands must have itched to spank
the little King of Rome when he artlessly asked
“What is divorce?” There was a “sensation”
at the Question, as if the d.c. were going to rise in
a body and enlighten his young mind. A shortly-
to-be-wed couple, Mr. Brunton and Miss Thorne,
were in the front seats, near a fair matron about
to depart for ’Ome, who has had as many “ farewell
appearances ” as a hungering pro. Mrs. Maes-
more Morris, in white, proved the assertion, often
made, that she looks better off than on the stage.
Miss E. Hughes was barred-in with black over
pink. Gloves were rare. It is the much be-ringed
woman’s day out now, as she gets her chance to
show her jewellery since Mrs. Grundy allowed
gloveless hands at theatres. I
Josephine gets most of the show in the Divorce.
Miss Eerrar trails her oft-described gown with
grace and ease. Julius Nap seems pale and wan
and is quite Napoleonic, even to the little curl in
the middle of his forehead, and lie is so careful of
his pose that he doesn’t remove his hand from his
chest even when he hugs Josephine. And the
hugs seem merely official. All the kissing in the
piece—there isn’t much—is loud and smacking.
Stage-kissing— i.e. , make-believe—always is noisy;
m real life nobody ever kisses with a sounding
bang. Miss Ferrar’s most realistic clothes are her
out-door suit of velvet, with a quaint grey hat
like an upside-down saucepan gushing feathers
at one side. Miss L. Raymond gets inside her
last-century dresses very convincingly, especially
her redmgote with plentiful fur-trimmings of
wile rabby.” Gaston Mervale lurks between
high bottle-green collars, and mutters about “ the
Emprah. . Elliott-Page, as the commonplace
Mane Louise, underacts consistently, just as the
original did. The Hapsburg lady never rose to
the occasion once. Josephine, though not born in
the purple, was fitted in the role of Empress
down to the ground.” The second Mrs. Bona-
parte had the chance of filling the eyes of Europe
as the fallen eagle’s widow. Instead, she married
an insignificant German person, and filled her
nursery with a large, stolid family, and was
stodgily happy and comfortable.
Napoleon’s young son, in the play, is correctly
clad m the garb of the period-a heavy velvet
frock with a raised pie of the same on his cork-
screw curls for a hat.« He looks like Tommy Good-
boy or a Maria Edgeworth hero. Compare the
construction of toggery of the “ Harry and Lucy ”
era with the easy breezy overalls and man-o’-war
suits of to-day. Wasn’t it in “ The Purple Jar ”
that, the priggish papa reproved his little daughter
for showing her ankles when she tumbled dovvn-
stairs Her ankles ! And now youngsters are
taught skirt-dancmg, and proud parents in the
stalls delightedly watch their darlings high-kick-
lng on the stage. And clothes are free and flow-
ing to match. The “Fauntleroy ” adjuncts of
wide sash-ribbon, flowing curls, and eavalierish
nop-hat till the eye much more picturesquely than
th» child-clothing of the First Empire.
Influences of Cora Urquharfc P. are traceable in
the garmenture of the new Rignold leading lady,
Miss Lilian Wheeler, who is tall and fair, but
stoops and totters somewhat on the boards. It
would do the persecuted heroines of melodrama
all the good in the world to put them through.a
dacily course of goose-step drill. In “ Man to
Man’’Miss W. looked willowy in her wedding-
dress of white, cut low, and topped by a vast
bridal-canopy-like hat. She was most I’otteresque
in her wingy black cape and large hat. As a
beauty, she will never come to the front rank till
she gets her hair right. She hasn’t got it to quite
the correct shade yet—her “ Man to Man ” wig
was too dead. A gleam of copper must warm the
fashionable stage coiffure of tho moment. The
late season at the Royal brought forward two or
three personable stage-struck damsels, who flut-
tered behind the footlights briefly and prettily as
“ bridesmaids ” and other accessories, and then
retired disappointedly into private lifo again.
Towards the end of their show, the Society of
Artists gave a swagger evening party to all the
political big-wigs, with music and an elabo-
rate supper. And alas ! when the art vote came
on for discussion in the House it was early morn,
and most members slumbered and slept on the
benches, and the vote wasn’t “ split ” between the
rival societies as the seceders wished. Either the
supper or the painting was too much for hon.
members, for nothing came of the conversazione.
A musical girly family in Sydney possesses a
cat which they thought a gentleman cat, and
called him “Mark Hambourg ” —“Marky” for
short —until one day “Marky’’ electrified them
by exhibiting a large and healthy family of
kittens. Then, of course, they had to change
pussy’s name. So they fixed on “ Marchesi,’’ and
still call their pet “ Marky ” for short.
The Sydney Brush Club, aged nine years and
six months, is defunct. At Paris House, Nov.
17th, Mr. L. Hopkins delivered an impressive
funeral - oration in the presence of about 50
mourners, and spoke feelingly of the long and
useful life spent by the club. As coroner, he
elicited the fact that the deceased had, some three
years ago, suffered from an acute attack of two
societies, from which it never thoroughly recovered.
Lately it had been despondent, and, as various
papers and acts showed that it was tired of life,
the jury, through their foreman (Souter), returned
a verdict of suicide whilst of unsound mind.
“ liinks, the Photographer,” is a feeble little
play that one would like to write about in a faint
whisper ; loud remark arid crash is out altogether.
The one brilliant idea in it is that Addie Conyers
is cast—though quite irrelevantly—in a part that
enables her t.o show her figure. Addie’s figure is
always a thing of beauty. She also wears a leg-
horn hat which curves satisfactorily under sprays
of geranium. Apart from A. Conyers, the drama
is chiefly domestic, with lucid intervals of accor-
cteon-pleating and sash—yards of sash fluttering
all through Act 11. The ballet is tied up in blue
ribbon, and not a girl in the cast but has a soul-
and-body lashing of some kind. Miss Nash has
slim white shoes and three rampant feathers, and
Amy Gourlay locks neat as a French maid of the
" Oui oui ’’ type. The piece hasn’t much inci-
dent : “ Binks ” has a most uneventful career, the
climax of the play being twins—and even the twins
happen “off.”
Theosophy has put spiritism out of the running
with the soulful of society, who must have a mind-
fad. Spooks, as such, are played out, but they
are now served up in esoteric form, “ another
way, as the cookery books say. A foreign lec-
turer, now in Sydney, used to aid in arranging the
Cissy-and-Geordie visits, but has cropped up again
as a humble disciple at the feet of the masters,”
and preaches mind-cure. Two leading adherents
of the new mode of healing disease by imagining
that you haven’t got it, are the wife and sister of
a daily-paper editor. The craze leaped into popu-
larity with .women soon after the arrival of a
youthful prophet with blue eyes and a fat smile.
1 he betting at one time was six to four on the
chance of a wedding coming off with the above
healer as a bridegroom ; but the lady is now lec-
turing in another province, and backers are doubt-
ful.
Afternoon tea-and-scandal coteries are still
brightened by the mysterious and much-hinted-at
divorce-case involving high and mighty potentates
which obstinately refuses to come on. The topic
has rushed onward like an incoming tide, succes-
sive waves being—first, that there was nothing in
it; next, that there must be something in it ;
then it really is all smoke (what a pity !); lastly’
that the alleged injured husband is coming out
per mail steamer, bristling with writs, and is pre-
pared to blue-paper all scandal-spreaders. This
douche has hosed the spreading flames into oblivion
and now everybody is saying that rumors one and
all owed their origin to a certain spiteful club-
man.
The Govt. House party is scattered abroad. On
Monday Gov. Hampden, accompanied by daughter
Dorothy, Miss K. Darley, and Capt. Fielden
sailed for Maoriland. Aide Sloane-Stanley has
departed for the North, and Capt. and Mrs. Fer-
guson have gone to the country. Lately His Ex.
has looked dejected and worried, and is probably
heartily shaking hands with himself at getting a
prolonged spell. Sad to say, His Excellency is
and has long been a martyr to Job’s prosaic com-
plaint. Her absentee Ex. will not return from
England till vvell on into the autumn. Of all the
family the pair who seem to be having the best
time are Capt. and Mrs. Ferguson;- One of their
great amusements is to go off together for the day
in a buggy with a billy and some books, and have
a restful picnic in some quiet nook. The Darley
vice-regal regime, which is now on, isn’t likely to
rouse local sassiety from its annual summer slum-
bers. C.J. Darley and wife take the deputyship
very seriously, and don’t popularise the billet like
the Vic. over-Acting-Gov. Madden, who, although
he plays to the gallery all the time, always
manages to make things hum socially.
Ranji-worship fell as flat as a game on a dead
wicket. The foreign prince wasn’t taking any
adoration; is said to be engaged at ’Ome, and,
apparently, though Eastern, can’t love more than
one at a time. Fellow travellers of his declare
their shipmate to be cold and coy and given to
reading in quiet corners. He wouldn’t play with
lady-passengers. Altogether, as a sassiety lion he
is a thorough frost.
Fashionable hair-dressing is still aspiring, and
the very latest knob sits fair on top of the
head. It will rejoice possessors of scanty locks
to know that the knob can be built gracefully up
by a series of puffs, separating the back-hair
into strands, twining them into sausage-rolls on
the tvvo fore-fingers. Abundant tresses are coiled
into shape. The absurd little “ tea-cup ’’ handle
ornaments back-hair no more ; it’s not the mode
now to show daylight through the coiffure. Also
the regular ripple undulated by crimping-irons is
breaking up into more natural-looking kinks and
waves. The sleek, alluring, loopy bunch on either
•ear is not a style that caught on here, though it
is a boon to the woman with saddle-flap ears.
T inally, the little curls on the nape of the neck
iolstoi’s pet fascination, are now anathema’
straggling hairs being severely gathered up and
caught with a glittering comb —diamond for
choice.
If the projected Irving-Terry tour to these parts,
hinted at by the two London stars to the jubi-
lating Premiers, should ever come off, somebody
will drop money over the venture. To recoup the
enormous exes, prices will have to be raised, and
most ot the high-price payers will be those who
have already “ clone ” the Lyceum along with St.
Paul's ar.d the Tower and all the other sights of a
trip 'Orne. So that the wealthy can’t be relied on
to fill the d.o. consistently throughout a season.
Irving and Terry haven’t the charm of novelty,
for them. And Australian audiences in general
won’t enthuse over the pair. Irving and Terry
are a purely British institution, and an irreverent
traveller who hints to a Londoner that the idols
aren’t quite as young as they were when they were
younger is frowned at as much as if he had spoken
disrespectful things about the House of Commons,
or declared that the Thames wasn’t as good a river
as the Yarra. But Cornstalk playgoers will pro-
bably see in Irving only an older and stiffer Kyrle
Bellew, and in Ellen Terry—herself, still charm-
ing, but a little the worse for wear. After Toole
and one or two other much-heralded imported
failures, the trumpet of Fame isn’t such an ac-
credited instrument with us as of yore.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Miss Hathorne, of Sydney, is trying to com
vert Melbourne to the ‘ mental healing,’ and at
the conclusion of one of her lectures the other
night invited her audience to ‘go into Hilence for
ten minutes, holding steadfastly to the thought
that there is a great power for good in the uni-
verse.’ The meeting dropped into silence, and
thought so hard that it became purple in the face,
but nothing happened, which was a great disap-
pointment. I remember a popular evangelist who
visited Ballarat about 18 years ago, and whose
great forte was the efficacy of prayer. lle used
to conclude his meetings by turning down the
1 ighta, and inviting all present to wrestle in silent
prayer, that wickedness might be banished from
the town. But one night, whilst he and his dis-
ciples were in the throes, somebody got away with
the whole of a phenomenal collection. After that
the evangelist was content to pray in the light,
and with one eye open. This story is meant to
convince Miss Hathorne’s audience that it should
be grateful if only for the reason that its hats,
cloaks, and umbrellas were not removed whilst it
was holding steadfastly to its faith.
“At a recent ‘ toney ’ religious fair, a sprink-
ling of dear good creatures had been got in to do
the dull work, and by their staunch dowdiness
leaven the main lump of tine clothes and frivolity.
One of these faithful few, presumedly unversed in
the facial landmarks of local bluebluods, bailed up
a stout intruder at the door of her side-show and
demanded entrance-fee. He pooh-poohed pay-
ment, and introduced himself as the principal
potentate on the premises. His story fell on un-
believing ears, and as the recipient pocketed the
extorted toll, she remarked snortfully that the real
Simon Pure, in similar case, would have handed
over a sovereign at the very least in, proof of his
bona tides. Later on, showing the ‘ Suspect ’to a
friend, she learned, to her horror, that the penny-
wise patriarch, so far from being a wicked story-
teller, had not exaggerated his importance by a
hair-breadth.
“ Lots of tidy little trades turn up these times
for women. Weeks before the Brassey ball,
fashionable dancing-and-deportment-teachers were
busy in the stately homes of Toorak, educating
the elect up to hoops, clouded canes, &c. On the
eventful day dressers had their hands full, acting
in many cases under the superintendance of re-
tired mummers, hardup historical authorities and
suchlike. Lady-hairdressers, too, found business
brisk during race-season. Some of then, had quite
a round of calls to make twice daily to visitors at
fashionable caravanserais.
. The Jonah type of humanity is not yet extinct.
One woman sees out three husbands, and there are
men warranted to precipitate wreck, collision or
fire whenever they go down to the sea in ships.
A family of attractive sisters have been hard on
their respective fiances. Both the elder girls wore
mourning in turn for a bridegroom-elect, carried
off suddenly. When the third saw her best boy
at death s door, she took it as a warning and broke
off the engagement in time to save his life.
‘ Pretty Janet Carmichael—younger sister of
Jennings Carmichael, the bardess—died at Mel-
bourne, the other day, of tvphoid fever. Yerv
like her sister in appearance, Jenny ’ Carmichael
was a bright, vivacious girl and saucy paragraph-
writer, who endeared herself to all her friends,
ohe had been a hospital-nurse at sundry times and
places, and the news of her death drew many tears
from eyes that are not easily affected.
Detective Christie as Svengali was positively
the most obtrusive figure at the Brassey fancy
ball, and everyone asked, ‘Who is it?’ Which,
of course, must have been the object of the modest
masquerader, who proudly displays a photographic
album of his disguises more various than those of
Lauri. And now people are disposed to ask why
the great man has just been so mightily over-
reached in his special department of the Customs.
A suggestion for Governor Brassey : why
does he not, instead of clothing his footmen and
coachmen in brown livery with white facings, rig
them out to suit the climate in white raiment
with brown facings ? The Old Man of the Sea is
fond of his chocolate-cream livery colors. He
would preserve these by adopting this suggestion,
and not make his servants on hot days look like
baked things with patches of white grease on
their collars and cuffs.
“A stranger, strolling into a recent Melb.
oavage Oiub smoke-night, expected the thing as
per invitation, to be a ‘ wild Bohemian night, old
boy, but he found a Supreme Court Judge in the
chair and a, programme of mostly classical music.
Being well up the visitor could not escape until
late, and, whilst in the act of getting away, he
was bailed up by his inviter, who poured out a
torrent of abject apologies, not for the dull
respectability of the ‘ function,’ but for the
presence of three guests in—haw !—morning cos-
f. trust, said the apologist, snorting in
the direction of the ill-attired trio, ‘ I trust you
will accept my assurance that they are not mem-
bets of the club.’ The visitor needed no assurance
on the point. He had seen for himself that the
true Bohemians would never dream of attempting
to be wild and wicked apart from a swallow-tailed
coat.
“ A certain vice-regal household, blessed with
vast private means, has been working its chari-
table and other unavoidable expenditure lately on
a few annual dollars. Two ladies of the family
patronised' a church fancy fair, and, after
making the rounds, the high-toned ‘patronage*
totted up to 2s. (id. for the elder and 3d. for the
younger ! Both loftily sailed out, leaving their
purchases to be sent per carrier, and when the
goods were delivered c.o.d. there was indignation
in Govt. House. Later a missive, on large em-
bossed note-paper, arrived at the bazaar, com-
plaining that the carriage of the purchase hadn’t
been prepaid, and requesting separate receipts for
the sum—two-and-ninepence !
“ Agnes Murphy, the Melb. Paunch penwoman
who went to London, seems to be acting as chief
collector of small items re Australians at ’Ome.
When young Jones, son of the famous Sydney
something or other, gets married to Miss
Smith, of Peckham Rye, the surprising news
is promptly mailed across the ocean by in-
defatigable Agnes. If boomster Mugs be return-
ing to the land of his ill-gotten gold, Agnes
hastily records the fact that Mr. M. is looking
well ; and every time Ada Crossley shakes hands
with a viscount her faithful Murphy takes a note
of the Anglo-Australian event. Also, she pauses
to mention that the viscount has lately quarrelled
with one of his aunts. Among hundreds of little
London societies for the diffusion of paragraphs
concerning its members the Agnes Murphy society
is the only one that places Australians on a plat-
form apart from humanity in general. Itamounts
to an Austral Salon in London. Such great
Occasions as the annual birthday of Miss M., or
her aspiring friend Aimee Moore, are celebrated
with a tea-party, and the names of the distin-
guished visitors find their way into print, if
possible. Australia would know nothing about
many of the brilliant intellects that have gone
’Ome if there were no Murphy to mention their
existence.
“ The unemployed woman begins to turn her
attention to hairdressing. Neat-handed girls
serve a short apprenticeship to the trade, which
includes shampooing, singeing, and traffic in
‘ladies’ own combings,’ a branch of business
hitherto monopolised by the ‘Wandering Jew’
who periodically pesters householders for old clo’,
worn-out store-teeth, greasy novels, and such-like
domestic flotsam and jetsam.
“ Rumor has it that, compared to the domestic
broils of an Australian gubernatorial clan, the
doings of the Kilkenny cats were kitten gambols.
Strong wills and long tongues run in families to the
sorrow and confusion of mild marriage con-
nections. The ‘ nieces of the temple,’ or some
such society of hard-worked young women, sab
round meekly the other day, and listened to the
wisdom of their pretty president—a vice-regal off-
shoot, by the way. ‘Didn’t she talk?’ they
asked one another afterwards. And so she did.
She laid down the law and harangued on the
duties of women, and went on anyhow to girls,
mostly older than herself, who, perforce, practise
more patience, self-restraint, and all the rest of ib
in a week than she gets the chance of doing in a
year. Patience and self-restraint, you know, are
not things that people care to achieve, as a rule,
nor are we born with them. They are thrust
upon us, whether we like them or not.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.

Scientific authorities are beginning to back
down about their opinion of the revolving ship in-
vented by a Canadian barrister named Knapp.
The vessel consists simply of a huge cylinder, in-
side which the passenger accommodation is so
slung as to remain always vertical, while the
cylinder, by means of paddle-floats, rolls over the
surface at a rate which the inventor asserts will
certainly reach one mile per minute, and possibly
more than two ! Experiments are being conducted
with a model of 110 ft. by 22ft. diameter, but whab
is contemplated is a 750 ft cylinder with 150fb,
diameter, and a capacity of 15,000 tons.

Premier Kingston’s wife, who is a good
Catholic, when in Rome on her way from the
Jubilee celebrations, was admitted to the Pope’s
private chapel, and received communion from the
hands of the Pontiff himself.
N.S.W. Colonial Secretary Brunker studied
law for three years, but gave up the pursuit to
go auctioneering. When you want to flatter him
say, “Had you stuck to law you would have been
Chief Justice to-day, uncle.”
Newspaper controversies about prosely-
tising the distant heathen receive a peculiar
topical puncture in the fact that a daughter of the
editor of Sydney Mail and a son of the sub-editor
of Sydney Daily Telegraph have just left Aus-
tralia to proselytise the Chinese.
It is stated that Mr. F. W. Haddon, editor
of Melb. Argus for the last 20 years, retires
shortly from that position to join the proprietary.
The editorship will, in all probability, fall to
“ Timotheus ” Willoughby, the suave and oul-
tured political leader-writer of to-day.
Labor-leader M‘Pherson (S.A.) is so ill
that he has resigned every office in connection
with his party. It is unlikely that he will be able
to take any active part in politics again during
this year. At one time it was feared that his
ailments, the result of worry and overwork, would
prove fatal, but he is now slowly recovering.
Speaker Abbott (N.S.W.) has now com-
Sleted seven years in the chair. The record
, peakership was that of W. M. Arnold (’65 to
7'5) Ex-Speakers who still live are Daniel Cooper,
Edmund Par ton and Works-Minister Young—
who will probably have the refusal of the chair
should Abbott go to London as Agent-General.
Michael Davitt, The Bulletin hears from
London, has completed his book on Australia,
dealing with his visit from an observant poli-
tician s and economist s standpoint. He is now
revising the proofs. The book will appear in
December early, and will thus get the start of
Mark l’wain’s book, to be out some time in
January.
The story goes that the fervent loyalty of
a big Australian surgeon was badly damped when
his vice-regal patient subjected his account to a
severe “ taxation,” and fought over each item with
the vigor of a second-hand furniture-dealer. It
was, in fact, only when the man of scalpels talked
law that he managed to get a cheque for the full
amount.
S.A. Assembly now rivals the Council in

Upper House toper, however, he manifests his
inebriation in the mildest and most good-tempered
way, although in him, too, excess of liquor brings an
irresistible impulse to take part in debate. Where
the Councillor raves, the Assembly - member
merely drivels.

Cavan Duffy snobbishly complains in his
current Contemporary article, that an Australian
colonist obtained no “special recognition” when
he was Oine 30 years ago. Yet he tells of social
relations he then had with Carlyle, Robert Brown-
-IPP> Newman, John Stuart Mill, John Forster
Disraeli, etc. What more did the man want ? He
w a s not satisfied, apparently, because dukes and
such things did not ask him to lunch. A man who
wasnot a downright snob would have thought
more of meeting and conversing with such men as
Browning, Mill and Carlyle than of being feted
by a whole peerage.
Ramsay Smith, one of
Kingston’s imported Ade-
laide hospital doctors—the
senior physician—had a de-
lightful moment at an in-
quest on an ether victim the
other day. A post -mcr‘cm
was called for, and, in con-
sequence of a row among
the hospital staff, Dr.
Humphrey Marten, an out-
sider, was invited to make
it. Now Marten, as a very
leading practitioner, is an
antagonist of the Napier-
Smith faction. Dr. Smith
disagreed with Dr. Marten’s
description of the internal
organs, and produced part
of the lungs. Instantly the
coroner asked, “How did
you get that?” This was
Smith’s chance. “As offi-
cer in charge of the post-
mortem room I entered. The
attendant drew my atten-
tion to the fact that Dr.
Marten had forgotten to re-
place the heart, so I had to
open the body to restore
the organ, and then saw
the diseased organ, which it
was my duty, as pathologist
of the institution, to take
charge of.” His enemy had
forgotten to replace the
heart—alj the rest was, in a
way, but “leather and pru-
nella.” *
