THE HISHOP OF BALLARAT.
London, Nov. 16.— Dr. Thornton, Bishop of Ballarat,
who is at present in England, has declined three benefices
which have been offered him, not considering them
spheres where he would be specially useful.
The Archbishop of Canterbury’ has just offered Dr
ihornton the vicarage of St. Matthew's, Brixton The
is considering- the offer. Mrs. Thornton is in deli-
cate health.
How characteristically Anglican ! The Bishop—
and the Bishop’s wife. What a heavy pull the
Pope has!
Frank A. Nankivell, the clever Prahran
Vic. black-and-white artist, who has made such a
hit in Yankeeland, is back in Australia, after six
years absence. Nankivell was formerly a drafts-
man in the Vic. Railway Dept., Melb., and got
six months leave to visit Japan. But Chrysan-
themum land held the impressionable Australian
for two years, during which he drew extensively
for a local Yankee print. Thence to ’Frisco,
where his reputation was made, and thence again
to New York ; he eventually landed on the staff
of Puck, where he is to-day. On his way through,
the Australian interviewed Phil May in London,
and brought out a lorry-load of good-wishes from
that artist to his friends here. A fellow-passenger
of Nankivell was R.C. Burt, the soulful Age
dramatic-erifcic, who resumes his place on David
?J'i ne s P a P er - Since Burt left, nearly a year ago,
Melb. Block has never seemed the same, but
with his green ves-ts and heliotrope ties again on
view, life in Melb.-is once more worth living.
GALL IT ENGLISH AND IT’S WELCOME.
Dear Bulletin, —I picked you up this
morning, date Sept. 4, and saw a par. beginning
“ What has become of Willie Wilde ? ” An hour
after, when riding on a bus down King’s-road,
Chelsea, I saw “ Willie ” slouching along the
street—a tall, heavy, clammy-lookingman with a
black beard, and hollow eyes. He must have been
a handsome fellow when young. I had seen him
previously at the Duke of York's School annual-
drill, well-groomed, but sleepy-looking. He lives
m the neighborhood of Chelsea, which contains
also little Haweis, who lives in Dante Rossetti’s
house, Leonard Courtney, of the Telegraph
Ackland(ex-Mimster-of-Education), Arthur Stree-
ton, T. P. O'Connor, Georgina Hogarth (Dickens’
sister-in-law), and yours truly— Cornstalk.
“ EGGS.”
(A well-known Sydney character.)
Theophilus Cooper, of Auckland (M.L.),
who is mentioned for the next Maoriland judgeship,
is a safe man. Commencing as a printer’s-devil,
he became a compositor, educated himself as a bar-
rister, and now, not much over 40, shares with
Stout, Q.C., the leadership of the Maoriland bar.
W. _J. Napier, another Auckland barrister, and
chairman of the local Harbor Board, had even a
more remarkable career.
Died lately, in England, G. C. Boase, the
best-known of Cornish antiquaries, whose
“ Bibliotheca Cornubiensis ” (the joint production
of Boase and W. P. Courtney) is the standard
work on the subject. Boase came to Australia
with the Bendigo gold-rushers in ’54, worked for
a time on the Age, then tried digging near Castle-
maine, afterwards storekeeping and tutoring, and
finally returned to England in ’64.
Late E. R. Drury, the Q.N. Bank s Man
Who’s Dead, bossed everything he was connected
with except Tommy MTlwraith, who bossed him.
Among other things he pretty well bossed the
Queensland Club Committee of his era, and, at
one meeting thereof, he wanted to carry something
or other with a high hand. Most of the committee-
men present acquiesced; but one veteran Ish-
maeliteobjected vehemently—perhaps on principle,
perhaps out of pure cussedness—and remarked :
“ It seems to me that this club is run by its trades-
men. There is So-and-So, the club’s stationer;
there is So-and-So, the club’s grocer ; there is So-
and-So the club’s ginger-beer merchant; there are
So-and-So, and So-and-So, who appear to divide
the club’s whisky and beer account between them ;
and, finally, there is Mr. Drury, who looks after
the club’s overdraft.” Then the roof fell in.
Captain Bickers, who, with his brother,
used to be in the Mauritius and interprovincial
trade (their 8.8. fleet traded all round the coast),
died at Port Adelaide the other day, aged 82.
The younger generation remembers' him only
for his eccentric observations from the bench
—for, living in S. Australia, he was, of course, a
J.P. The old captain was of the same pious cast
of mind as Kipling’s McAndrew; and it landed
him in difficulties with his old partner, David
Bower, still living. Bickers wouldn’t leave port
on the Sawbath, and Bovver wouldn't let the day
be wasted. So they split the difference. Bower,
who managed things ashore, ordered the vessels to
put out; and Bickers, who was the nautical man,
ordered the vessels to anchor when they had
reached a safe distance from the wharfside. But
while steam gave McAndrew a comfortable billet
it simply ruined poor old Bickers, who lost all he
had made in attempting, with his sailing craft to
keep level with the then newly-introduced steam-
coaster.
Dear Bulletin, —In a recent issue (unfor-
tunately I have lost the date) you mention the
case of an old Swedish sailor, at present employed
in a saw-mill, who had been capsized in a ship off
the Brazilian coast. The name is given as “ Loch
Li ley,” but, perhaps, his broken English failed to
pronounce correctly the name of the ship. I think
it must be the “Loch Maree.” She left Geelong
16 or 17 years ago, and was never heard of again,
and, as this time tallies with his statement, I am
confirmed in my belief. As he alleges that’ he is
the only survivor, and is, evidently, reticent over
the occurrence, never having officially reported it,
how would it be possible for me to find out the
truth ? lam somewhat interested, as I served my
apprenticeship in her, and several of my ship-
mates were on board when she vanished into
oblivion.— Shellback. [According to The Bul-
letin's correspondent, the sailor’s name is Arm-
strong and he is in the employ of Messrs Ellis
timber merchants, of Camden Haven N S W
Ed. B.]
With reference to a paragraph in our issue
of October 16, relating to the marriage of Mr.
Muddit Allie, a Hindoo licensed hawker, well and
favorably known in the Moama district of N.S. W.,
The Bulletin learns with regret that certain
statements therein contained are totally incorrect.
As a matter of fact, Mr. Muddit Allie was married
by a Church of England clergyman at Rochester
(Vic.), and his honeymoon was spent at the resi-
dence of a respected citizen of Carlton (Vic.). The
statement regarding police enquiries is quite un-
true- Mr. Muddit Allie has been a resident of
N.b. Wales for upwards of six years, and The
Bulletin is assured that both in business and
privately he bears an irreproachable reputation.

Aboriainalities.
Cork Constitution, of Sept. 24, thus airs
itself, begorra:—
Snakes are commonly to be met with in New South
Wales, and are useful for various purposes. Some make
pets of them, and others whips and missiles. A member
of the Government entered the Legislative Assembly re-
cently with a huge snake coiled round his arm, and took
his seat behind the Treasury bench. Several members
rose to call the Speaker’s attention to the presence of the
objectionable “stranger,” and the offending member
slipped out of the House before the Speaker could bring
him to order. Snakes appear, however, to be gradually
making their way into public life in the parent Australian
colony. A little time ago, at an election meeting, a black
snake was thrown at the candidate, and the meeting
ended with a fight among the electors, who pulled snakes
out of their coat-pockets, and lashed each other with
them.
* * * *
The Bryen Boiroimhe was the best-kept
local pub., and Mick O’Hagan, the landlord,
aided by his buxom wife, did his level best for his
guests. In a general way, Mick was a mild-
spolcen man and a pattern of politeness, but, at
t.mes, his language was strong —when he started
to instil right principles into his numerous off-
spring, for instance. Early one morning, the
chapel bell was ringing, and Mick was heard, in
sulphurous phrases, bawling to the boys to “ clean
themselves” and be off to mass. “If you call
yourselves blanky blank Christians, why the
blanky blank don’t you attind your religious
duties?” Then Mick came into the breakfast-
room, looking as mild as usual, and, seating him-
self at the head of the table, he solemnly ex-
plained the situation to his waiting guests. ‘ ‘ This
is a great day in our church, gintlemen. It’s
what they call Ash Wednesday—whativer the
divil that manes. Lamb’s fry or pork sausage.
Mr. Jacobs ? ”

John Smith, to The Bulletin :
Dear Bulletin, — Re Chows in Queensland ; revisiting
recently my native town in that province, I found not
only that by intermarriage I was related to the whole com-
munity, but also, to my dismav, that the Chows had
become largely mixed up in the connection. One big
family of half-bred Chinese owns a large portion of the
town, and, in fact, there are more of these half-yellows in
the place than in Cairns and Thursday Island put together.
And, what is worse, the whites appear to be proud of
their slant-eyed relatives ; in fact, it isn’t safe to say a
word against them, lest one of the family should lay you
out. I think I’m about the only white native of the place
who ever had go enough to leave it, and when I went
back I found I had no chance against the vellow curse
of getting any work. I’m glad I’ve no sin', ere in the
infernal hole, because one almond-eye wanted me to
introduce him to my cousin. “ Welly nicee gel 1” he
thought, so I introduced him to my boot—and k.t 1
The Chow is now much more fashionable than he
used to be. Twenty years ago The Bulletin
knew a young man whose pretty sister in his

* » • *
The latest dog-story, this time from far-
back N.S.W. A Gonnulpie Downs station-hand,
M‘Dermot, thrown from his horse, was lying
helpless in that awful wilderness when, with a
rabbit-bone, he scratched a message on his dog’s
collar and sooled the animal off to the station. Of
course the sagacious beast ought to have at once
raced off for help and whined piteously until the
message had been read, when it should have
bounded off ahead of the searchers. But it didn’t
do anything of the kind —it just hung round and
occasionally looked for more rabbit-bones till the
fourth day, when a search-party found the pair.
This, by the way, was M'Dermot’s second experi-
ence of this kind.

Alleged extract from letter to a Sydney
merchant apropos South Sea Island affairs
generally :
Things are goeing verey Bad down here Now business is
verey bad and lotts of Sickness anion the Natifes and I
believe a lott want to Die just to eseap paying the Taxes.
Since the Governmend come here (annexation to Great
Britain) the natifes are hardder to Get on with than they
ever was since I been here. There is a new Native mis-
sionary comeing here next year we have three here now
and onily one is any good the other 2 are Dam Lofers and
Put on more frill than a White Bishop. But they cannot
make the Laws now and try to fine us Traiders for smok-
ing of a Sunday as They did before the Commisner came
to live here. I had a letter from Harry pitman in the
Marshals (Marshall Islands) last Month the natife mis-
sionarys is pretty bad There, Harry went aboard a ger.
man Ship to buy some dinermite and a few Plates and
came Back drunk and Laid down on the Sofer and Went
to sleap, when in comes the Natife teachers wife and says
to Harrys wife your man has been and got Drunk your a
Liar says Harrys wife hes onily Tired. So off goes the
teachers wife for her Husband to come and smell Harrys
Breth but his Wife woak him up and when the Natife
teacher comes in Harry hit him on the Nose and now they
are At logerheds agane.

Dear Bulletin, Find the bard who
swims and your team of bush-poets will be com-
plete. You have remarked that in “The Banjo ”
you’ve got “ the bard who rides,” and in Henry
Lawson, “ the bard who walks.” I think Will.
H. Ogilvie is obviously the bard who drives—and
drives exceedingly well in his “Dray-Dreams,”
“Phantom Coach,” “Team of Roans,” “Grey
Leader, ” &e., &c. Re, Lawson’s “In the Days
When the World Was Wide,” perhaps it’s late in
t he season, but I’ve got a grievance of which I’d
like to be unburdened. It has much disappointed
me that not one of the book’s legions of town and
country reviewers—including yourself—quoted,
mentioned, or even hinted at what / think Law-
son’s prettiest and most truly characteristic and
graphic bush picture: “When the Army prays
for Watty,” which, next to “The Vagabond,” I
like better than all the rest of Lawson’s verses.—
6xB.
* * * *
Said the old battler, sitting on his 801 b.
swag : “ See that bitch there. She’s the best
breed in the country. They’d work anything—a
shearing-machine if you showed ’em how ! Well,
her first litter was by a dog what come of the next
best breed ” (he gave a yard of pedigree with
each); “and the pups! Good? Well, they just
was good. Tell ye how good they was. The day
their eyes opened I was away, and when I come
home, blow me if they wasn’t working all the old
mutton-bones about the place up into a heap, and
her sitten watchen ’em. Didn’t they, old girl ? ”
The old girl wagged an affirmative, and the gang
gazed in silent admiration upon her master as he
buried his nose in the champion pint they imme-
diately ordered for him.
A rather more earnest
effort is being made in
Adelaide than in the other
centres to gather and pre-
serve knowledge of the
disappearing aborigines.
A. T. Magarey, about the
most enthusiastic local in-
vestigator, lectured lately
on “ Black-tracking,” and
Tom Coward assisted.
Magarey mentioned (and
bushmen in the far North
know it to be a fact) that
youngsters are trained by
their mothers, who pub
them to work first on
lizards’ trails. But track-
ing comes natural to them.
A little toddler left in camp,
and losing its mother,
makes a circuit of the wur-
lies till it has picked up
its mother’s marks, and
then unhesitatingly follows
them out. Aj. wformance
vouched for by Mnrif, the
overland cyclist, is hard to
beat. A far-in settler had
three named cats, the same
size, and apparently pos-
sessed of precisely similar
feet. He got the three in
a room (the hut was on a
hard patch, by the way),
and then told Murif, who
had been informed as to
their names, to throw one
of them out—any one. A
few minutes later he called
up a blackfellow attached
to the camp. “ Harry,
one cat got out window.
Which way?” Harry,
though it was already deep
dusk, picked up the track
and’followed it. Then he
was called back. “ Whioh
one cat ? ” “ That Kittie ”
—and Kittie it was.
Ed. Bulletin, — Re the shearing record
lately posted in Bulletin, I don’t know how long
men work in America, but in Australia a shearing
day is nearly nine hours, not eight. Shearers
work 48 hours per week, and in nearly all sheds
cease work at 12 o’clock on Saturday, and to make
the 48 hours work considerably over eight hours
on the other five days.— Flong.

Werrimee : “A question has often puzzled
me. Why do so many men go mad on the
Darling? Perhaps the ‘Spirit of the Plains’ has
stretched its spider-neck along its sun-baked
shores. There one constantly hears such expres-
sions as ‘looney,’ ‘balmy,’ ‘ratty,’ ‘moon-struck,’
and ‘ feather-head ’ (brother of ‘ leather-’ead ’), in-
terspersed with ‘white-ants,’ ‘centipedes,’ and
‘ snakes.’ Further out, when a man is met with
woo is a little more foolish than the usual, they
si/, ‘He's from the Darling.’ Apropos. A
shearers’ cook struck W station late one
nilht, and, as he appeared ill, he was given a
r> >m and made comfortable. Soon after, he
rushed into the drawing-room and stood awhile,
">■ filing and glaring at its single occupant. Then
h e said that 40 odd shearers were climbing through
his window with axes to kill him. Nothing could
induce him to return to that room, and about
L' dnight it was found necessary to drive him into
r i oooburra. On the way, he continually entreated
t j be driven faster, as the shearers were still after
h.mwith the axes. Now, what sort of stuff had
h i been cooking on the Darling ? ”

“ Paddy the Dodger ” on Papuan fever :
Uoar Bullktin, The ‘‘white ants” must be getting
hold of “ Scotty, ’ or he couldn’t have said that Southern
well-fed and blooded men can stand climates like Papua
better than N. Queenslanders. Twelvemonths’ residence
on Woodlark Island enables me to state quite the con-
trary. without fear of contradiction by any man who
stayed there long enough to know what lie’s ta'kin
about. The average time the burly Southerner could
stand against the fever was four weeks, while the shrivelled
Bananaman on an average withstood it perhaps 16 weeks.
About a dozen of the latter escaped the fever, but not
a single mother’s son from south of Brisbane ; many
of the latter being knocked over inside of eight days.
Same in Westralia. Also, the Queenslander takes on
hand far more hardships than Southerners. Who but
acclimatised Queenslanders open up new country ? Why,
Queensland swagmeu, on johnny-cake, have succeeded
where gorgeously turned-out expeditions from the South
have failed ! Bananamen are not afraid to return to
Papua and face the fever a second, and third time. I
have been knocking about the North and the Islands for
16 years, and my battling weight, when I get an
unlimited supply of damper and salt-horse, is Bst. 31b.
My advice to a huge, fat-headed Southern man, when
going to a place like Papua, is to take a few dozen apples
and oranges in his portmanteau. Assuming that he will
stop the average time of Southerners—" till the next boat
comes in —he may then manage to get back to the land
of the living in safety, and stand a show of bein'-- alive 20
years afterwards. "

Some of the opal-buyers in Opalville,
N.S.W., come from the Fatherland with not
enough English at command to bail up the
garden cow. Profanity, however, in the
mother tongue seems to come easily within their
grasp, writes a Bulletin man. Imagine a staid
Teuton, in all good faith, at the dinner table of
one of the hotels, requesting the handmaid to give
him another helping of that “ B y beef steak.”
CHAS, F. BARDEN, a champion English cyclist .
Photo., Barroni & Co., Melbourne.
The long-legged gentleman astride his
bicycle in the portrait is Chas. F. Barden of Put-
ney a distinguished English cyclist. He arrived
Australia with his wife and book (“ The Book
of Charles F. Barden ”) a few weeks back, and to
his eternal praise, be it said, he can cycle as
well as he can talk, and that is as well as most
Australian cyclists can eat. Consequently he has
beaten almost every cyclist he has met, including
Michael, the Welsh Wonder. Bike all the leading
professional cyclists, he knows he has the fastest
sprint m the world, and, when he hasn’t it then
he says he is out of form. When he first came out
as a. rider he was always chaperoned by his pa
and under pa s mentorship he won o 6 prizes in
succession. In matches and competitions he has
a remarkable record, and, though he is suspected
of having the least little bundle all the same his
record of victories argues for a courage equal to
his dash. On Monday week, at Adelaide, he beat
Breen in the mile, but was beaten by him in the
five and ten miles. Green should beat Barden
now, however, for the newcomer is not acclima-
tised yet. Barden rides a Swift cycle with Dun-
lop tyres.
Woman’s Lot.
There are some men—especially bachelors—who
think that women have the best of it in life.
They have an idea that very little trouble falls to
the lot of that sex, and that the claims for
women’s rights spring from a desire on the part
of the ladies to escape from the monotony of an
existence which has no cares nor sorrow. But
this is wrong. Woman’s lot is the hardest—
socially, morally, and physically. She possesses
great influence, and it is by its exercise that men
are kept in order. Her moral altitude in society
is a test to which we are all agreed to submit.
Notwithstanding all this, woman is kept rigidly
within the boundary line of conventionalism.
They suffer, these delicate creatures, as we of
stronger and coarser fibre can form little idea of.
Their delicate organisation is highly susceptible
to ailments and diseases which are almost foreign
to man. Mrs. Ada Front, 158 Donald Street,
North Brunswick, says : “For two years previous
to last Christmas I felt almost an invalid, and at
times was unable to attend to my household
duties. My former robust health had been suc-
ceeded by a listless indifference to life, and the
doctors I consulted said 1 wassutiering from liver
complaint. Last Boxing Day T went to Beau-
maris to spend the holiday, and while there quite
collapsed. I had to be almost carried home and
put to bed. Three doctors were ca’led in, who
said I was suffering from inflaination of the
bowels. After weeks of suffering the inflamation
abated, but I was so weak as to be hardly able to
turn myself in bed, and wasted to a skeleton.
In that state I continued, expecting that death
would end my sufferings, when I thought of War-
ner’s Safe Cure. Some years previously a friend
of mine had used it with the most beneficial re-
sults. My husband got me the Safe Cure, and
although I have only used five large bottles my
health is quite restored. I feel the recovery is a
wonderful one, for which I cannot be too thank-
ful.” Her case is one in thousands who have had
strength renewed and health restored by resuming
to this remedial agent.

Gentlemen, —
In 1891 I obtained a machine from your Beeston Works, and it may be of
interest to you to know that I am still riding it, although it is six years and three
months old. I must express my unqualified satisfaction with the strength and
durability of the machine. My repairs bill is under £l. My riding has not been
confined to the roads, but includes bush tracks and much-neglected surfaces in
different parts of our colony—some thousands of miles altogether.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Those members of the M. 0.0. who are notori-
ously addicted to loading up the members’ reserve with
their female relatives and friends threaten to howl against
the new rule, which practically separates the women from
the men, and gives the smokers a haven of rest. But the
bitter cry of the tame-cat brigade is unlikely to restore
the old order of things. All the women in the reserve
are “ deadheads,” and as “admirers of the game ’’they
are mostly frauds. It was quite time for them to lose the
privilege of placing their parasols between the eye of the
genuine cricket patron and the game he has come forth
to see.
* * • •
Some genius writes to a Melb. paper suggesting
that the last batsman to go in should, when his partner
gets out before he, the 1.b., has a strike, be allowed to
continue, and, “if singles were made by him, the bowlers
could take turn-about at each end. ... I think it
would be very interesting to watoh the last man scoring,
probably winning a big match; then the eleven would
have taken part in the game.”
* » * •
Out of 12 Anglo-Australian cricket-matches
played at Sydney Australia won 7 ; out of same number at
Meib. Australia won 4 and drew 2 ; and out of three
Adelaide matches Australia got one. Out of the whole 27
matches, therefore—with two draws —England takes 13
and Australia 12. Honors all but easy !

* * * *
Melbourne cricket-olub officials—the Sydneyites
are just about as bad —do not miss many opportunities of
manifesting their devout respect for “ gentlemen ”
cricketers and their contempt for professionals, and made
the most of the chances during the Englishmen’s recent
visit. For instance, the drag containing the “gentlemen”
was allowed to drive right up to the pavilion entrance,
and land its superior freight there ; whereas the profes-
sionals found an inflexible officer at the outer wall, who
sternly warned their driver off, and the pros, had to dis-
mount ignominiously in the street, and pad it down the
track to the ground. The pros, could only express their
disgust in blasphemy. That is where they have the
advantage of The Bulletin.

George Giffen has alienated much of the sympa-
thy of Adelaide cricketers by quarrelling with a pro-
minent local player. Giffen claims to be in the right in
his dispute with Wardill and co., but he has made many
enemies by taking his grievance into local affairs.

Surcingle: “ With brightening times, the
V.R.O. has shifted back Secretary Byron Moore’s screw
from £9OO to £lOOO. Other top-lights have been cheer-
fully dealt with in a like spirit. Meanwhile, the bulk of
the employes are neglected, though some were cut down
as far as from £2 2s. a race-day to £1 ss. under stress of
the boom downfall. A few months ago, I here referred to
the beautiful enthusiasm of a V.R.C. committee member
for a rise being given to the paid steward, with this advo-
cate’s remark that ‘ the laborer is worthy of his hire.’
Such magnanimous principles are never applied to lower
ranks. A racecourse is like a theatre —with a mass of
employes practically' invisible to the public, though, in
this case, mixed up vyith them. These are, necessarily',
honorable and, mostly, long-tried men.”

Considering that the sporting paper by which
Southern starting-prices are largely regulated is owned
by a starting-price bookie, it is lictle wonder that winners’
prices are usually shorter in that paper than in any other
sporting medium. The exceptions are when the s.p.
man has had a “ skinner,” then the starting-price is
fabulously extravagant.

Melb. Cup comment in Hobart Mercury :
“ Since Arsenal’s year (1886) the big two-mile handicap,
with a single exception—that of Auraria—has remained
on the Australian mainland, but this year it has crossed
to N.S.W. by the aid of Gaulus.

The S.A. juvenile anti-gambling bill inflicts a
maximum penalty of “£lOO or six months” on persons
betting with youths under 21, and renders the minor so
betting liable to a £2O fine. The “three-card trick ” and
the “purse trick” are prohibited by name.

Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name arid address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, whether sent by po*t or handed in,
and in no case will MS. be returned unless stamps
(of any province) accompany.

“Premier Reid definitely announces that he will fight the next [?]. S. W. general-election on the question of reform of the Upper House.”
G. H. REIN (at vex general-election) : “Didn’t I promise you to reform the Upper House or abolish it altogether ? ” (Chorus of voices— “ You did! ” and cheers.) “ And
didnt. I promise to make everybody jolly and fat?” (C,ies of “ You did!” and laughter.) “ Well, I promise it again!” (Prolonged and deafening
cheers, and unanimous vote of conjiuence.!"

followed Charleston’s lead in deserting his Labor
colleagues inS.A. Assembly.
“ Medico ” writes to the Sydney daily press
advocating the abolition of dust-bins. What !
another Freetrade industry doomed.

London Echo prints the “ contemptuous remarks ”
of the Argus upon Ben Tillett as a sample of
unanimous Australian opinion.

Island, wrote lately to Premier Reid exhorting
him to smite, in season and out of season, all
schemes to imperialise Australia.

Australian Federal capital by, among others, Her-
oules Robinson, Hori Grey, Charles Dilke, Sam.
Griffith, and J;he late Railway Commissioner
Eddy.

country declaring that he wouldn’t allow one line

pertinently pointed out in the late discussion on

17 receive salaries far over that amount.

mingham gunmakers supplied many of the Afridis with

“ A nation of shopkeepers ! ”
Editorial aphorism from Brisbane (Q.)
Courier: “ Ministers cannot afford to contradict
themselves in this fashion.” Phoo! Under
“ Government by Contradiction ” Ministers can
afford to contradict themselves in any fashion.
Within a fortnight, M.L. ex-Treasurer
Ward was declared by the Court of Appeal duly
qualified to take his seat, was discharged from
bankruptcy, and was nominated by Seddon for
a seat on the Public Accounts Committee. Looks
as if next session w-ill see him Treasurer again.
Opposition-leader John Downer has been
so palpably wooing the S.A. Assembly Labor-
party lately that he has offended the more con-
firmed Tories who were once his most faithful
supporters. Sad to say, this loss has not been
compensated for by any real gain of Labor votes.
Inglis Clark’s acceptance of the leadership
of the Tasmanian Opposition should put some real
life into that dreary fragment. If there is one live
Democrat among the front-rank politicians of the
Isle of Snore it is Braddon’s ex-Attorney-General,
and the local dry-bones should now tie shunted
along with quite unusual celerity.
On 15th Oct. last N.S.W. aboriginals
numbered 3422 full-bloods and 3GG3 half-castes ;
full-bloods having decreased in 12 months by 81,
while half-castes increased by 182, a circumstance
which, while pointing to speedy disappearance of
the race, shows also that disappearance does not
mean death. The white race absorbs the black one.
“Nick” Fitzgerald, M.L.C., who repre-
sents the wind-power and, in the minds of many,
the wisdom of Vie. Leg. Council, is back from
his jubilee wanderings, and tells a;Melb. Herald
interviewer apropos of Federation: “I do not
think it necessary to provide against deadlocks,
as they only occur when obstinate and not com-
mon-sense men are in power.” “ What a ’ead ’e
’ave ! ” “ Nick ” must favor the abolition of the
police, since they are only needed when criminals
and not law-abiding citizens are at work.
Maoriland Opposition grows weary of
Russell, whose Roman nose, and langour, and
aristocratic “haw,” and aboriginal British Tory-
ism make the prospects of success under his leader-
ship microscopic. It is generally understood that
if Stout will sink his Cold 'tea principles and
won’t insist on establishing a compulsory regime
of root-beer, he may have the leadership for the
asking. And Stout, who only yearns for two
things in this world—office and Cold Tea—drops
his last remaining hair into his cup of misery as he
realises that he must sacrifice one of them to get
the other, or else lose them both.
One Maoriland Oppositionist indignantly
declares that Seddon, with his official salary and
his Jubilee expenses, will actually cost the country
this year “ nearly as much as the Governor.”
Well, why shouldn’t he ? If the imported figure-
head is worth, say, <£sooo a year, then even such
Premiers as Nelson and Reid are worth =£6ooo.
And men of the calibre of Turner, Seddon, and
Kingston should be cheap at £15,000. About the
most sinful grovel in this grovelsome land is the
grovel which values a mere useless Excellency at
J 35000 or <£7ooo, while Ballance received .£lOOO,
and Turner grinds out his very soul in his country’s
service at £l4OO.
The Federal Enabling
Bill just introduced into
Bananaland Assembly
bids fair to provoke a
pretty little Legislative
shindy. Inasmuch as the
Bill provides for the whole
province voting as one
electorate, it goes without
saying that the stagnant
capitalistic South will
simply annihilate all
chance of the Democratic
North and centre securing
any decent share of repre-
sentation. Consequently
North, South, and most
of the Labor-party clamor
for tripartite election so as
to give Torrea and Cen-
tralia a fair show. And
the Southern Fat Man
says he’ll see them hanged
first.
Parliament is buried in thought over
a proposed law to give the tenants of absentee
landlords powers of compulsory purchase on an
independent valuation. When are the other five
provinces going to take the first steps towards
sitting down squash on the dreadful absentee ?
It is seriously alleged that Australia is
being flooded with silver coins privately manufac-
tured in Germany. As there is only about 4d
worth of silver in the shilling, a splendid profit
can be realised by making good silver coin on a
large scale, and as there are as good workmen oufc-
the Mint as in it, there is really no certainty
that 5 or 25 or 50 or 75 per cent, of our silver coin
isn t illicit. Any country which passes off 4d
worth of metal for Is. is bound to be rushed by
illicit coinage.
A number of letters have been appearing
in the Maoriland papers recently, over the signa-
ture ‘Anna P. Stout” (the lady who lately
apologised for Chinamen). As they have con-
sisted j largely of rant about “ Purity of Govern-
ment, “Corruption of the Democracy, ” and so
on, they have much amused the public, who
remember how Stout cut up under George
l< isher, when charged by that member with having
been the most utterly corrupt administrator the
country had seen.”
A bill to make education free in N.S.W.
was thrown out in the Council by a five-to-one
majority, the Ministry rolling up its followers to
wipe out the proposal. The reason always given
in such cases is that the Treasury can’t afford to
lose the ,£50,000 a year or so which it makes out
u weekly threepences of the poorer classes —
the classes who mainly use the State schools
And the mystery of how it can afford to lose
<£sl\ooo a year by carrying newspapers free is
nev r by any chance explained.

According to a Melb. daily, Westralian
Premier Forrest has bought one or two choice
allotments in Ballarat, because he has an idea that
that city will be the Federal capital. Thoughtful
of Forrest thus to patriotically secure a site for
the Parliament - building before the property -
owners of the Garden City smell a rat and swell
the prices.

porters are wavering in their allegiance; and, last
but not least, a certain well-known ex-Opposition-
-Ist is quartered in Bundaberg, becoming popular,
and has aspirations for ’99.
SHOULD DRINK WITH SCIENCE.
SHE: “ My husband always gets drunk when he
comes to your place. I don't think he drinks
much. Perhaps you give him bad liquor."
PUBLLCAN: “ It’s not the quantity nor bad quality
of the liquor that makes him drunk, ma'am.
If he wants to avoid fights and keep sober, he
should drink with science."
The German press has been carefully
counting up to England for unrighteousness her
sympathy with Denmark in ’GS, with Austria in
’66, and with France in 70, and the Cologne
Gazette mutters darkly about what Germany
may have to say when the inevitable Anglo-
Russian crisis supervenes over Afghanistan. John
Bull's nearest approaches to ehivalric national
sentiment are precisely what Sauerkraut hates him
most for—as being farthest removed from the hog-
element, which the Englishman and the German
possess in common.

The general argument in N.S.W. Legis-
lative Council for rejecting the bill for free
education was that free education never ex-
isted in N.S.W., and that parents should be
compelled to pay at least a small sum in acknow-
ledgement of their responsibilities. And yet free
schools were started in Sydney in the first years
of the century. One large one was opened in
1810, and the duty of teaching the children at
State expense was clearly admitted by the Govern-
ment of the time. In 1804 a school was estab-
lished on the Hawkesbury, and as Government
funds were limited at the time, a special tax of
2a. an acre was imposed on the surrounding land-
owners to make good the teacher’s salary.
Melbourne magistrate Panton is a man of
broad, sledgehammer ideas. Giving evidence
bt‘tore Vic. Law Reform Commission, he sugges-
ted, as a valuable reform, that the right of.prison-
ers to appeal from the magistrates’ court and have
their cases reviewed by a judge should be abolished..
I lie privilege of appeal, Panton held, was “ very
undesirable.” If it was abolished, then the beak
could leave his muddy boot-marks and his hiccup,
all over the judicial records, and, if he was more
obfuscated than usual, and sentenced the plaintiff
in a small debt matter to 12 months, under the
impression that it was a manslaughter case, the
sentence would, presumably, have to stand. And
then there would be a blessed sense of rest and
security about the beak industry.

They run the party-government machine
after a fine, high-handed fashion in Maoriland
Four Liberal members, who held that Premier
Seddon should not be the paid servant of a British
mining syndicate, and voted to that effect, have
been thrown out of the Ministerial caucus and
have received information that they will hence-
forth be regarded as Oppositionists and Tories of
the worst description. The spectacle of a Premier
using his Ministerial power to compel his fol-
lowers to approve of his private jobs and syndi-
cates isn’t edifying, and Seddon’s definition of a
Liberal as a man who is in favor of Seddon
making money any way he darned well pleases
isn t edifying, eilher. Premier Dick has strained
the party-government machine nearly to breaking
point by this tune, and of late he has taken to
hobnailed methods which will yet lead to disaster.
Ihe boot-marks of their vigorous leader are grow-
altogether too conspicuous on the aggregate
hide of the Liberal party.
Writes “Vindex,” from Launceston
Dear Bulletin,— Your article, ‘Tasmania on
loast, much misrepresents Mr. Inglis Clark’s
action in resigning the office of Tasmanian
Attorney-General when it says he did so because
his professional feelings were piqued. Here are
the true facts of the matter. Pour of Mr. Clark’s
colleagues had promised, behind his back,’to exer-
cise a power under an Act of Parliament, which
involved a legal interpretation of the Act as to
the extent of the power. When the matter was
brought under the Attorney-General’s notice, he
advised them that they had not the power to do
what they had promised, and that he could not be
a party to advising the Governor to exercise thf
power; and, further, that, if they insisted on
domg it, he would feel compelled to retire bom
the Cabinet. In the face of this intimation, how-
ever, they declined to accept a resolution moved
m the House of Assembly that they should be
guided by the Attorney-General’s opinion in the
matter ; and I think you will agree with me that,
111 these circumstances, no self-respecting man
could do otherwise than instantlv resign, as Mr
Clark did.”
Does Queensland propose that Nelson—
the unspeakable fool who went through the ac-
counts of the Q.N. Bank in 1893. and couldn’t see
a single item of the =£3,000,000 of losses which were
staring him in the face, and who thereby illus-
trated that he didn’t know enough of finance to
run a retail banana-basket—shall continue to be
Treasurer of the province much longer? Or that
Barlow, who went through the accounts with him
and also saw nothing, shall remain in the Ministry?
Or that M‘llwraith, of the eight stinkful over-
drafts, and the .£200,000 of bad debts, and the
Mt. Perry copper mystery, shall remain in the
t ln [ e • r ? And hoUr do the policy-holders
of the Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society
feel with Boyd D. Morehead—one of the child-like
directorate which never knew anything, and which
didn t begin to suspect anything even when the
stink of its Q.N. Bank had been audible all over
Australia for years—helping to look after their
interests as a director ? Every institution which
has one of the old Q.N. Bank crowd helping to
direct or manage or audit it should shunt him
straight away for the sake of its own good name.
1 fc .Jj own showi ng one of that crowd—the one
who is dead was a rogue, and the rest were fools
and incompetent, and any concern which trusts
its interests to any one of them for the rest of their
natural lives, deserves whatever may happen to it.

“The Suffield Case,” a 64 pp. pamphlet
containing the evidence at the police-court and at
the trial in this notorious case, with a commentary
and plan, is now published. Post-free from
Bulletin Office, 7d. in stamps. Proceeds, after
expenses paid, go to help Suffield.
Journalist John Norton is a candidate for
Sydney city aldermanship. The mind staggers
under the contemplation of what John’s unspeak-
ableness would be jn full Mayoral fig, giving some
recalcitrant city father “rats,” and laying down
the gospel according to St. John.
Miss Sutherland, of Melb. philantlii’opic
repute, recently interviewed in London concern-
ing the evil ways of that city, prescribed fiery
destruction as a desperate but necessary remedy.
This seems to have partially arrived, but unfor-
tunately Smellbourne shares in the physic, thus
marring an otherwise hairy and convincing narra-
tive.
Sydney’s shade-temperature on Saturday,
20th Nov., came within 3d eg. of the record for
that month, and was itself a record for continuity
of heat, remaining above 90dqg. for eight succes-.
sive hours, and standing, shortly after noon, at
99‘4 at the Observatory—equivalent to about 110
in the lower parts of the city. Highest country
register was 111 at Wjleanniu and Nymagee; at
Brewarrina and Leadville 110 was reached, and
Yass had 98 at 10 o’clock at night.
The Bulletin has been asked to draw
attention to the fact that a movement is on foot
to provide some .useful memorial of the late Baron
Yon Mueller. The committee which has been
formed for the purpose, and of which Frof. Bald-
win Spencer is secretary, is quite distinct from
that which has to do with the projected mortuary
memorial. However desirable that may be from a
private point of view, the Baron’s world wide
scientific reputation, Professor Spencer points out,
calls for something besides, both in the way of a
commemorative bust or medallion and of an en-
dowed prize or medal, to be appropriately allotted
to distinction in botanical or horticultural work in
the various Australian provinces. Subscriptions
may be forwarded to treasurer, College of Phar-
macy, Melb., or to the secretaries, Melbourne
University.
A Sydney Chamber of Commerce deputa-
tion interviewed Premier Reid on Monday re the
proposed stamp and probate duties, and that emi-
nent financier gave in on every point where the
deputation remonstrated at all strongly—save
one. When it asked him not to charge probate-
duty on very small estates which were the sole
support of the widow and the undeveloped orphan,
the fat man, whose mission it is to shift the bur-
dens off the poor, was adamant. The deputation
suggested that the widow who is left’ with £lOO0 —
equal to about 15s. a week income—or from that
down to .£5O, should be relieved from special
taxation, but Reid “ didn’t see it.” Then he was
asked if there wasn’t some difference between a
case where, say, £2OO is left to a bereaved wife as
the only thing between her and the Benevolent
Asylum or the streets, and the case where the
same sum is left to charity;—possibly the society
for converting the Chinese, and if it wasn’t ad vis.
able to exempt the former recipient from a heavy
special tax. And Reid briefly said that he didn’t
see it.
Th 6 Age, in a recent leader on its.critics,
speaks of a copy of a despised contemporary that
“we happened to intercept on its way to the
butterrnan.” That is not wise. It is the unfor-
tunate fate of all papers to arrive at the butter-
man, and the public only intercepts and reads
them en route.

Proposed in S.A. to remove tlje break of
gauge on the Northern line from Terowie to Peters-
burg; at the latter place the line branches into
three—one going to Broken Hill, the other to Ft.
Pirie, while the third goes into the far north.
Apart from other considerations, the change would
give starved-out farmers and their teams some-
thing to do, and so is being barracked for.
In the Boer republic they lately appointed
an Inspector of Offices, and his first report was
discussed last month. As a proof of one of the
things of which the Rand new-comers complain
the document is pretty strong. The discovered
embezzlements for the year amount to .£18,590.
One Mining Commissioner’s clerk got away with
•£(1000, a Native Commissioner with .£lO,BOB, and
others had “cuts’’of from .£BOO down to a few
miserable sovereigns. And now Kruger wants to
borrow a million or two. ,
Melb. Herald publishes the 170-line
blank verse ode, written and recited by Father J.
J. Malone, at the reception of the R.C. dignitaries
present at the opening of St. Patrick’s Cathedral.
The remarkable thing about' the ode is that the
Bible verse. (First Epistle of John,- v. 4) which
heads it, is part of a chapter very Anglican and not
at all Roman, rind ending, “Little children keep
yourselves from idols.” The ode itself is absolutely
undenominational and might have been written
by Bishop Coe, or some other Anglican poet, con-
cerning St. Paul’s.
A S.A. return lately compiled shows, in a
striking way, the effect of the varying rainfall in
the North. In 1884 there was 13in. rain at Pt.
Augusta (the chief port for the North); next year
2,117,b1iS bushels of wheat were shipped thence.
Next year there were 9in., badly distributed, and
18,108 bushels were shipped. In 1888 there was
Gin., and 149,837 bushels were shipped in 1889
14in. fell, and the export was 1,858,408 bushels.
But, while 2,117,506 bushels were exported from
the port in 1885, the aggregate of the past three
years has been only 1,899,170 bushels.
The death of Chas. A. Dana, of New York
SUN, was cabled the other day. A few weeks ago,
when within two years of 80, he was well to the
front in the big journalistic fray started by the
World and the Journal over their rival con-
trivances for posting-up results of big base-ball
matches, one of the features of which was the
World’s offer of a tandem-bike and a free Euro-
pean tour to the most popular cycliste in New
York—to be chosen by coupon-voting. A great
circulation-increase resulted, but when the Jour-
nal came out with an answering offer of two
sterling silver tandems, and free European trips
to 10 wheelwomen and 10 wheelmen, it ran its cir-
culation up to 3,000,000.

G. H. Reid, Premier of N.S.W., by his
fast budget speech, showed himself to be the
champion figure-manipulator of the world ; but in
the Strand Arcade, Sydney, is to be seen a speci-
men of figure-manipulation, so to speak, that goes
one better than even the stout little man’s, for his
very own figure, eyeglass and all, worked in gold-
wire, is there presented with startling faithfulness.
A gold portrait is surely the most fitting compli-
ment that can be paid a paper financier; and the
public are invited to inspect the above and many
other beautiful specimens of Carl Stockle, the
lightning gold-wire artist’s work. He makes over
100 unique and original designs of jewellery from
gold wire while you wait, and the prices charged
are so reasonable and the goods themselves so
dainty and novel that it is not surprising to learn
that already the artist is experiencing difficulty in
supplying all his orders. For Xmas presents his
jewellery is specially suitable, and the public are
requested to place their orders at the earliest op-
portunity, otherwise execution of same cannot be
guaranteed.*

The deduction of a Vic. income-tax from the
stakes won by visiting horse-owners is easily made to look
ridiculous. For instance, why not demand income-tax
from the profits earned by a Sydney bookmaker in his
Victorian betting on each and every occasion when they
exceed t 200? If a Sydney man in Melbourne were to
arrange a sequence of ten tosses with a local gambler for
£lOOO a toss, and were to lose six times out of ten, why
shouldn’t he be taxed on each of his four wins? Presum-
ing that an owner, who doesn’t fancy nis horse, chooses
to “ hedge ” by laying against his chance to the amount
of the stake-money, is he to pay tax on the stake if his
animal should come in first? And if not. why not ?
* » * '*
“Maluma, in the Duke of York Stakes, lost
£3OOO of Mrs. Langtry’s capital,” writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent; “ and the Prince of Wales also
dropped a couple of hundred on the Australian. The
stable was very confident, going on the results of the
trials with Angelos, but the mare did not look too well at
the post, and after the Newmarket Handicap distance had
been covered she fell back and finished fifth, the Ameri-
can Diakka winning as he liked. It was a great dis-
appointment to the antipodean contingent who consoled
themselves by saying Angelos was a ‘ thief,’ and wouldn’t
' really try in the trials—hence the over-estimate of
Maluma’s fitness,” ....
“Gallantly indeed did* Merman avenge his
stable companion’s defeat,” continues the writer, “ and
Mrs. Langtry’s Oesarevvitch will live until an Australian
horse wins the Epsom Derby. . ‘.Mr. Jersey ’ was in
great luck altogether. Invited into the Jockey Club en-
closure—whence she had been excluded for over a twelve-
month —at the express wish of her old boy, Albert
Edward, she, on her birthday, saw Merman win her over
£lB,OOO by one of the finest fought-out finishes ever seen
at Newmarket. Forty ydrds from home The Rush lead
by half a length, but the plucky Merman gained inch by
inch, and, thanks to Sharpies, put his neck in the judge’s
eye. The excitement was enormous.”
• * * *
The details of Merman’s’victory in the Cesare-
witch show that the Australian horse was probably the
second best of the competitors, although he carried only
7st, 51b. In a field of 22 he received weight from five of
them, the top-weight being The Rush (Bst. 31b.), who was
beaten by a neck. Two others carried more than Bst.
and were placed third and fourth—nearly five lengths
behind Merman.
• * • •
One pertinent question arises naturally from the
Parthenopseus case, and a dozen other Flemington “ rop-
ing” cases that have passed unnoticed. What,, in the
sacred name of Salary, is the use of the V.R.C. stipendiary
steward ? If he couldn’t see Parthenopaius running cronk,
what can he see ?
* * • •
A few months back, The Bulletin remembers,
it uttered a snort of contempt at the glorification of book-
makers Sam Allen and Tom Arnfield by the Melb. daily
press, in connection with a “ banquet” given to those two
“ leading members of the ring.”
» » * •
The V.R.C. ought to have donned the black cap
when sentencing bookmakers Allen and Arnfield, over the
Parthenopißiis affair. For two men whose world is sport,
and sport alone, to be utterly shut out of the world’s
racing centres, and denied the places where sportsmen
foregather, is death indeed.
» * « •
What has Parthenopseus done that he should be
wiped out for life? He’s a clever, well-meaning, and
thoroughly honest horse, and his parents were perfectly
respectable, law-abiding’ people.
♦** • *
The vaporings of Ikey Mo and Co., just prior
and subsequent to a big race, are excruciatingly amusing.
From their utterances an outsider would be led to believe
that their sole dealings are in ponies and hundreds, with
occasional monkeys “just to oblige owners,” whereas
their real counters are the “ casers,” sovs. and half-sovs.
of the ever-to-be-bled public. When approached by an
owner or his accredited agent they shut up like a tele-
scope. In very truth, they bet in marbles now when com-
pared with the times of Joe Thompson.

cap on the Saturday before the Cup. The tale goes that
the horse, running very wide, got so close to the abat-
toirs that one slaughterman called out to another, “ ’E’s
looking for a bit o’ meat, Bill; throw ’im a pluck.” •
» * • *
It is related that on the day, a year or two ago
when a certain big Melb. scandal was revealed, one of the
principals strolled into David Svme’s sanctum and
abruptly announced, “ Look ’ere, Syire, we wancher not
to publish that there business of me and -Ikey Solomon.”
The autocrat looked up with an amazed stare. “Hoot
non," he cried, “ A shall publeesh just what a like.” The
other scratched his head. “Me an Ikev alius bought the
Age,” he said, reproachfully, “I think it’s on’y a fair
thing to keep it out.” King David grew - indignant.
“ Leave ma office,” he cried. The other started to obey.
He had his hand on- the door when a thought struck him.'
“ Look ’ere, S.vme, old man,” he said, with a smile of
certainty, “If you keep your mouth shut I’ll stand ver a
fiver, and I’ll guarantee Ikey ain’t particular to a bob or
two himself.”
* * * *
Had the Britishers seen Mr. .Tack Hagerty
despatch his 2ft horses per starting-machine in the Welter
Handicap at Moorefield on Saturday, they would have
seen that the machine is bound to' coine in England. In
France it is declared a success, and several owners are
having machines erected on their private training-tracks.
* * * »
“Carandtni’s starting-machine, exhibited in one
race at Newmarket,” writes Tub Bulletin’s London cor-
respondent, “ was not a success, the horses objecting to
standing behind ; while the riders, opposed to innovation,
sneered outright, and helped the failure. English flying-
starts accustom the horses to be on a restless move, and
handicap the gate here, so Corlett, of the Pink ’Un, sug-
gests it should be introduced to 2-year-clds only at’first,
2 and 3-year-olds the second year, and so on. The Sports-
man people, who are financially backing it, plump thick,
strong and often for the new starter, and there is no
country where it is wanted more than here, where the
starting is cruel.”
* • * •
The hill at Moorefield may have something to
do with “ reversals of form ” and consequent downing of
backers, but certain it is that the course is proverbially a
good one for the layers of odds. On Saturday last, of the
six winners two started at 10 to 1, and one each at 8 and
7 to 1 respectively.
* • • •
To the outsider, it seems an extraordinary state
of afFairs that a double-shop kteper should be heavily
fined whilst another man, at the same game, on the same
day, in the same street, almost immediately opposite, was
openly doing a business which needed three clerks to keep
pace with. What say the police?
* * * *
A certain influential club down South shows a
profit for the year just closed of over ±2OOO, no small part
of which has been earned by the poker-machine and the
racing totalisator, both run bv the “club.”
» * * *
To-day (Sat.),'some good racing should be seen
at Randwick. Aboriginal seems to have a fair chance of
winning the hurdles, whilst Princess Arena and Tripos
look well in the Course Handicap. Liska will likely be
the best youngster in the Nursery Stakes, and, unlucky
as he 'has been of late, the Captain may win the Club
Handicap. The High-weight Handicap should fall to
Sayles in the probable absence of the imported Voyou,
who will win a race ere long.
* * * *
Lillie Bridge (Sydney) racing-law is a bit mixed.
The rider of a pony the other night declared 31bs. over,
but the clerk of the scales called ‘2lbs. The pony won, but
was disqualified when weighed in for the alleged declaring
of only 21bs. over, and the race was awarded to the second
pony. The disqualified pony when weighing out for a
race later in the night, was ordered to carry a 71bs,
penalty, as winner of the race for which he was disquali-
fied.
* • * *
Barbara, one of the jumpers taken to England
by Mr. H. Bartlett, brought 100 guineas, Everton falling
to a hid of 80 guineas. The Childe Harold stallion. Era,
did not sell, and it is to he hoped he comes back here.
Era is a model as regards conformation and action, and
would worthily take the place of his deceased sire.

“Real lydie,” who had backed* Amberite, to

* * * •
Geo. Giffen was in wonderful fettle with Ithe
ball against Vic. in Adelaide. “ Qiflf.” vowed at the start
of the season that he would abandon bowling this year,
but he will change his mind now. He is in better form
with the ball than with the bat.
• * • *
Jack Lyons is a cool, sober batsman now. In
his first innings against Victoria he played just the same
game as he did against England. There was none of the
slogging of old, but sound, correct, powerful hatting.
“Carbine," his friends'think, cannot now be omitted
from the first test game.
» * * »
Victoria is very weak in bowling this year, and
is not too strong in batting. Ciller, one of the new men,
promises well all-round, and M'Michael is a batsman
.with a future, but the Sheffield Shield this year should be
battled for by N.S.W. and S.A. An illustration of weak-
ness is the fact that there is not a single Victorian
with a certainty of being in the first test! Trott arid
Truinble way get in, but they are neither of them cer-
tainties. Trott, by the way, is stated to be very unwell
and not fit to be playing cricket.
* • * *
The newspapers throughout Australia seemed
determined from the first to evolve a Ranjitsinhji boom,
and every mention of the dusky slogger was assiduously
scissored for republication. One Sydney daily printed a
lengthy article, with “ splash ” headings, and the sub-
editor was astonished to find that the interesting informa-
tion had previously been clipped by a firm of biscuit-
makers, and embodied in an advt. which had been running
for close on three weeks in the same up-to-date journal.
» * » •
N. : “Why is Mr. Ranjitsinhji styled Prince
in the Australian cricket scores, whereas, in England, he
is in variably ‘K. S. Ranjitsinhji?’ Reminds me again of
Higjnbotham’s remark about how much more Conserva-
tive Australia is than England. I heard Coppin say the
same after his last trip to London.”
* « * •
Veteran Charley Lawrence (now coaching the
colts for the Melbourne Cricket-club), writing to a friend
in Sydney, remarks that Ranjitsinhji is just such another
bat as Syd. Gregory, and there is little between them,
barring Gregory being the faster scorer. Lawrence
thinks the Britishers a strong team, but he reckons they
will be ou(plassed by all Australia.

WALK UP!
“ What a queer little walk Ranjitsinhji has got! ”
Remarked a sweet maid to her mate ;
Be replied : “ Well, as Ranji’s the draw of the lot,
’Tis largely a matter of gate."
* * * *
Managements of Vic. cricket continues funny.
Beecham, of Fitzroy, with every claim to be chosen to go
to Adelaide, was omitted until Trumble found he couldn’t
leave Melbourne. In Adelaide Beecham was left out of
the playing eleven, and Laver, but recently arrived from
the American “ baseball ” funeral, stepped in. Laver is
one of the “ selectors ” of the team.
* » * *
Johns got little or no praise for his admirable
wicket-keeping in Melb. against the Englishmen. His
chief; fault is a mannerism of throwing up both hands
after the batsman has played the ball, as if he had taken
a catch. It looks like a reminder to the crowd that Johns
is alive and well.
For Paddington against South Sydney, on
Saturday, Trumper played a perfect innings for 125. In
the match v. England, fortune (in the shape of a lightning
catch by Ranji), in the first innings, was dead against the
lad; should he continue to pile up his centuries in clulj
cricket, that catch should not stand against him re his
chance of inclusion versus Pye’s, which appears to he the
bone of contention in certain quarters. Last Saturday,
Trumper scored 125 as against Pye’s 9, and it will be
interesting to keep up a weekly comparison between the
pair for future reference by the selection committee.
* * * *
The English cricketers are both amused and in-
dignant with the Sydney crowd. Amused at the Ranji-
worship, and indignant at the brutal chivvying of Garrett
« * • *
From “ Noss ” Iredale’s cricket notes in the
Australian Star : —“There were many in the pavilion on
Saturday who were ready to cry ‘ Peccavi’ at the chances
of N.S. Wales holding her own with the English team.”
It would evidently be better for “ Noss ” to stick to the
vernacular in his “ cries.” Also, is it not in questionable
taste for a player to write criticisms on his comrades or
opponents while the contest is proceeding ?
* * * V
The Englishmen’s batting averages for the three
“colony” matches leave Ranji. at the head of the list
with 97. Storer is a close second with an average of 94 ;
Mason, 02 : Maclaren, 50. The others have done nothing
in particular, but Hearne happens to cut a respectable
-figure on paper with a total of 39 runs for four innings,
and only one “ out.”
mm • •
Megson had an easy task in the Vic. Brassard
contests. The one mile he won by 3J lengths, and if he
didn’t beat Forbes by more than half-a-length in the ten
miles it was solely because he was satisfied that that was
enough. The moral of the match is that Forbes is not in
the same class as the Sydneyite, and that probably Walne
is better than either.
A huge farce was perpetrated by having the men
paced by single machines, which amounts to about the
same thing as having a racehorse paced by a cow. Meg-
son and Forbes contesting the Vic. championship are
naturally assumed to be the two best men in Victoria,
and yet the league officials turn out or permit a lot of third-
classers on the tracks ostensibly to help the cracks along.
Multicycle pacing would have given Forbes a better show,
for, as a stayer, he might bother Megson in a fast-ridden
race ; but with such snails’ speed as that set by singles a
sprinter like Megson will score every time.
Bilmartin made his first reappearance since the
Adelaide episode, jumping about the stand with a snap-
shot camera. The gentle Bill has been letting his
moustache grow—evidently as a gauge of defiance to any-
body who might hint he was clean-shaved through his
gaoi experiences. Parsons was also about again after his
operation, and threatens to race within six months. He
busied himself exchanging ’Murkanisms with Nankivell,
the artist, who w-as himself a racing pedaller in Melb. six
years ago.
* * * *
Re Bill Martin having a “plugger’s brain,” a
large and foolhardy friend of The Bulletin authorises it
to say there is nothing the matter with Bill’s alleged
brain. Nine out of Bill’s 10 component parts are bull-dog
courage and ferocity. His only idea of an argument is to
turn on the other party and rend him. He is the ideal
Roman gladiator, and if he knew as much about boxing
as he does of cycling, he would be the dandiest fighter i hat
ever chewed tobacco, and would beat any middle-weight
on earth.
* • • *
The paced hero Lesna, who did Australia so
picturesquely with a retinue of retainers, has gone down
three times before Michael in America. The men met in
three matches over 20, 25 and 33 miles, and Michael wal-
loped out the Frenchman by four yards, a lap and four
laps respectively. Michael was, however, afterwards
beaten by the Yankee Starbuck, whom Lesna defeated
soon after he arrived in ’Murka.
* * • *
Peter Jackson is now in America ready to take
on any of the big ones, Corbett for choice. W. W.
Naughton, than whom there is no better judge, says
the blackfellow has not altered a bit in appearance, and
ridicules the reports of Jackson as a listless and worn-out
man. He still affects the tall, silk hat, gold-headed um-
brella, and fashionable overcoat.
* * * •
“ Joe Goddard, seen in Fleet-street, looked very
fit and strong," writes The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent. “He has just left for the States to meet
Sharkey or Fitzsimmons, and be looms big and ferocious
Suffield and “Justice.”
In N.S.W. Assembly, the other day, when the
estimates of the Justice Dept, were under con-
sideration, some members pled the cause of
Thomas Suffield—the alleged brutal criminal to
whom the Justice Dept, offers liberty before the
expiration of his sentence, the astonishing alleged
criminal who refuses the offer because he desires
liberty less than the enquiry which he believes
will restore his good name.
The narrow, callous Minister Gould rebuffed
protest with his insolently and ignorantly reiter-
ated assurance that there is no ground for an en-
quiry into Suffield’s case.
The net brutality of this rejoinder may be in-
ferred from the following facts :
1. Suffield was accused of outraging a woman,
and was condemned to ten years’ penal servitude,
on the evidence oj that woman alone.
2. Although he was a stranger to that woman,
had only once seen her, had never spoken to her.
3. Although he was an educated man of high
character, and had ample opportunities for grati-
fying his passions, if he so desired, without using
brutal violence to a stranger.
4. Although two witnesses swear that at the
tume of the alleged assault he.was in his bedroom,
which he could not leave without their knowledge
and did not leave.
5. Although his clothes and person bore no
marks whatsoever, though the woman—strong and
healthy—said she struggled with him for a long
time on wet ground, and was herself bruised
frightfully, while her clothes were torn in a
manner which only great force could have accom-
plished.
6. Although, if Suffield. committed the assault,
ho was such a fool as to let his victim pass by a
lonely bush road nearly a mile long, up to a spot
at most 78 yards from her employer’s windows
and there assault her about ten o’clock in the
evening, just when she was expected home, before
the household had gone to bed—and continue his
outrage though her screams were immediately
answered from the house !
7. Although the woman on whose sole evidence
he was convicted at first attributed her injuries to
the attack of a bull—which afterwards similarly
attacked, and similarly injured , another woman
and was shot for it.
9.- Although' the woman declared she was un-
conscious when according to the evidence of her
employer, his son, and a doctor, she was not un-
conscious.
9. Although the woman declared at the pre-
liminary hearing’ that she left for home at a cer-
tain time, apd declared at the trial that she did
not leave until 20 minutes after that time—appa-
rently in order to make her evidence tally with
that of other witnesses at the preliminary hearing.
10; Although there was no absolutely-conelusive
medical evidence of assault by a man ; and the
doctor who examined the woman an hour after the
alleged assault, and again the following morning
refused to swear she had been assaulted by a man!
11. Although the woman—though she was, ac-
cording to the evidence of two witnesses, quite
conscious when found, 10 minutes after the alleged
assault—said not a word to indicate that Suffield
had assaulted her; nor did she accuse Suffield to
the doctor who examined her and found her per-
fectly conscious one hour after the alleged assault.
It was only on the following morning that she said
a man had assaulted her; and it was some
hours later that she said Suffield had assaulted her.
e the woman’s identification of
Suffield was positively made after seeing him once
only, several weeks before, standing in a street—
and that when her companion swears his back was
turned to them ! She could identify Suffield at 10
o clock on a cloudy moonlight night, and swears
to him; yet when she passed him in broad day-
light, the same morning, she could not swear to
him—she could only say it was a man “ very like
him ” ! J
13. Although a score of other circumstances go
to show that the girl made a mistake, or was
suffering from sexual hallucination caused by the
shook of the bull’s attack.
_ 14. Although section 383 of the N.S. Wales
Criminal Law Amendment Act enacts that when
any doubt or question arises ” as to a prisoner’s
guilt an enquiry may be held before any justice of
the peace—and in Suffield’s case there are a hun-
dred doubts and questions.
The Bulletin still hopes that Parliament will
force, the Justice Department to hold an open
enquiry into Suffield’s case, allowing him to be
represented by counseiand to call witnesses whose
evidence has apparentbeen suppressed. If jus-
tice according to law is still denied to Suffield,
The Bulletin will appeal to the Judges of the
Supreme Court of N.S. Wales, any one of whom
has the legal right to order such an enquiry.

All Heaven laughed the other day to hear
a Victorian M.L.C. telling Randolph Bedford that
he oughtn’t to wear a wide straw hat over a frock-
coat.
It is good to see right rewarded. A recent
example is the way small wool-growers are appre-
ciating the equitable innovation lately introduced
by Pitt, Sob & Badgery, Ltd., of charging the Same
rate of commission on little clips as on large con-
signments. A rigid observance-of the truism that
woolbrokers should have no interests calculated to
conflict with their duty to obtain the highest
prices for their principals, is, no doubt, also a
factor of, popularity.* !
The hot weather is now upon us, turning
adipose tissue into perspiration, and pats of butter
into,oil. By way of preventing this latter annual
calamity, Brandt Bros., of 236 Pitt-street, Sydney,
place their improved butter-cooler on the market.
The cooler, which has proved a perfect success, is
worked by the capillary action of water, no
chemicals or ice being required, and the hotter the
weather the cooler the butter grows, owing to in-
creased evaporation of water.*
The progressiyenpss of Messrs. Harrington
and Company, Ltd., of 66 Ring-street, Sydney,
is such that the firm has added another warehouse
to its premises; and this new establishment in
Kent-street, Sydfiey, is used entirely for bulk
stock and manufacturing. This firm is offering
two splendid new cameras, the “Victor” pocket
camera, and the “ Duplex ” hand camera. The
“\ i .tor uses plates, thus doing away with two-
thirds the cost and all the risks of films; its
mechanism is as perfect as a large Camera ; it is
praised everywhere, and its cost is only one guinea.
The “ Duplex ” is a twin lens hand camera ; has
the distinct Advantage of having a full-size finder,
is eminently suited for cyclists, and is most simple
in movement. It costs only 255.*

* * * *
Bike-poio is catching ori rapidly in England,
knd presently li will follow to Australia the road-roller-
skate performed upon in Sydney the other day; and then
will come more and more hike and roller developments,
and all."sorts of other weird locomotion-fakes till the life
of the mere man-upon-feet shall be rendered a burden to
him. In a Sydney suburb the other day Tub Bulletin
saw a larrikin standing on roller-skates at one end of a
long stretch of asphalt, and holding a string attached to
his dog’s collar, and when the larrikin cried “Go 1” the
dog went for all he was worth, tooling his principal along
behind him at a pace that is dead sure forthwith to pro-
duce other tyke-dragged boys-on-wheels, and much
scorching, and then tracks, and prizes, and carnivals, and
a new terror to existence generally. The boy-and-dog
race —it is coming.
• * * •
Ten years hence. S.M. Herald news-summary
paragraph : “ The Japanese team of cricketers arrived by
mail-steamer yesterday. They are a small, but wiry, and
tough-looking lot. They comprise a nephew to the
Mikado and two titled players. Their two best bowlers
are said to be faster than English Richardson or Jones
ever were, and several of their hats are as good as Ranji.
They should give a better account of themselves than the
English team that visited us last year.”
* * * *
“Ascending a long and rough hill, the other
day,” writes a bikist to The Bulletin, “ I overtook two
stout elderly ladies perspiring up on heavy machines.
The first one I passed made no sign ; the second, about 20
yards ahead, evidently mistook me for her companion,
and, as she heard my wheel behind her, spake without
raising her head from the handle-bars : “ Say, Maggie 1
Damn the roads!’ ‘By all means 1’ said I, cordially.
Don’t think 1 ever before saw a woman get off her machine
in such a hurry.”
• • • *
At Samoa, where cricket-matches are played
with 100 aside, the team threatened with defeat detaches
a portion of the field to hold a prayer-meeting. The idea
is that a licking at cricket is a sign of divine wrath, and
it is necessary to turn that aside ere victory can he
gained.
* * * *
A good old Wellington (M.L.) sport, who is
also something in the Mercantile line, recently found that
his trusted Cashier was about £lOOO short in his cash.
Most rtiert would have simply sent for a policeman, but
the old man Had never shot a bird sitting, and his sports-
manlike: irtstirtcts Revolted. So he told the defaulter that
he could have lour days’ start, and at the end of that
time the force would be put on his track, and if he didn’t
keep far enough ahead he would have to stand the con -
sequences. The cashier promptly ran for his life, and
ho\y the police are after him, and the genial old man is
tnakirig a book among his friends on the race. His sym-
pathies apparently lie with the cashier, possibly because
fie feels that he gave him rather a short handicap ; any-
how he is backing his vanished employe.

A penitent bookmaker has been discovered ; in
fact, he discovered himself in a letter to one of the Lon-
don police-court missionaries. A recent raid on the
betting-houses seems to have shaken up the “ metalli-
cian’s ” conscience, for he wrote at once to the missionary
—“ I have found betting-men such a bad lot that I want
to give up all racing and everything appertaining thereto.
This raid business has but hurried my decision. Oh, the
horror of being in the dock with those men 1 As I have
said, I am not without means, but my object in writing
you is to get right away from my associates ; to secure a
berth where t might commence life anew, as it Were, and
to have a whole-souled man to look to as a friend.” THe
Bulletin begins to wonder whether Alf. Joseph, or Jack
Cohen, or Charlie Westbrook ever yearhs for salvation.
* * * *
The pressmen who prate about the orderliness
and sobriety of Cup crowds at Flemington must keep
Carefully aWay from the back of the Hill or the Flat near
the Cartiage-paddock after 4.30 or 5 p.m. On the latter
Spot the drunks most do congregate, and Writer has seen
there exhibitions of Bestial drunkenness and candid
brutality such as could not be outdone fob grossness in
any CroWd oh earth. This Ctip-day provided One cheerful
episode, at least —a crowd of beery larrikins and towsled,
blear-eyed girls with slavering lips and torn hair, squeal-
in# and cursing about a pal and his donah fighting in the
centre. The drunken larrikin was chastising his'gijl with
heavy blows, hef face was‘out afid puffed, and black with
bruises, and she gabbled shrill blasphemy, and dug at the
pal’s cheeks With feline ’claws, tearing double furrows
wherever she struck. Malf-an-hour later the scribe came
upon the pair again : they sat in the grass, supported by
a fence, their arms twined about each other’s necks, sleep-
ing lovingly, peacefully, like babes in the wood, their,
gory, blackened faces turned up to the setting sun.

The Bulletin allows “Pressman” (Melb.)
to have his say
Dear Bulletin,-— Your par. of Nov. 6 re the .defunct
Vic. Reporters’ Association and Randolph Bedford is
really all wrong. The beginning of the Association was a
combination of men on the Age staff who had a grievance.
Then it grew by the addition of men on the staffs of other
daily papers. It wg,s formed solely for the purpose of
fighting grievances froin which only regular members of
a daily paper staff could suffer—it was an association of
working reporters. Such'a thing as a protestive and pro-
tective combination of reporters had never beem dreamt
of by the proprietors, and once the Association was
established with carefully-considered and comprehensive
rules the evils at which it aimed vanished. Then, having
done its work, it became merely ornamental, and pro-
prietors, editors and “leaders” of staffs were admitted,
and were only too glad to join. The Association then
■was more social than fighting, and—the more the merrier.
Ultimately it died, but there was no revival of the
grievances which it Was formed to fight. Randolph Bed-
ford, at the time, was not a member of the reporting
staff of any of the papers, and, therefore, was not eligible
for admission to the Association. If it had been.a jour-
nalists’ club, Bedford would certainly have been most
welcome, and I am only one of many who would have
been very glad to nominate him, but the reasons
for the Association’s existence did not affect him in the
slightest—luckily for him. I could nCvet understand
why a brilliant, original writer like Bedford should feel
so keenly (even to this day) his non-admission to an asso-
ciation of mere reporters of other people’s words. The
association was not “ a conspiracy to deny membership
to the brighter class of press-workers.” After one meet-
ing the brighter class would have said : “We have
nothing at stake here. These fellows are fighting a battle
peculiarly their own.” You call the members “dullards.”
All the more reason why Bedford should have kept aloof
of his own will. There were other men in exactly the
same position as he, but they never worried, simply
because they stopped to find out and understand. You
say that the association is “ long since dead and forgotten
as far as Melbourne journalism is concerned.” This again
is wrong. One of the most useful things the Association
did was to institute badges for reporters, for use as pass-
ports with the police, firemen, &c., in big crowds when
titpe was the essence of the contract. So, in a very em-
phatic way, to anyone who is a daily paper reporter, the
Association, though dead, is by no means forgotten. De
mortuis nil nisi verum. —many of the most practical sug-
gestions emanated from the late Harry C. Rhys-Jcnes, then
'on the Herald, and one or two men on the Age. In no way
was Bedford affected, save in his own imagination. And
' I’rii very certain that the alleged buttonholing and quota-
tion of his non-admission to the Association “ by an Aon
reporter ” was not a part of “ the Agf. plan to damage his
candidature for Melb. eastern suburbs.” Doesn’tit sound
a bit too “cheap,” on the face of itV—Yours, etc., Press-
man, P.S., 16th NoV., —Have just heard frotn R.B. him-
' self 6f' settlement to-day of his libel-action against the
. Age. Perhaps he forgets now about that non-admission
td a Reporters’ Association.

on” the N.S.W. Treasury authorities. He was
fined at Hay £lOO or three months for sly-grog
selling, but as one of the adjudicating J’sP. had
an interest in a whisky-mill, the verdict was upset

Announced that “a movement is on foot
for settling Polish and Russian Jews on the aban-
doned farm lands of Essex.” Essex is a fertile
district, and it reaches within five miles of London,
the biggest city and the biggest market on earth,
rv othing could much better illustrate the ruin of
British agriculture under Freetrade. By-and-bye,
Britain will be able to supply Bogan scrub-cutting
jobs for the unemployed right at the back-door of
London.
At a meeting of the China Inland Mission, held at a
Baptist church in Melb. the other night, Mr. Philip
Kitchen, lion, treasurer of the noble mission, was re-
ported as saying : “ He would draw the attention of the
meeting to the fact that only one out of every 1500 per-
sons on the earth was a Christian, and consequently the
were going to eternal perdition. There were
"u " ens dymg every minute, and during the time
they had spent in that church that evening some seven
01 eight thousand had gone to a hopeless grave and
entered a Christless eternity.”

An angel, from the azure skies,
Was contemplating life below,
“How sad,” he said, and wiped his eyes,
Whence many tears began to flow,
11 That only one-fifteenth per cent.
Of all the mortals I behold
Can hope to fly the Firmament
Surmounted by a crown of gold—
As statisticians tell.
“/cannot, sav whose head shall wear
A halo round about its crown :
I cannot guess which creatures there
Will soon be garbed in snow-white gown j.
I merely know I’m plunged in grief
To think that one, by grace divine,
Gets saved because of his Belief,
Whilst fourteen ’undred and ninety-nine
Are bound to go to H—l.
“I touch my harp and daily sing
The dirge of them whose souls are lost
For want of Faith whereby to cling
To Father, Son, and Holy Ghost.
They live and die without desire
To walk the narrow, winding way,
Their fate is everlasting fire.
Ping, Ping, the awful Judgment Hay—
O, Lord ! Thy will be done.
“ I tune iny harp to mournful strains
Whene’er reflection’s cud I chew ;
Hereafter seems so full of pains
For all except the Chosen Few,
Though each contestant in the race
Would like to catch the eye of God,
The price against him for a place
Is fourteen ’undred ninety odd,
Yes, ninety odd to one.”
