Every man in Australia should read the
record of the Suffield case just published by The
Bulletin in a 64 pp. pamphlet, post-free 7d. in
stamps. The proceeds, after payment of ex-
penses, go to the fund for assisting Suffield to have
his case heard at an open public enquiry.
Amy Sherwin was being serenaded by the
Adelaide Liedertafel opposite Parliament House
on the night the division was taken as to whether
the age at which youths are permitted to gamble
with bookmakers should be 18 or 21. Three sup-
porters of the 18-year limit stopped outside to listen
to the al fresco concert, and the result was that
Govt, carried the clause as it stood (fixing the age
at 21) by a majority of one vote.
The odor of a Du bee cigarette is like the
fragrant breath of a 17-year houri on the hills of
Paradise ; the taste is as tender as that houri’s
first innocent kiss ; and the solid satisfaction
which comes after smoking is the kind that arrives
when the Paradise-boss tells you there are 39 more
houris in reserve—all 17-year-old, and all yours.*

A woman who had successfully played man
for 43 years was lately discovered in England in
the person of “Charley Wilson,” a painter, who,
amongst other decorative work, executed the
ornamentation of the music-saloons of the P. and
O. steamers Victoria, Oceana, and Arcadia. She
had been a well-educated girl, and took to trousers
as a means of disappearing from the ken of an un-
congenial hubby.

Public Notices.^
PUBLIC SERVICE BOARD,
60 Young-street,
Sydney, 29th October, 1897.
A COMPETITIVE EXAMINATION of Candidates for
JUNIOR CLERKSHIPS in the Public Service of the
Colony will be held in Sydney and at various countrv
centres, which will be fixed as far as possible to suit the
convenience of candidates, on the 20th DECEMBER next,
and four following days. The estimated number of
appointments to be made is 25. Candidates must be not
less than 16 nor more than 21 years of age on the first day
of examination; of good moral character, and in sound
health. Persons already employed in the General Division
of the Public Service, will be admitted to the examination,
provided their age does not exceed thirty years.
The subjects in which the candidates will be examined
are Handwriting, English (dictation precis writing,
grammar, and composition), Arithmetic (full course),
Geometry (Euclid, First Book), outlines of Physical and
Political Geography, and the Geography of the Austral-
asian colonies in moderate detail ; English History from
the middle of the eighteenth century to the present time,
especially with reference to the colonies and depen-
dencies. Candidates will also be allowed to take any
two, but not more than two, of the following subjects :
Mathematics, English—a prescribed author—(the same as
that prescribed for the Junior Examination of the current
year—Tennyson’s “ Gareth and Lynette ” (MacMillan),
Latin, French, German, Elementary Chemistry, and
Elementary Geology. Application to attend the examin-
ation must be made on a form for the purpose, w'hieh can
be obtained at the office of the Board, and must reach
the undersigned not later than the Ist December, 1897.
Candidates will be required to pay an examination fee
of 10s., which must be lodged with a clerk of petty sessions
or the Treasury, and the receipt therefor forwarded to
the Secretary of the Board with the form of application.
A list of registered candidates, with centres of examina-
tion, will be published .in the Government Gazette after
the time for the receipt of applications has expired, and
candidates will also be advised direct of the place at which
they will be examined. • The regulations and copies of the
papers set at the last examination can be obtained at the
Government Printing Office and the principal booksellers.
By order of the Board.
J. W. HOLLIMAN, Secretary.

for 1882 and 1893. Apply, Manager.

DYSPEPSIA and its treatment is receiving
much more than a share of attention from the
medical journalistic world. The British Phar-
maceutical Journal has published an article of
about four pages by the well-known Professor
I'inkier, going into a series of experiments com-
paring papain as a digestive ferment with pepsin,
and, in nearly every case, the papain results are
the most favorable. After showing the results of
the experiments, he winds up the article as fol-
lows :
Pepsine acts in the stomach, and not in the
intestines, as in the iatter the reaction is neutral or
alkaline. Papain has little effect in the stomach,
as the reaction there is acid ; when, however, the
stomach is neutral or alkaline, papain will pep-
tonise, while pepsine will be useless. The degree
of acid reaction in the stomach varies greatly,
especially in the case of a stomach out of order, in
which case the reaction can be so highly acid or
alkaline that pepsine would be of no value at all.
For the treatment of stomach catarrh we will,
however, find that as a general rule the contents
of the viscus have been rendered artificially neu-
tral or alkaline by the administration of remedies,
so that in these cases pepsine will have absolutely
no effect. Papain, on the contrary, will act ener-
getically. Regarding the time available for the
digestive ferment to act in the body, in the case of
pepsine this is necessarily very short, as the action
ceases when the food enters alkaline reaction of
the intestines. In the case of papain, which can
act well in alkaline reaction, the time is prac-
tically unlimited, as it continues acting on the
food during the whole time it remains in the body.
For all these reasons the conditions for the effect
in the human body are far more in favor of papain
than pepsine, and especially as under existing
oircumstances (high degree of concentration) pap-
pain has been shown to act more energetically.
It is only too clear that the preference should be
given to papain as a digestive ferment for the
treatment of dyspepsia. Besides for dyspepsia,
the papain is of great importance in the treatment
of diphtheria. Its effective power consists in the
circumstance that no free acid need be present in
the application, and, further, that the moisture of
the diphtheric membranes is quite sufficient to
allow the ferment to dissolve the solidified sub-
stance of the membranes. No other ferment has
so far been able to obtain these results. It is on
this account that many competent experimentalists
and specialists have lately made observation as to
these points. From all these circumstances there
is no doubt that in papain we have a ferment
which has a great future before it, and it is for
this reason that I have made a special study of the
manner of preparing the papain. I have had the
satisfaction of obtaining the most favorable results
from its use in the treatment of disease.”
The Extra Pharmacopaeia, page 305, says
that Papian is the dried juice of the Paw Paw
(Carica Papaya,) and goes on to show its medical
value. The Medical Annual of the current
edition also publishes a long article on the fruit
ferment Papain from the Paw Paw, and quotes
from an American work the Polyclinic, of June,
1896, showing the very beneficial results obtained
from the use of Papain in cases of indigestion,
proving that its digestive power, while in the sys-
tem, is greater than any other ferment known.
In a supplement to Chambers Encyclopaedia
Papain is described as follows:—“Papain is a
Nitrogenous Body, isolated from the juice of the
tropical Paw Paw. . , . The juice has for a
long time been used in the West Indies for
making meat tender; but it has only recently
been shown that Papain possesses, like Pepsin
and Trypsin, the power of digesting meat fibre,
and this digestion will go on in an alkaline, a neu-
tral, or an acid solution. Hence, it belongs to the
group of Digestive Ferments, and, like them, is
employed in casefof Dyspepsia, or employed for
the predigestion of food.”
The proprietor of the Cairns Preserving Works,
noting the great digestive ability of the Paw Paw
Fruit, which grows prolifically in North Queens-
land, has manufactured a delicious table sauce
from it. All the principal grocers sell it. This
digestive tropical novelty is receiving much atten-
tion.*

In Nelson (M.L.), the other day, a man
took out his money in the street— probably to
gloat over it. Two hours afterwards he missed a
fiver. After vainly searching his pockets he re-
turned to the spot where he had taken out Ins
lucre, and there, serenely lying on the footpath
(main street, too), was his fiver —neatly, folded as
he had last seen it. “ Sleepy Hollow,” indeed.
Noteworthy that out of 8476 Berlin
divorces—’Bs to ’94—more than half were of child-
less couples, while 1766 pairs had but one child,
and 1042 onlv two. In two cases, however, there
were 10 children ; in two, 11; and in one, 12 the
mother in this case being only 36 ! In two in-
stances the parties had been married over 25 years,
and in 500 over 20; though 316 couples had been
under one year married, and 497 under two years.
Rhea or ramie, whose cultivation is shortly
to be boomed in Australia, failed dismally in New
Australia because, though the plant flourished ex-
ceedingly there, no practicable means had then
been devised of separating the silky fibre from the
gummy sap. “ Besides,” a returned New Austra-
lian says, “don’t go into hysterics when the high
price obtainable for the fibre is mentioned —so
many pounds a ton. A room-full of raniie would
weigh more than the same bulk of thistle-down,
but not much.”
They have a queer way of doing business
on the W. A. railwavs. Recently, while travelling
from Albany to the goldfields, a Bulletin cor-
respondent saw lady passengers who had boarded
the train at two different stations get out at their
respective destinations and pay before leaving the
platform. “ During the journey alluded to,” con-
tinues the writer, “I heard a tall yarn told by an
ancient sandgroper. He said that wlrle livin'! at
Geraldton he had found a dead horse with a stout
chain firmly embedded in its neck. Suspended
from the chain was a very heavy bell, and the
weight of the bell and chain combined had caused
the chain to chafe its way through the flesh, which
had grown over it. The chain had continued to
grow downwards until it became embedded in the
spinal column, when death had evidently resulted.
He has, he said, placed it upon record by having
it photographed.” Wanted, that photo.
“The Snag,” on a subject of bush interest:
Dear Bulletin, — Be tools of the Australian bushman,
H.F. must have struck a very queer box of axes. The
bushman of my acquaintance only cuts half the nob off
the handle —not because it is too long, but simply to
enable him to drive the handle in without splitting it.
Ring-barkers invariably cut from 3 to 9 inches off the
handle to give speed in chopping; and, as to temper, it
would be hard to beat “Collins” or “ the Chopper’s
Pride.” Seventy-five per cent, of these axes will stand
in any of our timbers, if not ground too thin. Also, in all
my years of bush-work X never knew a bushman who
broke out every other tooth in a cross-cut saw, although
for soft timber, like cedar, fine beach, or brushwoods, we
prefer an open-space cross-cut without drag-teeth. For
hardwoods of all kinds, Diston’s peg, or full-lightning, is
second to none, and, I think, cannot be improved upon.
His pit-saws are most used, especially in cedar, being of
better temper and balance than the other makers. As to
the bushman’s wedges, they are locally made to order.
The most inferior-article we have in the bush is the im-
; or ted bushman! —The Snag.

Are YoG Listless, Weak, ai)d PeYoid of Energy ?
DR. WILLIAMS’
PINK - PILLS • FOR - PALE - PEOPLE
Will Restore your Lost Strength and Vigor,
And Fit You to Face the Most Trying Weather.
Indigestion.
WHAT A SYDNEY LADY THINKS OF IT.
Of all the ills that flesh is heir to perhaps the
commonest and, at the same time, the most try-
ing, is indigestion. It is impossible to over-esti-
mate the terrible results that may follow in its
wake. Mrs. Maud Hughes, of 414 Victoria-
street, Darlinghurst, Sydney, had it in a severe
way, and very kindly placed the following details
in the possession of a reporter :
“ It is now about two years since the indigestion
first attacked me. It commenced with gnawing
pains, and every time I breathed it hurt me. My
appetite left me, and the very sight of food made
'Y
M
\j>y
V
me feel sick, and very often at night I could not
sleep; when I did, my rest was disturbed by
frightful dreams. Although I had the very best
medical advice in New South Wales and other
colonies my condition did not improve, but on the
contrary got worse. I suffered from terrible head-
aches and a sort of mist seemed to hang over my
eyes. Knowing how ill I was, for I had by this
time lost a great deal of flesh, a lady who called
to see me one day said, ‘ I have made a great dis-
cover, which lam going to tell you all about. It
is the great virtue of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills,
and they have done me all the good in the world.’
My friend spoke in such glowing terms of the
great benefit they had been to her, and having
also seen a number of advertisements of this
medicine, proclaiming it a positive cure for
dyspepsia and indigestion, I determined to
give it a trial, and bought three boxes to
start with, and commenced taking the contents,
following the directions that were wrapped round
each box. I felt better from the first. Before
commencing them I had been brought down so
low with suffering that my medical adviser had
ordered me a change of air, and strongly recom-
mended a sea .voyage. This advice I followed,
and in a few days left for Europe, taking a supply
of the pills with me. By continuing with them
my health and appetite improved wonderfully,
and I am sure the Messageries Maritimes Co.
didn’t make much out of me, for I never missed a
meal whilst at sea, ‘falling in ’ to breakfast, tiffin,
and dinner most religiously. Instead of a fasting
lady I think I may justly lay claim to the title of
a feasting lady. I decided to get a fresh supply
of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills at Paris; I experi-
enced no difficulty in obtaining them there. After
spending a short time in England I returned
home to Australia, and I am sure I feel a good ten
years younger. My spirits have revived, my
complexion has improved, and I take a fresh
interest in life. I attribute all these good results
to the use of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People, as before taking them I felt invariably
listless and drowsy. I am quite convinced it is
this medicine that has cured me ; in fact, I have
taken no other. Yes, you are quite at liberty to
publish this statement if you choose, and if I can
give you any more information will gladly do so.”

An Enervated System Revived.
(From the Carlton Gazette, Melbourne.)
Mr. A. Sayers, of 57 Adam-street, South Yarra,
is perhaps one of the best-known mining men of
Victoria. It would be a difficult thing, in fact,
UJ
to find any
mining centre
where this gen-
tleman has not,
with more or
less success, as-
sisted in the
development of
Victoria’s gol-
den treasures.
At Ballarat,
Sandhurst,
Malden, &c.,
his name and
person are fa-
miliar. While
awaiting fresh opportunities, and at the same
time recuperating— there is nothing so wearing on
the system as a life on ’Change—he has been so
successful in the latter respect that one of our
representatives, visiting him on business, could
not help remarking it.
“I feel better now than I have for many years
past. Tlie last two months my whole system has
been re tuned—wound up to concert-pitch, as it
were,” was the reply.
“You suffered severely? ”
“Yes, but not so much physically as from an
entire absence of tone in the system. I could
transact my business fairly well; but the con-
tinual excitement of over three decades of mining
life—prospecting, promoting, speculating, and
Exchange life —had hold on me of late years. The
system had run down ; that was about it.”
“ Why not have visited a medical man and
secured a tonic ? ”
“ I did ; several doctors agreeing that it was a
tonic I required. But they failed to supply that
much-needed mental and also physical bracer.
They supplied plenty of them, both in quantity
and variety, but with no appreciable benefit—the
least business effort bringing about a recurrence
of the all-overish feeling.”
“ What do you attribute the change to ? ”
“ Well, a barrister friend of mine, a great suf-
ferer from paralysis, mentioned to me the immense
benefit he had experienced from a course of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People, and, some
two months since, I invested in a couple of boxes.”
“ How did they pan out ? ”
“ Much better than some of my mining specs.—
like pure gold. A few boxes strung me up pro-
perly ; my appetite improved, and work (before a
heavy task) became a pleasure.
“ No. A few boxes of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
did for me what pounds’ sterling worth of doctors’
Btuff failed to accomplish.”
“ You are perfectly satisfied of the merits of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for purifying the blood and as
a nerve tonic ?
“ Certainly. For re-toning a system run-down
by overwork or mental anxiety, they cannot be
beaten, and I lose no opportunity of so informing
my friends."

(From the Tarrangower Times, Victoria.)
“My daughter had been strong and healthy
until about two years ago,” said Mrs. Bowe, of
North Maldron, Victoria, to our reporter, when
she grew very pale and thin. She became weak
and low-spirited, lost her appetite, her sleep was
troubled and restless, and the least exertion fatigued
her—in fact, she had to take her sister’s arm for
sa

walking. She was
of a wonderfully
active and cheer-

fore this change
took place, and the

sought. But no
good resulted.
Several medicines
were tried, but she
became weaker
and more listless
every day. The
doctor said she was suffering from anaemia, or
poorness of blood. We then became seriously
alarmed, and consulted different medical men, who
prescribed, and told Miss May to take as much
exercise as possible. She, being fond of flowers,
naturally turned her attention to the garden, but
had to abandon the work, as the least exertion ex-
hausted her. Often, when I thought she was out
taking exercise in the fresh air, I have found her
in the drawing-room in a somnolent state. Then,
when disturbed, she would steal quietly to her
room, and remain there for hours at a time. At
this stage we became extremely anxious concerning
her health, and again sought medical advice, as
we thought she was going into a decline. It was
of no use, though ; nothing did her any good.
She was then so thin and weak that the slightest
exertion tired her. She also suffered from severe
oramps in the legs.
“ Friends then recommended patent medicines,
and two or three kinds were tried, unsuccessfully.
“At last I decided to take her away to the
country for a change, but just as I was about to
do so I saw in the Tarrangower Times an adver-
tisement about Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People, and noticing that people with symptoms
similar to those of my daughter had been cured, I
determined to give them a trial.
“ After usiirg half a box a great improvement
was noticeable, and after using three boxes she
regained her appetite, was able to sleep with
comfort, and was so strong and well that we con-
sidered that there was no necessity for her con-
tinuing to take Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. So she
discontinued using them. I honestly believe my
daughter’s recovery was due to the great remedy.
She is now as well as ever she was in her life.
She has a fine color, i 3 able to eat and sleep well,
and can take any amount of outdoor exercise
without feeling fatigued. I feel very grateful to
Dr. Williams for my daughter’s recovery, and
would like to have the case made known, as it
may induce others who suffer as my daughter did
to try this remedy to their benefit, and thus
perhaps bring sunshine and happiness to their
homes.”
Great satisfaction has been expressed by Miss
Bowe’s large circle of friends that she is again
enjoying good health, and able to take her accus-
tomed place at local social gatherings.

Rheumatism and Neuralgia,
(From the National Advocate.)
For many years Mr. J. H. Potter, of Wiagdon,
a small rural settlement on the outskirts of
Bathurst, has been subject to severe attacks of
rheumatism and neuralgia. Believing the history
of his maladies and recovery would interest our
readers, we despatched to his residence a reporter,
who successfully solicited the following details
“Well,” began Mr. Potter, “I must tell you
that in my younger days I did a good deal of
camping out; I continually got wet through, and
sometimes put on damp clothes. As a conse-
quence, I contracted rheumatism, and later on
neuralgia in the head. 1 became a perfect martyr
to these trying ills, which kept me company for
many years ; they affected my appetite, rest, and
temper to such a degree that I hardly ate or slept
at all, and was irritable with everthing and every-
body. The pains about my eyes were very agon-
ising. I was in Bathurst the time the last show
was held, and had a fearful time of it. I tried all
sorts of remedies but got no relief.”
“ Did you consult a doctor ? ”
“ Yes, I consulted one in Melbourne, one of the
most prominent there, but he could do nothing for
me; he said I had all sorts of complaints, and
wanted to perform a similar operation on me to
that he had lately achieved in another case, viz.,
that of piercing the bone of the affected part, but
this I declined, and left him, deciding rather to
endure my pains than to submit to it.”
‘ ‘ What did you do then ? ”
“Just about this time I read in the Sunday
Times some accounts of cures brought about by
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People, and
then and there made up my mind to see what they
would do for me. I bought a box ; when I had
finished that I seemed a different man. The third
box worked wonders, and I felt grand. I reckon
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People are a
splendid remedy. I can now eat heartily and
sleep well.”
“You hold a high opinion of their value, then ?”
Of course I do. They do what is claimed for
them, and I can strongly recommend them to all
sufferers. My wife knows what they did for me,
and consequently thinks as much of them as I do.
If I went to a friend’s house for tea, Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People went with me, and
directly the meal was over I took the prescribed
number. My friends used to laugh at me, but I
didn t mind. I told them the laugh would be on
my side soon, and so it is ; they know it too.”
Mr. Potter does not present the appearance of a
man who has endured so much suffering. He is
the picture of health, and has the best of spirits ;
his business (bee-farming), which was necessarily
sadly neglected during his illness, is also thriving.
And these desirable changes he attributes to the
marvellous efficacy of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Palo People.

About six years ago, when residing in Hobart,
Tasmania,” said Mrs. Nellie Denver, of Countess
Cottage, Double Bay, Sydney, to a reporter, “I
had the misfortune to meet with a serious accident,
which _ happened in the simplest manner possible,
as I will endeavor to relate. On leaving my house
by the back gate, the bolt slipped whilst I had
hold of it, which caused me to fall very heavilv,
which I subsequently discovered had seriously in-
jured my spine, although, strange to say, I felt no
ill-effect for about two years later. I then com-
menced to suffer from great weakness and dizzi-
ness, and an intolerable feeling of exhaustion ; in
fact, I felt so nervous and uneasy that I feared to
cross the street, alarmed that I might meet with
some accident from a passing vehicle. Not at first
recognising the really serious condition to which I
was reduced, I did not seek medical attendance,
but when I at last became aware of my serious
condition, I was persuaded by my friends to go to
the Sydney Hospital, where I remained two months
without receiving any permanent benefit. On
leaving the hospital I again rather neglected my
state of health, and on applying for re-admission
was informed that my oase was perfectly
hopeless. Being, unfortunately, not over-blessed
with this world’s goods, I obtained admission to
the Newington Asylum, where I remained seven
months ; yet still I got no better, being unable to
swallow any nourishing food. I then took two
courses of electric baths, for which I paid six
guineas, and after them, with the exception of
some slight alleviation of my suffering, I cannot
say that they did me much good ; in fact. I could
not even walk, so I returned home, feeling very
despondent, and imagining that there was no cure
in store for me. One day, on reading the daily
paper, I noticed an account of a marvellous re-
covery effected by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People, and considering that this case (the
one of Boilermaker Jarvis, suffering from loco-
motor ataxia) closely resembled mine, I deter*
<3
mined to make a trial of them, and sent a boy
from the house to buy some for me. I experienced
relief after the first box, and have steadily im-
proved ever since; my appetite returned, and I
ceased to suffer from that terrible feeling of ner-
vousness. lam now in my seventh box, and can
confidently assert that I am most decidedly im-
proved in health. My friends also notice a
marked improvement in my condition, and this I
entirely attribute to the health-giving properties
of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills : in fact, it must be
solely due to them, for I have tried no other
remedy. The proprietors of Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills are at perfect liberty to make whatever use
they may think fit of this statement, for I feel
deeply grateful for the benefit I have derived from
them.”
Such is the remarkable history, and confirms
what is said in all the chief newspapers of this
country of the astonishing cures wrought by Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People. They cure
locomotor ataxia, partial paralysis and spinal

an impoverished state of the blood, such as
anaemia, pale and sallow complexion, general mus-
cular weakness, indigestion, loss of appetite, in-
fluenza’s evil after effects, shortness of breath,
pains in the back, nervous headaches, etc.

Pale and Sallow Girls.
What can be more distressing; than to see a girl
drooping and fading in the springtime of youth?
Instead of bright eyes, glowing, rosy cheeks, and
an elastic step, there are dull eyes, pale, sallow, or
greenish complexion, and a languidness of step
that bespeak disease and an early death if proper
treatment is not promptly resorted to and per-
sisted in until the impoverished blood is enriched
and the functions of life become regular.
Upon parents and guardians rest a great re-
sponsibility at the time their daughters are bud-
ding into womanhood. If your daughter is pale,
complains of weaknesses “tired out” upon the
slightest exertion ; if she is troubled with head-
ache or backache, pain in the side ; if her temper
is fitful and her appetite poor, she is in a condition
of extreme peril, a fit subject for the development
of the most dreaded of all diseases—Consumption.
If you notice any of the above symptoms, lose no
time in procuring Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. They
will assist the patient to develop properly and
regularly; they will enrich the blood and restore
health’s roses to the cheeks; bright eyes and
lightness of step will surely follow their use, and
all danger of consumption and premature death
will be averted. Wise and prudent mothers will
insist upon their daughters taking Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills upon the approach of the period of
womanhood, and thus avoid all chances of disease
or early decay.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People have

pains in the back, nervous headache, early decay,
all forms of female weakness, and hysteria. These
pills are a tonic, not a purgative. They are
genuine only with the full name, Dr. Williams
Pink Pills for Pale People, and are sold only in
boxes, encircling which is a blue warning label,

may be obtainedi from chemists and storeki epers

We wake and sigh, we sleep and, 10,
The gentlest eyes look lone and weep I
Ah, why do lost loves tease us so
When we lie down to sleep?
Awake, my wife, how shall I dare
The silence ! Closer —heart to heart I
Your breath is wandering in my hair,
But yet how far apart!
Long have we lived like those that loved
With wedded hand and mingled mouth,
The truth suppressed —but strangely moved,
My heart is dust and drouth.
I d eamt she came—a maiden pale,
A maiden pale, dead long ago ;
And she spake words of no avail,
An I she gave looks of woe.
Dowr streamed the brown hair round her head ;
She whispered, and with sudden start
I rose and kissed her roses red,
And pressed her to my heart.
The roses paled ; like shadowed light
She vanished, and I could not speak j
Upon the blind the moon was white,
My lips were on your cheek !
The woman said, “ We sleep and, 10,
The gentlest eyes look love and weep !
Ah, why do lost loves tease us so,
When we lie down to sleep ?
“ I dreamt he came, the king of men,
Who drifted deathward long years gone,
I le love-light in his eyes as when
With fire of Life they shone :
‘ A rose-bud like a lonely pearl
From his pale fingers trembled low,
And to his lips he pressed a curl—
A curl of long ago.
“ And * This,’ he said, ‘ was love of mine ’ —
The rosebud waved in mist away ;
And ‘ This,’ he said, 1 was gift of thine *•—
The curl of gold grew grey.
“ Then down he bent in true love-wise—
I could but sigh, I could not spo>ak ;
We kissed—the moon shone in these eyes,
Your lips were on my cheek.”
They slept no more that dreamful night;
A cloud made dark the moonlit room ;
Lost Youth was nigh them with its white
Ghost lovers from the tomb.
RODERIC QUINN.
Business, Bobbery, etc.
The affairs of a large southern mercantile firm
have become so desperate that, in default of the dis-
counting facilities formerly accorded to their bills on
their dummy branches, they now “ put in ” dummy bills
signed by their employes, whose names are sufficiently
obscure to pass the discount clerks of the many banks
interested in the firm’s welfare.
* * * *
On change. “ Anything to avoid, here ? ” said
an intending broker to an old Exchange hand. “Yes;
any man with a pale face and a weak voice. Every time
I’ve fallen in here, it has been to a man with a pipeclay
face and squeaky talk. Lombroso says pallor is the color
of genius. I say it is also the color of hypocrisy.”
A witness before the Westralia Royal Mining
Commission was asked '‘Have you any knowledge of
geology as applied to mining?” “Well, just to the ex-
tent of being able to use a few big phrases,” was the re-
ply. Novel truthfulness in a land of experts.
• • • *
A very “fly” man stepped into a very well-
known Melb. shop, and asked to look at a good and very
cheap opera-glass in the window. It was handed out,
and, knowing all about the change-ringing business, he
kept tight hold of it. The Oriental in charge tried all
other dodges, but the man was “ fly,” paid his money, and
was about to go. “ Buy a case for it,” said one of the
Eastern gentlemen. “ No.” “ Veil, veil, ve’ll gif you
one, den." The case was produced, and the glass put in.
When the “ fly” man got home one lens was missing, and
is still. The quickness of the hand had deceived the eye,
and thus the “trader,” done out of the good glass, had
his revenge.
* * * •
T.D.: “On two or three recent occasions, I
have received printed lists (issued by rival firms) of sur-
names purporting to be those of persons to whom money
is owing by wind or windfall. ‘ Christian ’ names are not
given, and as a guinea has to be forwarded before the
slightest information is vouchsafed, the firms must make
fine hauls out of numerous Smiths, Browns and Joneses
whose imaginations have been stimulated to visions of
untold wealth. The scheme is probably worked on
registry-office lines, with a hot scale of charges for more
definite information, solicitor’s fees, and so forth—until
the time comes for telling the anxious client that he
hasn’t a leg to stand on.”
* * * *
In view of the removal of the Tasmanian Par-
liamentary difficulty re the Great Western Railway,
elaborate corralling arrangements have been completed
for the capture of Bull investors. One of the promoters
left for Europe last week, and a number of satellites will
follow at once.
• * * *
Exposure of the alleged drawback swindle on
Melb. has, strange to say, already curtailed interpro-
vincial exports from Victoria enormously. The Customs
have an eye on other large exporters of goods subject to
drawback, and names are freely mentioned in Southern
commercial circles. Considering the magnificent possi-
bilities offered to dishonest traders by the laxity of draw-
back regulations, it is almost incredible that so few prose-
cutions have been instituted.
• « » *
At least two prominent Australian pencillers
are run by their respective banks, an ex-chureh deacon
has a “joint” in the bag of another outside bookie, and
the senior partner in a big Southern clothing firm runs
a principal Melb. S.P, layer.
• c * *
H.M.: “What price the public patience in
re the languid bank ledger-keeper ? For years have
studied anxious customers standing in dumb humiliation
beside an impenetrable barricade six feet high, until the
hawty figure-official, who ignores all manner of appeal, is
pleased to peer over and inquiah their wishes. This state
of ‘ splendid ’ isolation inflicted upon bank-clients while
awaiting their cheques or pass-book, is calculated to
damage that instinct of lawn«?der alleged to be the pro-
perty of every right-thinking citizen.”
On account of the high-rate the insurance co.’s
charge in some of the mining centres in W.A., one store-
keeper has erected poppet-legs over his store, and has a
large tank of water on top, with hose attached long
enough to reach all over his premises in case of fire, and
large enough fp drown the establishment in one act.
Then he does his own insurance.
» * » •
Mismanagement, writes a Bulletin correspon-
dent, has a great deal to do with the disasters which have
overtaken many W.A. mines I know a mine not 30 miles
from Ooolgardie where it costs £l7 per week for wages to
supervise one shaft in which there are but six men. The
contract price is £7 15s. per foot, and one drill will put a
hole in. There is a man paid £5 per week to superintend
sinking operations, a clerk at £5 per week, and the
manager (not a miner) £7 per week. I could give you a
long list of such things—not perhaps quite so glaring.
* * * *
Westralian mining managers are asking the
Govt. “ to either fight the patent rights of the cyanide
syndicate or buy out their claim. If royalties are paid it
will mean that only good-grade ore will be treated, leaving
entirely untouched lodes worth 7dwt. to i-oz.” So well-
trained in boom-time lying are these hummers that they
can’t shake it off even when the truth is the right thing
commercially. How can a 5 per cent, royalty, such as is
proposed for the use of cyanide, and that only on the gold
won by its distinct application, make all this difference?
It is reasoning of this kind which has made the public so
tired of mining news from W.A. as published by the
principal Eastern dailies.
* * * *
A very big mining lawsuit is reported to be
under weigh at Charters Towers. The question at issue
is whether or not the agent for a wealthy mine and mill-
owner, resident down South, in disposing of some hun-
dreds of thousands of tons of tailings, the accumulation
of years, to various cvanide parties, exceeded his powers.
* * • *
Another monopoly gone ! The Morning Star
gold-mining co. at Sale (Vic.) is managed, Tub Bulletin
is told, by a woman—and young and unmarried at that!
The mammoth grows apace. 14,400 new
houses built in London in ’96. The average is
about 1190 per month. The cities grow larger
and larger, and the proportion of Englishmen in
England grows smaller and ever smaller.
The New Woman has been whooping over
the refusal of some of her to leave a Melbourne
court during the taking of some extremely under-
done evidence. Which recalls Baron Penne-
father’s remark when it was pointed out to him
that a couple of ‘ladies’ had remained in court
after he had made, in similar circumstances, a
similar request—“Oh, it doesn’t matter —all the
modest women are gone ! ”
“The metaphysical Christian,” writes a
Bulletin correspondent, “is the funniest of all
modern faddists. An acquaintance prominent
among the faith-healers recently squashed a finger
in a door-jamb. Five minutes afterwards I found
him prancing up and down his verandah, holding
the bruised and bleeding digit aloft and proclaim-
ing loudly, * There is no pain ! I say there is no
pain ! ’ But simulated exultation could not over-
come the agony that contorted his face. I notice,
also, that no Christian Metaphysist fails to ‘ lie
up ’ when assailed by anything worse than in-
digestion or a sore throat. Surely, faith should
pull the brethren through without the ulterior
assistance of a holiday.”
The " Round-Turn.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He had gone back to the bedroom, after break-
fast, for some forgotten trifle. The clothes were
turned back over the foot of the bed, and some-
thing red attracted his attention. Mechanically
pulling it from between the sheets, he found it
was a man’s red silk sock ! He never wore other
than cotton.
And not quite two years married !
Furiously he paced the room for a space ; then,
with a violent effort, calmed himself sufficiently
to go downstairs and out of the house without a
sign.
* » * *
“I’d bring him up with a round turn, if I were
you,” a friend of his wife’s had advised her in a
previous conversation.
“ I’ll have to do something ! Not married two
years—and the way he goes on with that hussy !”
But, how to do it !
Tears, rages and protests had only driven him
further over to the enemy.
* * * *
The cursed thing was in his pocket. When he
felt for his pouch, to fill his pipe, he put his hand
on it, and it seemed to scorch his fingers. Off he
went at once to his solicitor’s. His own delin-
quency was nothing; his wife’s was white-hot
damnation !
“ Keep cool, old man; no good making a splash
over these things.”
“ Splash ! You’re not in my socks / ”
“Let’s have a look at the thing,” the lawyer
said.
For some seconds he turned it over in his hand
—then he grinned. “This is a plant, old man I
The sock has never been worn !
The “ round turn ” was a success !

Western American epitaph—“ Jack King.

Queensland, arrowroot is declared by
British experts to be not true arrowroot at all,
and should, therefore, not be labelled arrowroot.
The true article is prepared from the genus
Maranta, but the Queensland article is a Ganna
product. The British Board of Inland Revenue
therefore advises, under pain of prosecution and
penalty, a label—“ Queensland Arrowroot, pre-
pared from Ganna cdulis.” Last year Q. exported
381,3781 b.
The Chosen Race prides itself on its free-
dom from such diseases as tubercle, but it has its
own very weak spots. • Thus the Journal of
Mental Science ■—
Dr. Buschau brings out in clearness and detail the
great frequency of insanity among the Jews From all
information, it appears that with the Jewish population
mental derangement is from four to six times as common
as in the Gentile Even in Palestine this proclivity holds
good. According to Tobler, almost all Jewish women are
hysterical. Diabetes is very common. As Ziemmsen has
observed, there is a neurotic strain through the whole
race of Israel. The extraordinary proclivity is not due to
heightened mental exertion, since many Jews, as in Hun-
gary and Poland, lead a very quiet life, and yet there the
tendency to neurosis is still marked.

On Our Selection: Fourteen
Years Ago.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was a real scorcher. A soft, sweltering
summer’s day. The air quivered ; the heat drove
the fowls under the dray and sent the old dog to
sleep upon the floor inside the house. The iron
on the ski 11 ion creaked and sweated—so did Dad
and Dave down the paddock, grubbing—grubbing
in 130 deg. of sunshine. They were clearing a
piece of new land—a heavily-timbered box-tree
flat. They had been at it a fortnight, and if any
music was in the ring of the axe or the rattle of
the pick when commencing, there was none now.
Dad wished to be cheerful and comp'acent. He
said (putting the pick down and dragging his
flannel off to wring it) : “It’s a good thing to
sweat well.” Dave didn’t say anything. I don’t
know what he thought, but he looked up at Dad-
just looked up at him—while the perspiration
fi led his eyes and ran down over his nose like
r tin off a shingle ; then he hitched up his pants
and “ wired in ” again. Dave was a philosopher.
He worked away until the axe flew off the handle
with a ring and a bound and might have been lost
in the long grass for ever only Dad stopped it
with his shin ; but I fancy he didn’t mean to stop
it when I think how he jumped—it was the only
piece of excitement there had been the whole of
that relentlessly solemn fortnight. Dad got vexed
—he was in a hurry with the grubbing—and said
lie never could get anything done without some-
thing going wrong. Dave wasn’t sorry the axe
came off—he knew it meant half-an-hour in the
shade fixing it on again. “ Anyway,” Dad went
on, “ we’ll go to dinner now.”
On the way to the house he several times looked
at the sky—that cloudless, burning sky—and said,
to no one in particular, “I wish to God it would
rain ! ” It sounded like an aggravated prayer.
Dave didn’t speak, and I don’t think Dad expected
he would.
* » » *
Joe was the last to sit down to dinner, and
he came in steaming hot. He had chased out of
sight a cow that had poked into the cultivation.
Joe mostly went about with green bushes in his
hat, to keep his head cool, and a few gum-leaves
were now sticking in his moist and matted hair.
“ I put her out, Dad ! ” he said, casting an eager
glare at everything on the table. “She tried
to jump and got stuck on the fence, and broke it
all down. On’y I couldn’t get anything, I’d er
broke ’er head—there wasn’t a thing, on’y
dead cornstalks and cowdung about.” Then
he lunged his fork desperately at a blowfly
that persistently hovered about his plate, and
commenced, Joe had a healthy appetite. He
had charged his mouth with a load of cold meat,
when his jaws ceased work, and, opening his
mouth as though he were sleepy, he leaned for-
ward and calmly returned it all to the plate. Dad
got suspicious, and asked Joe what was up; but
Joe only wiped his mouth, looked sideways at his
plate, and pushed it away. All of us stopped
eating then, and stared at each other. Mother
said, “ Well, I—l wrapped a cloth round it so
nothing could get in, and put it in the safe—l
don’t know where on earth to put the meat, I’m
sure; if I put it in a bag and hang it up that thief
of a dog gets it.”
“ Yes,” Dad observed, “ I believe he’d stick his
nose into hell itself, Ellen, if he thought there was
a bone there—and there ought to be lots by this
time.” Then lie turned over the remains of that
cold meat, and, considering we had all witnessed
the last kick of the slaughtered beast, it was sur-
prising what animation this part of him yet
retained. In vain did Dad explore for a really
dead piece—there was life in all of it. Joe wasn’t
satisfied. He said he knew where there were a
lot of eggs, and disappeared down the yard. Eggs
were not plentiful on our selection, because we
too often had to eat the hens when there was no
meat—three or four were as many as we ever saw
at one time. So on this day, when Joe ap-
peared with a hatful, there was excitement. He
felt himself a hero. We thought him a little
saviour.
“My!’’said mother, “where did you get all
those '! ”
“Get ’em ! I’ve had these planted for three
munce—they’re a nest I found long ago; I thought
I wouldn’t say anythink till we really wanted
’em.” Just then one of the eggs fell out of the
hat and went off “ pop ” on the floor. Dave
nearly upset the table, he rose so suddenly ; and
covering his nose with one hand he made for the
door; then he scowled back over his shoulder at
Joe. He utterly scorned his brother Joe. All of
us deserted the table except Dad —he stuck to his
place manfully ; it took a lot to shift him. Joe
must have had a fine nerve. “That’s on’y one
bad ’n’,” he said, taking the rest to the fireplace
where the kettle stood. Then Dad, who had re-
mained calm and majestic, broke out. “Damn
y’ boy ! ” he yelled, “ take tli’ awful things out-
s;de—you tinker ! ” Joe took them out and tried
them all, but I forget if he found a good one.
* * * *
Dad peered into the almost-empty water-
cask and again muttered a short prayer for rain.
He decided to do no more grubbing that day, but
to run wire around the new land instead. The
posts had been in the ground some time, and were
bored. Dave and Sarah bored them. Sarah was
as good as any man—so Dad reckoned. She could
turn her hand to anything, from sewing a shirt
to sinking a post-hole. She could give Dave
inches in arm-measurements, and talk about a leg !
She had a leg—a beauty ! It was as thick at the
ankle as Dad s was at the thigh, nearly,
, Anyone who would know what real amusement
is should try wiring posts. What was to have
been the top wire (the No. 8 stuff) Dad com-
menced to put in the bottom holes, and we ran
it through some 12 or 15 posts before he saw the
mistake—then we dragged it out slowly and
savagely ; Dad swearing adequately all the time.
At last everything went splendidly. We
dragged the wire through panel after panel, and at
intervals Dad would examine the blistering sky for
signs of rain. Once when he looked up a red
bullock was reaching for his waistcoat which
hung on a branch of a low tree. Dad sang out.
The bullock poked out his tongue and reached
higher. Then Dad told Joe to run. Joe ran—so
did the bullock, but faster, and with the waist-
coat that once was a part of mother’s shawl half-
way down his throat. Had the shreds and ribbons
that dangled to it been a little longer, he might
have trodden on them and pulled it back, but he
didn’t. Joe deemed it his duty to follow that red
bullock till it dropped the waistcoat, so he ham-
mered along full split behind. Dad and Dave
stood watching until pursued and pursuer vanished
down the gully ; then Dad said something about
<Joe_ being a fool, and they pulled at the wire
again They were nearing a corner post, and
Dad was hauling it through the last panel when
there came the devil’s own noise of galloping
hoofs. Fifty or more cattle came careering along
straight for the fence, bellowing and kicking np
their heels in the air, as cattle do sometimes after
a shower of rain. Joe was behind them—con-
siderably still at full speed and yelping like a
dog. Joe loved excitement.
For weeks those cattle had been accustomed to
go m and out between the posts ; and they didn’t
seem to have any thoughts of wire as they bounded
along. Dave stood with gaping mouth. Dad
groaned, and the wire’s-end he was holding in his
hand flew up with a whiz and took a scrap of his
ear away. The cattle got mixed up in the wires.
Some toppled over; some were caught by the legs;
some by the horns. They dragged the wire 20 and
30 yards away, twisted it round logs, and left a lot
of the posts pointing to sunset. Oh, Dad’s
language then ! He swung his arms about and
foamed at the mouth, Dave edged away from
him. Joe came up waving triumphantly a chewed
piece of the waistcoat. “ D-d-did it g-give them
a buster, Dad ! he said, the sweat running over
his face as though a spring had broken out on top
of his head. Dad jumped a log arid tried to un-
buckle his strap and reach for Joe at the same
time, but Joe fled.
That threw a painful pall over everything. Dad
declared he was sick and tired of the'whole thing
and wouldn’t do another hand’s-turn. Dave
meditated and walked along the fence, plucking
off scraps of skin and hair that here and there
clung to the bent and battered wire.
* * * *
We just had finished supper when old Bob
Wren, a bachelor who farmed about two miles
from us, arrived. He used to come over every
mail-night and bring his newspaper with him.
Bob couldn’t read a word, so he always got Dad to
spell over the paper to him. We didn’t take a
newspaper.
Bob said there were clouds gathering behind
Flat Top when he came in, and Dad went out and
looked, and for the fiftieth time that day prayed
in his own way for rain. Then he took the paper
and we gathered at the table to listen. “Hello,”
he commenced, “ this is M'Doolan’s paper you’ve
got, Bob.”
Bob rather thought it wasn’t.
“Yes, yes, man, it is” Dad put in; “see, it’s
addressed to him.”
Bob leaned over and looked at the address, and
said : “ No, no, that’s mine ; it always comes like
that.” Dad laughed. We all laughed. He
opened it, anyway. He hadn’t read for five
minutes when the light flickered nearly out.
Sarah reckoned the oil was about done, and poured
water in the lamp to raise the kerosene to the
wick, but that didn’t last long, and, as there was
no fat in the house, Dad squatted on the floor and
read by the fire-light.
He plodded through the paper tediously from
end to end, reading the murders and robberies a
second time. Fhe clouds that old Bob said were
gathering when he came in were now developing
to a storm, for the wind began to rise, and the
giant iron-bark tree that grew close behind the
house swayed and creaked weirdly, and threw out
those strange sobs and moans that on wild nights
impart terror to the hearts of bush children. A
glimmer of lightning appeared through the cracks
in the slabs. Old Bob said he would go before it
came on, and started into the inky darkness.
It scorning!” Dad said, as he shut the door
and put the peg in after seeing old Bob out. And
it came—in no time. A fierce wind struck the
house. Then a vivid flash of lightning lit up
every crack and hole, and a clap of thunder fol-
lowed that nearly shook the place down.
Dad ran to the back door and put his shoulder
against it; Dave stood to the front one, and Sarah
sat on the sofa with her arms around mother, telling
her not to be afraid. The wind blew furiously—its
one aim seemed the shifting of the house. Gust
after gust struck the walls and loft them quiver-
ing. Ihe children screamed. Dad called and
shouted, but no one could catch a word he said.
1 hen there was one tremendous crack—we under-
stood it the iron-bark tree had gone over. At
last, the shingled roof commenced to give. Several
times the ends rose (and our hair too) and fell
back into place again with a. clap. Then it went
clean away in one piece, with a rip like splitting a
ribbon, and there we stood, affrighted and shelter-
less, inside the walls. Then the wind went down
and it rained—rained on us all night.
* * * *
Next morning .Toe had been to the new
fence for the axe for Dad, and was off again as
fast as he could run, when he remembered some-
thing and called out, “Dad, old B-B-Bob’s just
over there, lyin’ down in the gully.”
Dad started up. “It’s ’ini all right —T
w-w-wouldn ter noticed, on’y Prince s-s-smelt
him.
“ Quick and show me where ! ” Dad said
Joe showed him.
“My God ! ” and Dad stood and stared. Old
Bob it was—dead. Dead as Moses
“Poor old Bob!” Dad said. “Poor-old-
tellow ! Joe asked what could have killed him »
Poor—old— Bob ! ”
Dave brought the dray, and we took him to the
house—or what remained of it.
Dad couldn't make out the cause of death—per-
haps it was lightning. He held a post-mortem
and after thinking hard for a long while, told
mother he was certain, anyway, that old Boh
would never get up again.
It was a change to have a dead man about the
place, and we were very pleased to be first, to tell
anyone who didn’t know the news about old Bob
We planted him on his own selection beneath a
gum-tree, where for years and years a family of
jackasses nightly roosted, Dad remarking: “As
there might be a chance of his bearin’, it’ll be
company for the poor old cove.”
Bananaland.
